taken away the sweetness of his voice and made him silent and definitively solitary, but in return, it had restored the intense expression he had in his eyes at birth.</t539> <t540>He was so concentrated on his silversmithing experiments that he barely left the laboratory to eat.</t540> <t541>Concerned about his self-absorption, José Arcadio Buendía gave him keys to the house and a little money, thinking that perhaps he needed a woman.</t541> <t542>But Aureliano spent the money on muriatic acid to prepare aqua regia and embellished the keys with a gold plating.</t542> <t543>His exaggerations were only comparable to those of Arcadio and Amaranta, who had already started to lose their teeth and still clung all day to the Indian blankets, stubborn in their decision not to speak Spanish but the Guajiro language.</t543> <t544>"You have nothing to complain about," Úrsula told her husband.</t544> <t545>"Children inherit the madness of their parents."</t545> <t546>And while she lamented her bad luck, convinced that her children's extravagances were something as dreadful as a pig's tail, Aureliano fixed on her a gaze that enveloped her in an aura of uncertainty.</t546>
<t547>—Someone is going to come —he told her.</t547>
<t548>Úrsula, as always when he expressed a prediction, tried to discourage him with her homespun logic.</t548> <t549>It was normal for someone to arrive.</t549> <t550>Dozens of strangers passed through Macondo daily without arousing concerns or foretelling secret announcements.</t550> <t551>However, beyond all logic, Aureliano was certain of his premonition.</t551>
<t552>—I don't know who it will be —he insisted—, but whoever it is, they are already on their way.</t552>
<t553>On Sunday, indeed, Rebeca arrived.</t553> <t554>She was no more than eleven years old.</t554> <t555>She had made the arduous journey from Manaure with some fur traders who were tasked with delivering her along with a letter to the house of José Arcadio Buendía, but they could not precisely explain who had asked them to do the favor.</t555> <t556>All her luggage consisted of a small trunk of clothes, a small wooden rocking chair with hand-painted colorful flowers, and a canvas sack that made a constant cloc cloc cloc noise, in which she carried her parents' bones.</t556> <t557>The letter addressed to José Arcadio Buendía was written in very affectionate terms by someone who still loved him very much despite the time and distance and who felt compelled by a basic sense of humanity to do the charity of sending him this poor helpless orphan girl, who was Úrsula's second cousin and therefore also a relative of José Arcadio Buendía, although more distantly, because she was the daughter of that unforgettable friend Nicanor Ulloa and his very dignified wife Rebeca Montiel, whom God had in His holy kingdom, whose remains were enclosed with the letter so they could be given a Christian burial.</t557> <t558>Both the mentioned names and the signature of the letter were perfectly legible, but neither José Arcadio Buendía nor Úrsula remembered having relatives with those names nor did they know anyone named like the sender, much less in the remote town of Manaure.</t558> <t559>It was impossible to obtain any additional information through the girl.</t559> <t560>From the moment she arrived, she sat sucking her thumb in the rocking chair and observing everyone with her large frightened eyes, without giving any sign of understanding what she was being asked.</t560> <t561>She wore a black-dyed diagonal dress, worn from use, and some chipped patent leather boots.</t561> <t562>Her hair was held behind her ears with black ribbon bows.</t562> <t563>She wore a scapular with images faded by sweat and on her right wrist a carnivorous animal's fang mounted on a copper support as a charm against the evil eye.</t563> <t564>Her green skin, her round and tense belly like a drum, revealed poor health and a hunger older than herself, but when they gave her food, she kept the plate on her lap without tasting it.</t564> <t565>It was even believed that she was deaf-mute, until the Indians asked her in their language if she wanted some water, and she moved her eyes as if she recognized them and nodded yes with her head.</t565>
<t566>They stayed with her because there was no other choice.</t566><t567>They decided to call her Rebeca, which according to the letter was her mother's name, because Aureliano had the patience to read the entire list of saints' names to her and she did not react to any of them.</t567><t568>Since at that time there was no cemetery in Macondo, as no one had died until then, they kept the sack with the bones waiting for a worthy place to bury them, and for a long time they were in the way everywhere and were found where least expected, always with their clucking like a brooding hen.</t568><t569>A long time passed before Rebeca joined the family life.</t569><t570>She would sit in the little rocking chair sucking her thumb in the most secluded corner of the house.</t570><t571>Nothing caught her attention except the music of the clocks, which she searched for with frightened eyes every half hour, as if expecting to find it somewhere in the air.</t571><t572>They couldn't get her to eat for several days.</t572><t573>Nobody understood how she hadn't died of hunger, until the indigenous people, who noticed everything because they roamed the house incessantly with their stealthy feet, discovered that Rebeca only liked to eat the damp earth from the patio and the lime cakes she scraped off the walls with her nails.</t573><t574>It was evident that her parents, or whoever had raised her, had scolded her for this habit, as she practiced it secretly and with a sense of guilt, trying to hide the portions to eat them when no one was watching.</t574><t575>From then on, they subjected her to relentless surveillance.</t575><t576>They sprinkled cow bile in the patio and smeared hot pepper on the walls, believing they could defeat her pernicious habit with these methods, but she showed such signs of cunning and ingenuity to procure the earth that Úrsula was forced to employ more drastic measures.</t576><t577>She would put orange juice with rhubarb in a pot and leave it out overnight, giving her the potion the next morning on an empty stomach.</t577><t578>Although no one had told her that this was the specific remedy for the habit of eating earth, she thought that any bitter substance on an empty stomach had to make the liver react.</t578><t579>Rebeca was so rebellious and strong despite her rickets that they had to hold her down like a calf to make her swallow the medicine, and they could barely restrain her tantrums and endure the convoluted hieroglyphics she alternated with bites and spit, which according to the scandalized indigenous people were the grossest obscenities conceivable in their language.</t579><t580>When Úrsula found out, she complemented the treatment with lashings.</t580><t581>It was never established whether it was the rhubarb or the beatings, or both combined, that took effect, but the truth is that within a few weeks Rebeca began to show signs of recovery.</t581><t582>She participated in the games of Arcadio and Amaranta, who welcomed her as an older sister, and ate with appetite using the cutlery properly.</t582><t583>It soon became apparent that she spoke Spanish as fluently as the indigenous language, that she had a remarkable skill for manual crafts, and that she sang the waltz of the clocks with a very funny lyric she had invented herself.</t583><t584>They soon considered her as another member of the family.</t584><t585>She was more affectionate with Úrsula than her own children ever were, and she called Amaranta and Arcadio little siblings, Aureliano uncle, and José Arcadio Buendía grandpa.</t585><t586>Thus, she ended up deserving as much as the others the name of Rebeca Buendía, the only one she ever had and carried with dignity until her death.</t586>
<t587>One night, around the time when Rebeca was cured of the habit of eating dirt and was taken to sleep in the room with the other children, the Indian woman who slept with them woke up by chance and heard a strange intermittent noise in the corner.</t587> <t588>Alarmed, she sat up, believing that an animal had entered the room, and then saw Rebeca in the rocking chair, sucking her thumb and with eyes glowing like those of a cat in the dark.</t588> <t589>Stunned with terror, troubled by the fatality of her destiny, Visitación recognized in those eyes the symptoms of the disease whose threat had forced her and her brother to exile themselves forever from a millennial kingdom in which they were princes.</t589> <t590>It was the plague of insomnia.</t590>
<t591>Cataure, the Indian, did not wake up in the house.</t591> <t592>His sister stayed, because her fatalistic heart indicated to her that the lethal ailment would pursue her no matter what to the last corner of the earth.</t592> <t593>Nobody in the house understood Visitación's alarm.</t593> <t594>"If we don't go back to sleep, even better," said José Arcadio Buendía, in good spirits.</t594> <t595>"That way life will be more productive for us."</t595> <t596>But the Indian woman explained to them that the most fearsome aspect of the insomnia disease was not the inability to sleep, since the body did not feel any fatigue, but its inexorable progression towards a more critical manifestation: forgetfulness.</t596> <t597>She meant that when the patient got used to their state of wakefulness, childhood memories began to fade from their memory, then the name and notion of things, and finally the identity of people and even the awareness of one's own self, until sinking into a kind of idiocy without a past.</t597> <t598>José Arcadio Buendía, laughing heartily, considered it to be one of the many ailments invented by the superstition of the indigenous people.</t598> <t599>But Úrsula, just in case, took the precaution of separating Rebeca from the other children.</t599>
<t600>After several weeks, when Visitación's terror seemed to have subsided, José Arcadio Buendía found himself one night tossing and turning in bed, unable to sleep.</t600> <t601>Úrsula, who had also awakened, asked him what was wrong, and he replied: "I'm thinking about Prudencio Aguilar again."</t601> <t602>They didn't sleep a minute, but the next day they felt so rested that they forgot about the bad night.</t602> <t603>Aureliano commented in amazement at lunchtime that he felt very well despite having spent the whole night in the laboratory gilding a brooch he planned to give Úrsula on her birthday.</t603> <t604>They didn't become alarmed until the third day, when at bedtime they felt wide awake and realized they had been awake for more than fifty hours.</t604>
<t605>—The children are also awake —said the indigenous woman with her fatalistic conviction—.</t605> <t606>Once it enters the house, no one escapes the plague.</t606>
<t607>They had indeed contracted the disease of insomnia.</t607> <t608>Úrsula, who had learned from her mother the medicinal value of plants, prepared and made everyone drink a concoction of aconite, but they did not manage to sleep; instead, they spent the whole day dreaming awake.</t608> <t609>In that state of hallucinatory lucidity, they not only saw the images of their own dreams, but also those dreamed by others.</t609> <t610>It was as if the house had been filled with visitors.</t610> <t611>Sitting in her rocking chair in a corner of the kitchen, Rebeca dreamed that a man very much like her, dressed in white linen and with the collar of his shirt closed by a gold button, brought her a bouquet of roses.</t611> <t612>He was accompanied by a woman with delicate hands who took a rose and placed it in the girl's hair.</t612> <t613>Úrsula understood that the man and woman were Rebeca's parents, but although she made a great effort to recognize them, she confirmed her certainty that she had never seen them before.</t613> <t614>Meanwhile, due to a carelessness that José Arcadio Buendía never forgave himself for, the little candy animals made in the house continued to be sold in the town.</t614> <t615>Children and adults delightedly sucked on the delicious green roosters of insomnia, the exquisite pink fish of insomnia, and the tender yellow horses of insomnia, so that the dawn of Monday found the whole town awake.</t615> <t616>At first, no one was alarmed.</t616> <t617>On the contrary, they were glad not to sleep, because there was so much to do in Macondo that time was barely enough.</t617> <t618>They worked so much that soon they had nothing left to do, and they found themselves at three in the morning with their arms crossed, counting the number of notes in the waltz of the clocks.</t618> <t619>Those who wanted to sleep, not out of tiredness but out of nostalgia for dreams, resorted to all kinds of exhausting methods.</t619> <t620>They gathered to talk without pause, to repeat the same jokes for hours and hours, to complicate the story of the capon rooster to the limits of exasperation, which was an infinite game where the narrator would ask if they wanted him to tell the story of the capon rooster, and when they answered yes, the narrator would say he hadn't asked them to say yes, but if they wanted him to tell the story of the capon rooster, and when they answered no, the narrator would say he hadn't asked them to say no, but if they wanted him to tell the story of the capon rooster, and when they stayed silent, the narrator would say he hadn't asked them to stay silent, but if they wanted him to tell the story of the capon rooster, and no one could leave, because the narrator would say he hadn't asked them to leave, but if they wanted him to tell the story of the capon rooster, and so on, in a vicious circle that lasted entire nights.</t620>
<t621>When José Arcadio Buendía realized that the plague had invaded the town, he gathered the heads of families to explain what he knew about the insomnia disease, and measures were agreed upon to prevent the scourge from spreading to other populations in the swamp.</t621> <t622>Thus, the bells were removed from the goats that the Arabs traded for macaws, and they were placed at the entrance of the town for those who ignored the advice and pleas of the sentinels and insisted on visiting the population.</t622> <t623>All the outsiders who roamed the streets of Macondo at that time had to ring their bell so that the sick would know they were healthy.</t623> <t624>They were not allowed to eat or drink anything during their stay, as there was no doubt that the disease was only transmitted through the mouth, and all food and drink were contaminated with insomnia.</t624> <t625>In this way, the plague was kept confined to the perimeter of the population.</t625> <t626>The quarantine was so effective that the day came when the emergency situation was considered natural, and life was organized in such a way that work regained its rhythm and no one worried anymore about the useless habit of sleeping.</t626>
<t627>It was Aureliano who conceived the formula that would defend them for several months from the evasions of memory.</t627> <t628>He discovered it by chance.</t628> <t629>An expert insomniac, having been one of the first, he had learned the art of silversmithing to perfection.</t629> <t630>One day he was looking for the small anvil he used to laminate metals, and he couldn't remember its name.</t630> <t631>His father told him: "tas."</t631> <t632>Aureliano wrote the name on a piece of paper and glued it to the base of the little anvil: tas.</t632> <t633>That way he was sure he wouldn't forget it in the future.</t633> <t634>It didn't occur to him that this was the first manifestation of forgetfulness, because the object had a name that was difficult to remember.</t634> <t635>But a few days later he discovered that he had difficulty remembering almost everything in the laboratory.</t635> <t636>So he labeled them with their respective names, so that it was enough to read the inscription to identify them.</t636> <t637>When his father expressed his alarm at having forgotten even the most impressive events of his childhood, Aureliano explained his method to him, and José Arcadio Buendía put it into practice throughout the house and later imposed it on the entire town.</t637> <t638>With an inked swab, he marked each thing with its name: table, chair, clock, door, wall, bed, pot.</t638> <t639>He went to the corral and labeled the animals and plants: cow, goat, pig, hen, yucca, taro, banana.</t639> <t640>Little by little, studying the infinite possibilities of forgetfulness, he realized that a day might come when things would be recognized by their inscriptions, but their utility would not be remembered.</t640> <t641>Then he became more explicit.</t641> <t642>The sign he hung around the cow's neck was an exemplary display of how the inhabitants of Macondo were willing to fight against forgetfulness: This is the cow, it must be milked every morning so that it produces milk and the milk must be boiled to mix it with coffee and make coffee with milk.</t642> <t643>Thus they continued living in a slippery reality, momentarily captured by words, but which would inevitably escape when they forgot the values of the written letter.</t643>
<t644>At the entrance of the swamp road, a sign had been put up that said Macondo and another larger one on the main street that said God exists.</t644> <t645>In all the houses, keys had been written to memorize objects and feelings.</t645> <t646>But the system required so much vigilance and moral strength that many succumbed to the spell of an imaginary reality, invented by themselves, which was less practical but more comforting.</t646> <t647>Pilar Ternera was the one who contributed the most to popularizing this mystification, when she conceived the artifice of reading the past in the cards as she had previously read the future.</t647> <t648>Through this resource, the insomniacs began to live in a world constructed by the uncertain alternatives of the cards, where the father was barely remembered as the dark-skinned man who had arrived at the beginning of April and the mother was barely remembered as the olive-skinned woman who wore a gold ring on her left hand, and where a birth date was reduced to the last Tuesday when the lark sang in the laurel tree.</t648> <t649>Defeated by those practices of consolation, José Arcadio Buendía then decided to build the memory machine that he had once desired to remember the marvelous inventions of the gypsies.</t649> <t650>The device was based on the possibility of reviewing every morning, from beginning to end, the entirety of the knowledge acquired in life.</t650> <t651>He imagined it as a rotating dictionary that an individual situated at the axis could operate by means of a crank, so that in a few hours the most necessary notions for living would pass before their eyes.</t651> <t652>He had managed to write about fourteen thousand cards when an eccentric old man appeared on the swamp road with the sad bell of the sleepers, carrying a bulging suitcase tied with ropes and a cart covered with black cloths.</t652> <t653>He went directly to the house of José Arcadio Buendía.</t653>
<t654>Visitación did not recognize him when she opened the door, and she thought he intended to sell something, unaware that nothing could be sold in a town that was hopelessly sinking into the quagmire of oblivion.</t654> <t655>He was a decrepit man.</t655> <t656>Although his voice was also cracked with uncertainty and his hands seemed to doubt the existence of things, it was evident that he came from a world where men could still sleep and remember.</t656> <t657>José Arcadio Buendía found him sitting in the living room, fanning himself with a patched black hat, while he read the signs pasted on the walls with compassionate attention.</t657> <t658>He greeted him with wide displays of affection, fearing he had known him in another time and now could not remember.</t658> <t659>But the visitor noticed his falseness.</t659> <t660>He felt forgotten, not with the remediable forgetfulness of the heart, but with another more cruel and irrevocable forgetfulness that he knew very well, because it was the forgetfulness of death.</t660> <t661>Then he understood.</t661> <t662>He opened the suitcase crammed with indecipherable objects, and from among them he took out a briefcase with many bottles.</t662> <t663>He gave José Arcadio Buendía a drink of a calm-colored substance, and light dawned in his memory.</t663> <t664>Tears moistened his eyes, before he saw himself in an absurd room where the objects were labeled, and before he was ashamed of the solemn nonsense written on the walls, and even before he recognized the newcomer in a dazzling flash of joy.</t664> <t665>It was Melquíades.</t665>
<t666>While Macondo celebrated the reconquest of memories, José Arcadio Buendía and Melquíades dusted off their old friendship.</t666><t667>The gypsy was determined to stay in the town.</t667><t668>He had indeed been in death, but he had returned because he could not bear the loneliness.</t668><t669>Repudiated by his tribe, deprived of all supernatural faculties as punishment for his fidelity to life, he decided to take refuge in that corner of the world not yet discovered by death, dedicated to the operation of a daguerreotype laboratory.</t669><t670>José Arcadio Buendía had never heard of that invention.</t670><t671>But when he saw himself and his entire family captured in an eternal age on an iridescent metal plate, he was left speechless with astonishment.</t671><t672>From that time dated the rusted daguerreotype in which José Arcadio Buendía appeared with bristly and ashen hair, the stiff collar of his shirt fastened with a copper button, and an expression of astonished solemnity, which Úrsula described, laughing to death, as "a frightened general."</t672><t673>In truth, José Arcadio Buendía was frightened on that clear December morning when they took the daguerreotype, because he thought that people would gradually wear out as their image passed onto the metal plates.</t673><t674>By a curious inversion of custom, it was Úrsula who got that idea out of his head, as it was also she who forgot her old resentments and decided that Melquíades should stay living in the house, although she never allowed them to take a daguerreotype of her because (in her own exact words) she did not want to be a laughingstock for her grandchildren.</t674><t675>That morning, she dressed the children in their best clothes, powdered their faces, and gave each of them a spoonful of marrow syrup so they could remain absolutely still for almost two minutes in front of Melquíades' cumbersome camera.</t675><t676>In the family daguerreotype, the only one that ever existed, Aureliano appeared dressed in black velvet, between Amaranta and Rebeca.</t676><t677>He had the same languor and the same clairvoyant look that he would have years later in front of the firing squad, but he had not yet felt the premonition of his destiny.</t677><t678>He was an expert goldsmith, esteemed throughout the swamp for the meticulousness of his work.</t678><t679>In the workshop he shared with Melquíades' chaotic laboratory, he was barely heard breathing.</t679><t680>He seemed to be sheltered in another time, while his father and the gypsy loudly interpreted Nostradamus' predictions, amid a clatter of bottles and tubs, and the disaster of spilled acids and lost silver bromide due to the constant jostling and stumbling.</t680><t681>That dedication to work, the good judgment with which he managed his interests, had allowed Aureliano to earn more money in a short time than Úrsula with her delightful candy animals, but everyone was surprised that he was already a grown man and had not been known to have a woman.</t681><t682>In reality, he had never had one.</t682>
<t683>Months later, Francisco the Man returned, an elderly wanderer of almost 200 years who frequently passed through Macondo, spreading the songs he composed himself.</t683> <t684>In them, Francisco the Man recounted with meticulous details the news that occurred in the towns along his route, from Manaure to the edges of the swamp, so that if someone had a message to send or an event to publicize, they paid him two cents to include it in his repertoire.</t684> <t685>This was how Úrsula learned of her mother's death, purely by chance, one night when she was listening to the songs hoping they would say something about her son José Arcadio.</t685> <t686>Francisco the Man, so called because he defeated the devil in a duel of improvised songs, and whose real name no one knew, disappeared from Macondo during the insomnia plague and one night reappeared without any announcement in Catarino's store.</t686> <t687>The whole town went to listen to him to find out what had happened in the world.</t687> <t688>On this occasion, he arrived with a woman so fat that four Indians had to carry her in a rocker, and a teenage mulatto girl with a helpless appearance who protected her from the sun with an umbrella.</t688> <t689>Aureliano went to Catarino's store that night.</t689> <t690>He found Francisco the Man, like a monolithic chameleon, sitting in the middle of a circle of curious onlookers.</t690> <t691>He sang the news with his old, hoarse voice, accompanying himself with the same archaic accordion that Sir Walter Raleigh had given him in Guiana, while he kept the rhythm with his large, salt-cracked walking feet.</t691> <t692>In front of a back door through which some men were entering and leaving, the matron of the rocker was sitting and fanning herself in silence.</t692> <t693>Catarino, with a felt rose in his ear, was selling bowls of fermented guarapo to the crowd, and took the opportunity to approach the men and put his hand where he shouldn't.</t693> <t694>By midnight, the heat was unbearable.</t694> <t695>Aureliano listened to the news until the end without finding any that interested his family.</t695> <t696>He was about to return home when the matron signaled to him with her hand.</t696>
<t697>—You come in too —he said to him—.</t697> <t698>It only costs twenty cents.</t698>
<t699>Aureliano tossed a coin into the piggy bank that the matron had on her lap and entered the room without knowing why.</t699><t700>The teenage mulatto girl, with her little dog-like breasts, was naked on the bed.</t700><t701>Before Aureliano, that night, sixty-three men had passed through the room.</t701><t702>From so much use, and kneaded in sweat and sighs, the air in the room was beginning to turn into mud.</t702><t703>The girl removed the soaked sheet and asked Aureliano to hold it on one side.</t703><t704>It was as heavy as a canvas.</t704><t705>They wrung it out, twisting it by the ends, until it regained its natural weight.</t705><t706>They turned the mat over, and the sweat came out the other side.</t706><t707>Aureliano longed for that operation to never end.</t707><t708>He knew the theoretical mechanics of love, but he couldn't stand up due to the discouragement in his knees, and although his skin was bristling and burning, he couldn't resist the urge to expel the weight from his guts.</t708><t709>When the girl finished fixing the bed and ordered him to undress, he gave her a flustered explanation: "They made me come in.</t709><t710>They told me to toss twenty cents into the piggy bank and not to take too long."</t710><t711>The girl understood his confusion.</t711><t712>"If you toss another twenty cents on your way out, you can take a little longer," she said softly.</t712><t713>Aureliano undressed, tormented by shame, unable to shake the idea that his nakedness couldn't compare to his brother's.</t713><t714>Despite the girl's efforts, he felt increasingly indifferent and terribly alone.</t714><t715>"I'll toss another twenty cents," he said in a desolate voice.</t715><t716>The girl thanked him in silence.</t716><t717>Her back was raw.</t717><t718>Her skin clung to her ribs and her breathing was altered by an unfathomable exhaustion.</t718><t719>Two years earlier, far from there, she had fallen asleep without extinguishing the candle and had woken up surrounded by fire.</t719><t720>The house where she lived with the grandmother who had raised her was reduced to ashes.</t720><t721>Since then, her grandmother had taken her from town to town, laying her down for twenty cents, to pay for the value of the burned house.</t721><t722>According to the girl's calculations, she still had about ten years of seventy men per night left, because she also had to pay for the travel and food expenses for both of them and the wages of the Indians who carried the rocker.</t722><t723>When the matron knocked on the door for the second time, Aureliano left the room without having done anything, dazed by the desire to cry.</t723><t724>That night he couldn't sleep thinking about the girl, with a mixture of desire and pity.</t724><t725>He felt an irresistible need to love and protect her.</t725><t726>At dawn, exhausted by insomnia and fever, he made the serene decision to marry her to free her from the grandmother's despotism and enjoy every night the satisfaction she gave to seventy men.</t726><t727>But at ten in the morning, when he arrived at Catarino's store, the girl had left town.</t727>
<t728>The passage of time calmed his reckless purpose, but aggravated his sense of frustration.</t728> <t729>He took refuge in work.</t729> <t730>He resigned himself to being a man without a woman for his entire life to hide the shame of his uselessness.</t730> <t731>Meanwhile, Melquíades finished capturing everything that could be captured in Macondo on his plates, and abandoned the daguerreotype laboratory to the delusions of José Arcadio Buendía, who had resolved to use it to obtain scientific proof of the existence of God.</t731> <t732>Through a complicated process of superimposed exposures taken in different parts of the house, he was sure he would sooner or later make a daguerreotype of God, if He existed, or put an end once and for all to the supposition of His existence.</t732> <t733>Melquíades delved into the interpretations of Nostradamus.</t733> <t734>He stayed up very late, suffocating in his faded velvet vest, scribbling papers with his tiny sparrow-like hands, whose rings had lost the luster of another era.</t734> <t735>One night he thought he found a prediction about the future of Macondo.</t735> <t736>It would be a luminous city, with large glass houses, where no trace of the Buendía lineage remained.</t736> <t737>“It’s a mistake,” thundered José Arcadio Buendía.</t737> <t738>“They won’t be glass houses but ice houses, as I dreamed, and there will always be a Buendía, for centuries and centuries.”</t738> <t739>In that extravagant house, Úrsula struggled to preserve common sense, having expanded the candy animal business with an oven that produced baskets and baskets of bread all night and a prodigious variety of puddings, meringues, and sponge cakes, which vanished in a few hours through the swamp’s pathways.</t739> <t740>She had reached an age where she had the right to rest, but was, however, increasingly active.</t740> <t741>She was so busy with her prosperous enterprises that one afternoon she looked distractedly towards the patio while the Indian woman helped her sweeten the dough, and saw two unknown and beautiful adolescents embroidering in a hoop by the twilight light.</t741> <t742>They were Rebeca and Amaranta.</t742> <t743>They had just taken off their grandmother’s mourning clothes, which they had kept with inflexible rigor for three years, and the colored clothes seemed to have given them a new place in the world.</t743> <t744>Rebeca, contrary to what might have been expected, was the most beautiful.</t744> <t745>She had a clear complexion, large and calm eyes, and magical hands that seemed to weave the embroidery’s pattern with invisible threads.</t745> <t746>Amaranta, the younger one, was a bit graceless, but she had the natural distinction, the inner poise of the dead grandmother.</t746> <t747>Next to them, although already revealing his father’s physical drive, Arcadio seemed like a child.</t747> <t748>He had dedicated himself to learning the art of silversmithing with Aureliano, who had also taught him to read and write.</t748> <t749>Úrsula suddenly realized that the house was filled with people, that her children were about to marry and have children, and that they would be forced to disperse due to lack of space.</t749> <t750>Then she took out the money accumulated over long years of hard work, made commitments with her clients, and undertook the expansion of the house.</t750> <t751>She arranged for the construction of a formal living room for visitors, another more comfortable and cool for daily use, a dining room for a table with twelve places where the family could sit with all their guests; nine bedrooms with windows facing the patio and a long corridor protected from the midday glare by a rose garden, with a handrail for placing fern pots and begonia planters.</t751> <t752>She arranged to expand the kitchen to build two ovens, to destroy the old barn where Pilar Ternera had read José Arcadio’s future, and to build another twice as large so that food would never be lacking in the house.</t752> <t753>She arranged to build in the patio, under the shade of the chestnut tree, a bathroom for women and another for men, and at the back a large stable, a wired chicken coop, a milking stable, and an aviary open to the four winds so that the aimless birds could settle as they pleased.</t753> <t754>Followed by dozens of masons and carpenters, as if she had contracted her husband’s hallucinatory fever, Úrsula ordered the positioning of the light and the conduct of the heat, and distributed the space without the slightest sense of its limits.</t754> <t755>The primitive construction of the founders was filled with tools and materials, with workers overwhelmed by sweat, who asked everyone to please not get in the way, without realizing that it was they who were in the way, exasperated by the bag of human bones that followed them everywhere with its dull rattling.</t755> <t756>In that discomfort, breathing quicklime and tar molasses, no one quite understood how from the bowels of the earth emerged not only the largest house that would ever be in the town, but the most hospitable and cool that there ever was in the swamp’s domain.</t756> <t757>José Arcadio Buendía, trying to surprise Divine Providence in the midst of the cataclysm, was the one who understood it the least.</t757> <t758>The new house was almost finished when Úrsula pulled him out of his chimerical world to inform him that there was an order to paint the facade blue, not white as they wanted.</t758> <t759>She showed him the official order written on a piece of paper.</t759> <t760>José Arcadio Buendía, without understanding what his wife was saying, deciphered the signature.</t760>
<t761>—Who is this guy? —he asked.</t761>
<t762>—The magistrate —said Úrsula disconsolately—.</t762> <t763>They say he is an authority sent by the government.</t763>
<t764>Don Apolinar Moscote, the magistrate, had arrived in Macondo without making any noise.</t764> <t765>He got off at Jacob's Hotel —established by one of the first Arabs who arrived bartering trinkets for macaws— and the next day he rented a small room with a door to the street, two blocks from the Buendía house.</t765> <t766>He set up a table and a chair that he bought from Jacob, nailed a republic shield he had brought with him to the wall, and painted the sign on the door: Magistrate.</t766> <t767>His first order was to have all the houses painted blue to celebrate the anniversary of national independence.</t767> <t768>José Arcadio Buendía, with a copy of the order in hand, found him taking a nap in a hammock he had hung in the sparse office.</t768> <t769>“Did you write this paper?” he asked him.</t769> <t770>Don Apolinar Moscote, a mature, timid man of sanguine complexion, answered yes.</t770> <t771>“With what right?” José Arcadio Buendía asked again.</t771> <t772>Don Apolinar Moscote searched for a paper in the desk drawer and showed it to him: “I have been appointed magistrate of this town.”</t772> <t773>José Arcadio Buendía didn't even look at the appointment.</t773>
<t774>—In this town, we don't rule with papers —he said without losing his calm—.</t774> <t775>And just so you know once and for all, we don't need any magistrate because there's nothing to correct here.</t775>
<t776>In the face of Don Apolinar Moscote's impassiveness, always without raising his voice, he gave a detailed account of how they had founded the village, how they had divided the land, opened the roads, and introduced the improvements that necessity had demanded, without bothering any government and without anyone bothering them.</t776> <t777>"We are so peaceful that we haven't even died of natural causes," he said.</t777> <t778>"You see, we still don't have a cemetery."</t778> <t779>He did not complain that the government had not helped them.</t779> <t780>On the contrary, he was glad that until then it had let them grow in peace, and he hoped it would continue to do so, because they had not founded a town for the first newcomer to come and tell them what they should do.</t780> <t781>Don Apolinar Moscote had put on a drill jacket, as white as his pants, without losing the purity of his gestures at any moment.</t781>
<t782>—So if you want to stay here, like any other ordinary citizen, you are very welcome —concluded José Arcadio Buendía—.</t782> <t783>But if you come to create disorder by forcing people to paint their houses blue, you can pack your things and leave the way you came.</t783> <t784>Because my house must be white like a dove.</t784>
<t785>Don Apolinar Moscote turned pale.</t785> <t786>He took a step back and clenched his jaws to say with a certain distress:</t786>
<t787>—I want to warn you that I am armed.</t787>
<t788>José Arcadio Buendía did not know at what moment the youthful strength with which he could knock down a horse came into his hands.</t788> <t789>He grabbed Don Apolinar Moscote by the lapel and lifted him to the height of his eyes.</t789>
<t790>—I do this —he told him— because I prefer to carry him alive and not have to keep carrying him dead for the rest of my life.</t790>
<t791>He carried him down the middle of the street, suspended by his lapels, until he set him on his two feet on the path to the swamp.</t791> <t792>A week later, he was back with six barefoot and ragged soldiers, armed with shotguns, and an ox cart in which his wife and seven daughters traveled.</t792> <t793>Later, two more carts arrived with the furniture, trunks, and household utensils.</t793> <t794>He installed the family in the Hotel de Jacob while he found a house, and reopened the office protected by the soldiers.</t794> <t795>The founders of Macondo, determined to expel the invaders, went with their eldest sons to offer their services to José Arcadio Buendía.</t795> <t796>But he opposed it, as he explained, because Don Apolinar Moscote had returned with his wife and daughters, and it was not a man's thing to humiliate others in front of their family.</t796> <t797>So he decided to resolve the situation amicably.</t797>
<t798>Aureliano accompanied him.</t798> <t799>By then, he had already started to grow a black mustache with waxed tips, and he had the slightly stentorian voice that would characterize him in the war.</t799> <t800>Unarmed, ignoring the guard, they entered the corregidor's office.</t800> <t801>Don Apolinar Moscote did not lose his composure.</t801> <t802>He introduced them to two of his daughters who happened to be there by chance: Amparo, sixteen years old, dark-skinned like her mother, and Remedios, barely nine years old, a beautiful girl with lily-white skin and green eyes.</t802> <t803>They were graceful and well-mannered.</t803> <t804>As soon as they entered, before being introduced, the girls brought them chairs to sit down.</t804> <t805>But both remained standing.</t805>
<t806>—Very well, my friend —said José Arcadio Buendía—, you will stay here, but not because you have those blunderbuss bandits at the door, rather out of consideration for your wife and daughters.</t806>
<t807>Don Apolinar Moscote was taken aback, but José Arcadio Buendía did not give him time to reply.</t807> <t808>“We only have two conditions,” he added.</t808> <t809>“The first: that everyone paints their house whatever color they want.</t809> <t810>The second: that the soldiers leave immediately.</t810> <t811>We guarantee the order.”</t811> <t812>The magistrate raised his right hand with all his fingers extended.</t812>
<t813>—Word of honor?</t813>
<t814>—Word of an enemy —said José Arcadio Buendía.</t814> <t815>And he added in a bitter tone—:</t815> <t816>Because there's one thing I want to tell you: you and I are still enemies.</t816>
<t817>That same afternoon the soldiers left.</t817> <t818>A few days later, José Arcadio Buendía found a house for the corregidor's family.</t818> <t819>Everyone was at peace, except Aureliano.</t819> <t820>The image of Remedios, the corregidor's youngest daughter, who could have been his own daughter due to her age, remained aching somewhere in his body.</t820> <t821>It was a physical sensation that almost bothered him when walking, like a small stone in his shoe.</t821>
<t822>The new house, white as a dove, was inaugurated with a dance.</t822> <t823>Úrsula had conceived that idea since the afternoon she saw Rebeca and Amaranta turned into teenagers, and it can almost be said that the main reason for the construction was the desire to provide the girls with a worthy place to receive visitors.</t823> <t824>So that nothing would diminish the splendor of that purpose, she worked like a galley slave while the renovations were being carried out, so that before they were finished, she had ordered costly items for decoration and service, and the wonderful invention that was to arouse the town's astonishment and the youth's joy: the player piano.</t824> <t825>They brought it in pieces, packed in several crates that were unloaded along with the Viennese furniture, Bohemian glassware, China Company dishes, Dutch tablecloths, and a rich variety of lamps and candlesticks, and vases, hangings, and tapestries.</t825> <t826>The importing house sent at their own expense an Italian expert, Pietro Crespi, to assemble and tune the player piano, instruct the buyers in its operation, and teach them to dance to the fashionable music printed on six rolls of paper.</t826>
<t827>Pietro Crespi was young and blond, the most handsome and well-mannered man that had been seen in Macondo, so meticulous in his dress that despite the suffocating heat he worked in a brocaded vest and a thick dark cloth coat.</t827><t828>Soaked in sweat, keeping a reverent distance from the owners of the house, he spent several weeks locked in the living room, with a dedication similar to Aureliano's in his goldsmith workshop.</t828><t829>One morning, without opening the door, without summoning any witness to the miracle, he placed the first roll in the pianola, and the tormenting hammering and the constant clatter of the wooden slats ceased in a silence of astonishment, before the order and cleanliness of the music.</t829><t830>Everyone rushed to the living room.</t830><t831>José Arcadio Buendía seemed struck not by the beauty of the melody, but by the autonomous keystrokes of the pianola, and he installed Melquíades' camera in the living room with the hope of obtaining a daguerreotype of the invisible performer.</t831><t832>That day the Italian had lunch with them.</t832><t833>Rebeca and Amaranta, serving the table, were intimidated by the fluidity with which that angelic man with pale hands and no rings handled the cutlery.</t833><t834>In the sitting room, adjacent to the living room, Pietro Crespi taught them to dance.</t834><t835>He indicated the steps without touching them, marking the rhythm with a metronome, under the kind supervision of Úrsula, who did not leave the room for a single moment while her daughters received the lessons.</t835><t836>Pietro Crespi wore special pants in those days, very flexible and tight, and dance slippers.</t836><t837>"You don't have to worry so much," José Arcadio Buendía told his wife.</t837><t838>"This man is a sissy."</t838><t839>But she did not give up her vigilance until the learning was over and the Italian left Macondo.</t839><t840>Then the organization of the party began.</t840><t841>Úrsula made a strict list of the guests, in which the only ones chosen were the descendants of the founders, except for Pilar Ternera's family, who had already had two other children by unknown fathers.</t841><t842>It was actually a class selection, only determined by feelings of friendship, as the favored ones were not only the oldest close friends of José Arcadio Buendía's house since before undertaking the exodus that culminated in the founding of Macondo, but their children and grandchildren were the usual companions of Aureliano and Arcadio since childhood, and their daughters were the only ones who visited the house to embroider with Rebeca and Amaranta.</t842><t843>Don Apolinar Moscote, the benevolent ruler whose actions were reduced to supporting two policemen armed with wooden clubs with his meager resources, was an ornamental authority.</t843><t844>To cope with household expenses, his daughters opened a sewing workshop, where they made felt flowers, guava snacks, and love notes on request.</t844><t845>But despite being modest and helpful, the most beautiful in town and the most skilled in the new dances, they could not get themselves considered for the party.</t845>
<t846>While Úrsula and the girls unpacked furniture, polished the dishes, and hung pictures of maidens in boats laden with roses, breathing new life into the bare spaces built by the masons, José Arcadio Buendía gave up the pursuit of the image of God, convinced of its nonexistence, and disassembled the player piano to decipher its secret magic.</t846> <t847>Two days before the party, bogged down in a mess of leftover pegs and hammers, fumbling among a tangle of strings that he unwound from one end only to have them wind up again at the other, he managed to roughly reassemble the instrument.</t847> <t848>There were never as many shocks and scurrying about as in those days, but the new tar lamps were lit on the scheduled date and time.</t848> <t849>The house opened, still smelling of resins and damp lime, and the children and grandchildren of the founders discovered the corridor of ferns and begonias, the silent rooms, the garden saturated with the fragrance of roses, and gathered in the visiting room in front of the unknown invention that had been covered with a white sheet.</t849> <t850>Those who knew the pianoforte, popular in other swamp towns, felt a bit disheartened, but Úrsula's disappointment was even more bitter when she placed the first roll for Amaranta and Rebeca to start the dance, and the mechanism didn't work.</t850> <t851>Melquíades, nearly blind and crumbling with decrepitude, resorted to the arts of his ancient wisdom to try to fix it.</t851> <t852>At last, José Arcadio Buendía accidentally moved a stuck device, and the music came out first in spurts, and then in a stream of convoluted notes.</t852> <t853>Striking against the strings placed without rhyme or reason and tuned with recklessness, the hammers went haywire.</t853> <t854>But the stubborn descendants of the twenty-one intrepid men who had explored the mountains seeking the sea to the west, eluded the pitfalls of the melodic mishmash, and the dance lasted until dawn.</t854>
<t855>Pietro Crespi went back to fixing the pianola.</t855> <t856>Rebeca and Amaranta helped him arrange the strings and joined in his laughter at the complexity of the waltzes.</t856> <t857>He was extremely affectionate, and so honest by nature that Úrsula gave up her vigilance.</t857> <t858>The night before his trip, a dance was improvised with the restored pianola to bid him farewell, and he gave a virtuosic demonstration of modern dances with Rebeca.</t858> <t859>Arcadio and Amaranta matched them in grace and skill.</t859> <t860>But the exhibition was interrupted because Pilar Ternera, who was at the door with the curious onlookers, got into a biting and hair-pulling fight with a woman who dared to comment that young Arcadio had a woman's buttocks.</t860> <t861>Around midnight, Pietro Crespi said goodbye with a sentimental little speech and promised to return very soon.</t861> <t862>Rebeca accompanied him to the door, and after closing the house and turning off the lamps, she went to her room to cry.</t862> <t863>It was an inconsolable cry that lasted for several days, and whose cause not even Amaranta knew.</t863> <t864>Her secrecy was not strange.</t864> <t865>Although she seemed expansive and cordial, she had a solitary character and an impenetrable heart.</t865> <t866>She was a splendid teenager, with long and firm bones, but she insisted on continuing to use the little wooden rocking chair she had arrived with, often reinforced and now devoid of arms.</t866> <t867>No one had discovered that even at that age she still had the habit of sucking her thumb.</t867> <t868>That's why she never missed a chance to lock herself in the bathroom, and she had acquired the habit of sleeping with her face turned against the wall.</t868> <t869>On rainy afternoons, while embroidering with a group of friends in the begonia corridor, she would lose the thread of the conversation and a tear of nostalgia would salt her palate when she saw the streaks of damp earth and the mounds of mud built by the worms in the garden.</t869> <t870>Those secret tastes, once defeated by oranges with rhubarb, burst into an irrepressible longing when she began to cry.</t870> <t871>She went back to eating dirt.</t871> <t872>The first time she did it almost out of curiosity, sure that the bad taste would be the best remedy against the temptation.</t872> <t873>And indeed, she couldn't stand the dirt in her mouth.</t873> <t874>But she persisted, overcome by the growing urge, and little by little she recovered the ancestral appetite, the taste for primary minerals, the unblemished satisfaction of the original food.</t874> <t875>She would stuff handfuls of dirt into her pockets and eat it grain by grain without being seen, with a confused feeling of joy and rage, while she trained her friends in the most difficult stitches and talked about other men who did not deserve the sacrifice of eating the lime off the walls for them.</t875> <t876>The handfuls of dirt made the only man who deserved such degradation less remote and more certain, as if the ground he walked on with his fine patent leather boots in another part of the world transmitted to her the weight and temperature of his blood in a mineral taste that left a rough ember in her mouth and a sediment of peace in her heart.</t876> <t877>One afternoon, without any reason, Amparo Moscote asked for permission to see the house.</t877> <t878>Amaranta and Rebeca, bewildered by the unexpected visit, attended to her with a hard formality.</t878> <t879>They showed her the renovated mansion, played the pianola rolls for her, and offered her orangeade with cookies.</t879> <t880>Amparo gave a lesson in dignity, personal charm, and good manners, which impressed Úrsula in the brief moments she attended the visit.</t880> <t881>After two hours, when the conversation began to languish, Amparo took advantage of a momentary distraction by Amaranta and handed a letter to Rebeca.</t881> <t882>She managed to see the name of the very distinguished Miss Rebeca Buendía, written in the same methodical handwriting, the same green ink, and the same precious arrangement of words as the instructions for operating the pianola, and she folded the letter with her fingertips and hid it in her bodice, looking at Amparo Moscote with an expression of endless and unconditional gratitude and a silent promise of complicity until death.</t882>
<t883>The sudden friendship between Amparo Moscote and Rebeca Buendía raised Aureliano's hopes.</t883> <t884>The memory of little Remedios had not stopped tormenting him, but he couldn't find the opportunity to see her.</t884> <t885>When he walked around the town with his closest friends, Magnífico Visbal and Gerineldo Márquez —sons of the founders with the same names—, he searched for her with anxious eyes in the sewing workshop and only saw the older sisters.</t885> <t886>The presence of Amparo Moscote in the house was like a premonition.</t886> <t887>"She has to come with her," Aureliano said to himself in a low voice.</t887> <t888>"She has to come."</t888> <t889>He repeated it so many times, and with such conviction, that one afternoon while he was assembling a little gold fish in the workshop, he was certain that she had responded to his call.</t889> <t890>Shortly after, indeed, he heard the little child's voice, and when he looked up with his heart frozen in fear, he saw the girl at the door in a pink organdy dress and white boots.</t890>
<t891>—Don't go in there, Remedios —said Amparo Moscote in the hallway—.</t891> <t892>They are working.</t892>
<t893>But Aureliano did not give him time to respond.</t893> <t894>He lifted the little golden fish hanging from a chain that came out of its mouth, and said to him:</t894>
<t895>—Come in.</t895>
<t896>Remedios approached and asked some questions about the little fish, which Aureliano could not answer because a sudden asthma attack prevented him.</t896> <t897>He wanted to stay forever next to that lily-like skin, next to those emerald eyes, very close to that voice that with each question called him sir with the same respect with which she addressed his father.</t897> <t898>Melquíades was in the corner, sitting at the desk, scribbling indecipherable signs.</t898> <t899>Aureliano hated him.</t899> <t900>He could do nothing except tell Remedios that he was going to give her the little fish, and the girl was so frightened by the offer that she hurriedly left the workshop.</t900> <t901>That afternoon, Aureliano lost the hidden patience with which he had waited for the chance to see her.</t901> <t902>He neglected his work.</t902> <t903>He called her many times, in desperate efforts of concentration, but Remedios did not respond.</t903> <t904>He looked for her in her sisters' workshop, in the curtains of her house, in her father's office, but he only found her in the image that saturated his own terrible solitude.</t904> <t905>He spent entire hours with Rebeca in the visiting room listening to the waltzes of the pianola.</t905> <t906>She listened to them because it was the music with which Pietro Crespi had taught her to dance.</t906> <t907>Aureliano listened to them simply because everything, even the music, reminded him of Remedios.</t907>
<t908>The house was filled with love.</t908><t909>Aureliano expressed it in verses that had neither beginning nor end.</t909><t910>He wrote them on the rough parchments that Melquíades gave him, on the bathroom walls, on the skin of his arms, and in all of them Remedios appeared transfigured: Remedios in the soporific air of two in the afternoon, Remedios in the silent breath of the roses, Remedios in the secret clepsydra of the moths, Remedios in the steam of the bread at dawn, Remedios everywhere and Remedios forever.</t910><t911>Rebeca waited for love at four in the afternoon, embroidering by the window.</t911><t912>She knew that the mail mule only arrived every fifteen days, but she always waited for it, convinced that it would arrive any day by mistake.</t912><t913>The opposite happened: once the mule did not arrive on the scheduled date.</t913><t914>Mad with desperation, Rebeca got up at midnight and ate handfuls of earth in the garden, with suicidal avidity, crying in pain and fury, chewing tender worms and splintering her molars with snail shells.</t914><t915>She vomited until dawn.</t915><t916>She sank into a state of feverish prostration, lost consciousness, and her heart opened in a shameless delirium.</t916><t917>Úrsula, scandalized, forced the lock of the trunk, and found at the bottom, tied with pink ribbons, the sixteen perfumed letters and the skeletons of leaves and petals preserved in old books and the dried butterflies that turned to dust when touched.</t917>
<t918>Aureliano was the only one capable of understanding such desolation.</t918> <t919>That afternoon, while Úrsula was trying to rescue Rebeca from the mangrove of delirium, he went with Magnífico Visbal and Gerineldo Márquez to Catarino's store.</t919> <t920>The establishment had been expanded with a gallery of wooden rooms where women who smelled of dead flowers lived alone.</t920> <t921>An ensemble of accordion and drums played the songs of Francisco the Man, who had disappeared from Macondo several years ago.</t921> <t922>The three friends drank fermented guarapo.</t922> <t923>Magnífico and Gerineldo, contemporaries of Aureliano but more experienced in worldly matters, drank methodically with women sitting on their laps.</t923> <t924>One of them, withered and with gold-capped teeth, gave Aureliano a shuddering caress.</t924> <t925>He rejected her.</t925> <t926>He had discovered that the more he drank, the more he remembered Remedios, but he could better endure the torture of her memory.</t926> <t927>He didn't know at what moment he began to float.</t927> <t928>He saw his friends and the women navigating in a radiant reverberation, without weight or volume, saying words that didn't come from their lips and making mysterious signals that didn't correspond to their gestures.</t928> <t929>Catarino put a hand on his back and said, "It's going to be eleven."</t929> <t930>Aureliano turned his head, saw the enormous disfigured face with a felt flower in the ear, and then he lost his memory, as in the times of oblivion, and regained it in a foreign dawn and in a room that was completely unfamiliar to him, where Pilar Ternera was in a slip, barefoot, disheveled, lighting him with a lamp and stunned with disbelief.</t930>
<t931>—Aureliano!</t931>
<t932>Aureliano stood firm on his feet and raised his head.</t932> <t933>He did not know how he had arrived there, but he knew the purpose, because he had carried it hidden since childhood in an inviolable corner of his heart.</t933>
<t934>—I have come to sleep with you —he said.</t934>
<t935>His clothes were smeared with mud and vomit.</t935> <t936>Pilar Ternera, who then lived only with her two younger children, did not ask him any questions.</t936> <t937>She took him to bed.</t937> <t938>She cleaned his face with a damp rag, took off his clothes, and then undressed completely under the mosquito net so her children wouldn't see her if they woke up.</t938> <t939>She had grown tired of waiting for the man who stayed, the men who left, the countless men who lost their way to her house confused by the uncertainty of the cards.</t939> <t940>In the waiting, her skin had cracked, her breasts had emptied, the embers of her heart had died out.</t940> <t941>She searched for Aureliano in the darkness, placed her hand on his belly, and kissed his neck with maternal tenderness.</t941> <t942>“My poor little boy,” she murmured.</t942> <t943>Aureliano shuddered.</t943> <t944>With a calm dexterity, without the slightest stumble, he left behind the cliffs of pain and found Remedios turned into a swamp without horizons, smelling of raw animal and freshly ironed clothes.</t944> <t945>When he surfaced, he was crying.</t945> <t946>First, there were involuntary and broken sobs.</t946> <t947>Then he emptied himself in an unleashed spring, feeling that something swollen and painful had burst inside him.</t947> <t948>She waited, scratching his head with her fingertips, until his body was emptied of the dark matter that wouldn't let him live.</t948> <t949>Then Pilar Ternera asked him: “Who is it?”</t949> <t950>And Aureliano told her.</t950> <t951>She let out the laugh that once scared the pigeons and now didn't even wake the children.</t951> <t952>“You'll have to finish raising her,” she mocked.</t952> <t953>But beneath the mockery, Aureliano found a haven of understanding.</t953> <t954>When he left the room, leaving behind not only the uncertainty of his manhood but also the bitter weight he had borne in his heart for so many months, Pilar Ternera had made him a spontaneous promise.</t954>
<t955>—I'm going to talk to the girl —he said to him—, and you'll see that I'll serve her to you on a silver platter.</t955>
<t956>He fulfilled it.</t956> <t957>But at a bad time, because the house had lost the peace of other days.</t957> <t958>Upon discovering Rebeca's passion, which could not be kept secret due to her screams, Amaranta suffered a bout of fever.</t958> <t959>She too suffered from the thorn of a solitary love.</t959> <t960>Locked in the bathroom, she relieved the torment of a hopeless passion by writing feverish letters that she contented herself with hiding at the bottom of the trunk.</t960> <t961>Úrsula barely managed to take care of the two sick women.</t961> <t962>She did not manage, through prolonged and insidious interrogations, to find out the causes of Amaranta's prostration.</t962> <t963>Finally, in another moment of inspiration, she forced the lock of the trunk and found the letters tied with pink ribbons, swollen with fresh lilies and still wet with tears, addressed to but never sent to Pietro Crespi.</t963> <t964>Crying with fury, she cursed the hour she thought of buying the player piano, prohibited embroidery classes, and decreed a sort of mourning without a dead person that was to last until the daughters gave up their hopes.</t964> <t965>José Arcadio Buendía's intervention was useless, as he had changed his first impression of Pietro Crespi and admired his skill in handling musical machines.</t965> <t966>So when Pilar Ternera told Aureliano that Remedios was determined to marry, he understood that the news would further trouble his parents.</t966> <t967>But he faced the situation.</t967> <t968>Summoned to the sitting room for a formal interview, José Arcadio Buendía and Úrsula listened impassively to their son's declaration.</t968> <t969>However, upon hearing the name of the bride, José Arcadio Buendía flushed with indignation.</t969> <t970>“Love is a plague,” he thundered.</t970> <t971>“With so many pretty and decent girls, the only thing you can think of is to marry the enemy's daughter.”</t971> <t972>But Úrsula agreed with the choice.</t972> <t973>She confessed her affection for the seven Moscote sisters, for their beauty, industriousness, modesty, and good upbringing, and celebrated her son's good judgment.</t973> <t974>Overcome by his wife's enthusiasm, José Arcadio Buendía then set one condition: Rebeca, who was reciprocated, would marry Pietro Crespi.</t974> <t975>Úrsula would take Amaranta on a trip to the provincial capital, when she had time, so that contact with different people would relieve her of her disappointment.</t975> <t976>Rebeca regained her health as soon as she learned of the agreement, and wrote her fiancé a joyful letter that she submitted for her parents' approval and sent by mail without using intermediaries.</t976> <t977>Amaranta pretended to accept the decision and gradually recovered from the fever, but she promised herself that Rebeca would only marry over her dead body.</t977>
<t978>On the following Saturday, José Arcadio Buendía put on the dark wool suit, the celluloid collar, and the suede boots that he had worn for the first time on the night of the party, and went to ask for the hand of Remedios Moscote.</t978> <t979>The magistrate and his wife received him both pleased and troubled, because they were unaware of the purpose of the unexpected visit, and then they thought that he had confused the name of the intended bride.</t979> <t980>To clear up the mistake, the mother woke Remedios and carried her in her arms to the living room, still dazed from sleep.</t980> <t981>They asked her if she was truly determined to get married, and she replied, whimpering, that she only wanted to be left to sleep.</t981> <t982>José Arcadio Buendía, understanding the confusion of the Moscotes, went to clarify things with Aureliano.</t982> <t983>When he returned, the Moscote couple had dressed in formal clothes, rearranged the furniture, and put fresh flowers in the vases, and they awaited him in the company of their older daughters.</t983> <t984>Weighed down by the ingratitude of the occasion and the discomfort of the stiff collar, José Arcadio Buendía confirmed that, indeed, Remedios was the chosen one.</t984> <t985>"This makes no sense," said a dismayed don Apolinar Moscote.</t985> <t986>"We have six more daughters, all single and of marriageable age, who would be delighted to be the worthy wives of serious and hardworking gentlemen like your son, and Aurelito sets his eyes precisely on the only one who still wets the bed."</t986> <t987>His wife, a well-preserved woman with sorrowful eyelids and gestures, reproached him for his impropriety.</t987> <t988>When they finished drinking the fruit shake, they had gladly accepted Aureliano's decision.</t988> <t989>Only that Mrs. Moscote begged the favor of speaking alone with Úrsula.</t989> <t990>Intrigued, protesting that she was being entangled in men's affairs, but actually intimidated by the emotion, Úrsula went to visit her the next day.</t990> <t991>Half an hour later she returned with the news that Remedios was prepubescent.</t991> <t992>Aureliano did not consider it a serious obstacle.</t992> <t993>He had waited so long that he could wait as long as necessary, until the bride was of childbearing age.</t993>
<t994>The regained harmony was only interrupted by the death of Melquíades.</t994> <t995>Although it was a foreseeable event, the circumstances were not.</t995> <t996>A few months after his return, he had undergone such a rapid and critical aging process that he was soon considered one of those useless great-grandfathers who wander like shadows through the bedrooms, dragging their feet, reminiscing aloud about better times, and whom no one really cares about or remembers until the day they are found dead in bed.</t996> <t997>At first, José Arcadio Buendía assisted him in his tasks, enthusiastic about the novelty of daguerreotype photography and Nostradamus's predictions.</t997> <t998>But little by little, he abandoned him to his solitude, as communication became increasingly difficult.</t998> <t999>He was losing his sight and hearing, seemed to confuse his interlocutors with people he had known in remote times of humanity, and answered questions with an intricate jumble of languages.</t999> <t1000>He walked feeling the air, although he moved among things with an inexplicable fluidity, as if endowed with an instinct for orientation based on immediate premonitions.</t1000> <t1001>One day he forgot to put in his dentures, which he left in a glass of water by the bed at night, and he never put them back in.</t1001> <t1002>When Úrsula arranged for the expansion of the house, she had a special room built for him next to Aureliano's workshop, away from the noise and domestic hustle, with a window flooded with light and a shelf where she herself arranged the books almost destroyed by dust and moths, the brittle papers filled with indecipherable signs, and the glass with the dentures where some aquatic plants with tiny yellow flowers had taken root.</t1002> <t1003>The new place seemed to please Melquíades, as he was no longer seen even in the dining room.</t1003> <t1004>He only went to Aureliano's workshop, where he spent hours and hours scribbling his enigmatic literature on the parchments he had brought with him, which seemed to be made of a dry material that crumbled like puff pastry.</t1004> <t1005>There he took the meals that Visitación brought him twice a day, although in recent times he lost his appetite and only ate vegetables.</t1005> <t1006>Soon he acquired the forlorn appearance typical of vegetarians.</t1006> <t1007>His skin became covered with a tender moss, similar to that which thrived on the anachronistic vest he never took off, and his breath exhaled a scent of a sleeping animal.</t1007> <t1008>Aureliano eventually forgot about him, absorbed in writing his verses, but on one occasion he thought he understood something from his droning monologues and paid attention.</t1008> <t1009>In reality, the only thing he could isolate from the stony paragraphs was the insistent hammering of the word equinox equinox equinox, and the name Alexander Von Humboldt.</t1009> <t1010>Arcadio got a little closer to him when he started helping Aureliano in the silversmithing.</t1010> <t1011>Melquíades reciprocated that effort of communication by sometimes uttering phrases in Spanish that had very little to do with reality.</t1011> <t1012>One afternoon, however, he seemed illuminated by a sudden emotion.</t1012> <t1013>Years later, in front of the firing squad, Arcadio would remember the tremor with which Melquíades made him listen to several pages of his impenetrable writing, which he of course did not understand, but which, when read aloud, seemed like sung encyclicals.</t1013> <t1014>Then he smiled for the first time in a long while and said in Spanish: "When I die, burn mercury in my room for three days."</t1014> <t1015>Arcadio told José Arcadio Buendía, and he tried to get more explicit information, but only got one response: "I have achieved immortality."</t1015> <t1016>When Melquíades's breath began to smell, Arcadio took him to bathe in the river on Thursday mornings.</t1016> <t1017>He seemed to improve.</t1017> <t1018>He would undress and get into the water along with the boys, and his mysterious sense of orientation allowed him to avoid deep and dangerous spots.</t1018> <t1019>"We are of the water," he said on one occasion.</t1019> <t1020>Thus, a long time passed without anyone seeing him in the house, except for the night he made a touching effort to repair the player piano, and when he went to the river with Arcadio carrying under his arm the gourd and the corozo soap ball wrapped in a towel.</t1020> <t1021>One Thursday, before they called him to go to the river, Aureliano heard him say: "I have died of fever in the sands of Singapore."</t1021> <t1022>That day he went into the water by a wrong path and was not found until the next morning, several kilometers downstream, stranded in a luminous bend with a solitary buzzard perched on his belly.</t1022> <t1023>Against the scandalized protests of Úrsula, who mourned him more deeply than her own father, José Arcadio Buendía opposed burying him.</t1023> <t1024>"He is immortal," he said, "and he himself revealed the formula for resurrection."</t1024> <t1025>He revived the forgotten athanor and set a cauldron of mercury to boil next to the corpse, which gradually filled with blue bubbles.</t1025> <t1026>Don Apolinar Moscote dared to remind him that an unburied drowned body was a danger to public health.</t1026> <t1027>"None of that, since he is alive," was José Arcadio Buendía's reply, who completed the seventy-two hours of mercurial fumigations when the corpse was already beginning to burst into a livid bloom, whose faint whistles filled the house with a pestilent vapor.</t1027> <t1028>Only then did he allow him to be buried, but not just in any way, but with the honors reserved for the greatest benefactor of Macondo.</t1028> <t1029>It was the first and most attended burial ever seen in the town, surpassed only a century later by the funeral carnival of the Mamá Grande.</t1029> <t1030>He was buried in a tomb erected in the center of the land designated for the cemetery, with a headstone that bore the only thing known about him: MELQUÍADES.</t1030> <t1031>They held his nine nights of wake.</t1031> <t1032>In the tumult gathered in the courtyard to drink coffee, tell jokes, and play cards, Amaranta found an opportunity to confess her love to Pietro Crespi, who a few weeks earlier had formalized his engagement to Rebeca and was setting up a store of musical instruments and wind-up toys in the same area where the Arabs, who once traded trinkets for macaws, vegetated, and which people knew as the Street of the Turks.</t1032> <t1033>The Italian, whose head covered with glossy curls aroused in women an irrepressible need to sigh, treated Amaranta like a capricious little girl not worth taking too seriously.</t1033>
<t1034>—I have a younger brother —he said to her—.</t1034> <t1035>He is going to come help me in the store.</t1035>
<t1036>Amaranta felt humiliated and told Pietro Crespi, with virulent resentment, that she was willing to prevent her sister's wedding even if she had to lay her own corpse across the door.</t1036> <t1037>The Italian was so impressed by the dramatism of the threat that he couldn't resist the temptation to discuss it with Rebeca.</t1037> <t1038>Thus, Amaranta's trip, always postponed due to Úrsula's occupations, was arranged in less than a week.</t1038> <t1039>Amaranta did not resist, but when she gave Rebeca the farewell kiss, she whispered in her ear:</t1039>
<t1040>—Don't get your hopes up.</t1040> <t1041>Even if they take me to the ends of the earth, I will find a way to stop you from getting married, even if I have to kill you.</t1041>
<t1042>With Úrsula's absence, and the invisible presence of Melquíades who continued his stealthy wandering through the rooms, the house seemed enormous and empty.</t1042> <t1043>Rebeca had been left in charge of domestic order, while the Indian woman took care of the bakery.</t1043> <t1044>At dusk, when Pietro Crespi arrived preceded by a fresh breath of lavender and always bringing a toy as a gift, his fiancée received him in the main room with doors and windows open to be safe from any suspicion.</t1044> <t1045>It was an unnecessary precaution, because the Italian had proven to be so respectful that he didn't even touch the hand of the woman who would be his wife within a year.</t1045> <t1046>Those visits gradually filled the house with marvelous toys.</t1046> <t1047>The wind-up dancers, the music boxes, the acrobat monkeys, the trotting horses, the drumming clowns, the rich and astonishing mechanical fauna that Pietro Crespi brought, dispelled José Arcadio Buendía's grief over Melquíades' death, and transported him back to his old alchemist days.</t1047> <t1048>He then lived in a paradise of gutted animals, dismantled mechanisms, trying to perfect them with a continuous motion system based on the principles of the pendulum.</t1048> <t1049>Aureliano, for his part, had neglected the workshop to teach little Remedios to read and write.</t1049> <t1050>At first, the girl preferred her dolls to the man who came every afternoon, and who was the reason she was taken away from her games to be bathed, dressed, and seated in the living room to receive the visit.</t1050> <t1051>But Aureliano's patience and devotion eventually won her over, to the point that she spent many hours with him studying the meaning of letters and drawing in a notebook with colored pencils, little houses with cows in the corrals, and round suns with yellow rays that hid behind the hills.</t1051>
<t1052>Only Rebeca was unhappy with Amaranta's threat.</t1052> <t1053>She knew her sister's character, the haughtiness of her spirit, and was frightened by the virulence of her rancor.</t1053> <t1054>She spent entire hours sucking her thumb in the bathroom, clinging to an exhausting effort of will to not eat dirt.</t1054> <t1055>In search of relief from her anxiety, she called Pilar Ternera to read her future.</t1055> <t1056>After a string of conventional vagueness, Pilar Ternera predicted:</t1056>
<t1057>—You will not be happy as long as your parents remain unburied.</t1057>
<t1058>Rebeca shuddered.</t1058> <t1059>As in the memory of a dream, she saw herself entering the house, very young, with the trunk and the little wooden rocking chair and a sack whose contents she never knew.</t1059> <t1060>She remembered a bald gentleman, dressed in linen and with the collar of his shirt fastened with a gold button, who had nothing to do with the king of cups.</t1060> <t1061>She remembered a very young and very beautiful woman, with warm and perfumed hands, that had nothing in common with the rheumatic hands of the queen of diamonds, and who would put flowers in her hair to take her for a walk in the afternoon through a town with green streets.</t1061>
<t1062>—I don't understand —he said.</t1062>
<t1063>Pilar Ternera seemed bewildered:</t1063>
<t1064>—Me neither, but that's what the cards say.</t1064>
<t1065>Rebeca was so worried about the enigma that she told José Arcadio Buendía, and he scolded her for giving credit to card predictions, but he silently set about searching cupboards and trunks, moving furniture, and turning over beds and floorboards, looking for the bag of bones.</t1065> <t1066>He remembered not having seen it since the times of the reconstruction.</t1066> <t1067>He secretly called the masons, and one of them revealed that he had walled up the bag in some bedroom because it was in his way while working.</t1067> <t1068>After several days of auscultations, with their ears pressed against the walls, they perceived the deep cloc cloc.</t1068> <t1069>They drilled into the wall, and there were the bones in the intact bag.</t1069> <t1070>That same day they buried it in an unmarked grave, improvised next to Melquíades', and José Arcadio Buendía returned to the house freed from a burden that for a moment weighed as heavily on his conscience as the memory of Prudencio Aguilar.</t1070> <t1071>As he passed through the kitchen, he gave Rebeca a kiss on the forehead.</t1071>
<t1072>—Get those bad ideas out of your head —he told her—.</t1072> <t1073>You will be happy.</t1073>
<t1074>Rebeca's friendship opened the doors of the house to Pilar Ternera, which had been closed by Úrsula since Arcadio's birth.</t1074> <t1075>She would arrive at any hour of the day, like a herd of goats, and would unleash her feverish energy on the heaviest chores.</t1075> <t1076>Sometimes she would enter the workshop and help Arcadio sensitize the daguerreotype plates with an efficiency and tenderness that eventually confused him.</t1076> <t1077>That woman bewildered him.</t1077> <t1078>The glow of her skin, her smoky scent, the disorder of her laughter in the darkroom disturbed his focus and made him stumble over things.</t1078>
<t1079>On one occasion Aureliano was there, working on goldsmithing, and Pilar Ternera leaned on the table to admire his patient industriousness.</t1079> <t1080>Suddenly it happened.</t1080> <t1081>Aureliano confirmed that Arcadio was in the darkroom before looking up and meeting Pilar Ternera's eyes, whose thoughts were perfectly visible, as if exposed to the midday light.</t1081>
<t1082>—Alright —said Aureliano—.</t1082> <t1083>Tell me what it is.</t1083>
<t1084>Pilar Ternera bit her lips with a sad smile.</t1084>
<t1085>—That you are good for war —he said—.</t1085> <t1086>Where you set your eye, you set the lead.</t1086>
<t1087>Aureliano rested with the confirmation of the omen.</t1087> <t1088>He went back to concentrating on his work, as if nothing had happened, and his voice took on a calm firmness.</t1088>
<t1089>—I admit it —he said—.</t1089> <t1090>It will bear my name.</t1090>
<t1091>José Arcadio Buendía finally achieved what he was seeking: he connected a wind-up dancer to the clock mechanism, and the toy danced nonstop to its own music for three days.</t1091> <t1092>That
