discovery excited him much more than any of his wild enterprises.</t1092> <t1093>He stopped eating.</t1093> <t1094>He stopped sleeping.</t1094> <t1095>Without Úrsula's vigilance and care, he let himself be dragged by his imagination into a state of perpetual delirium from which he would never recover.</t1095> <t1096>He spent the nights pacing the room, thinking out loud, looking for ways to apply the principles of the pendulum to ox carts, plowshares, to everything that could be useful when set in motion.</t1096> <t1097>The fever of insomnia wore him out so much that one early morning he couldn't recognize the old man with a white head and uncertain gestures who entered his bedroom.</t1097> <t1098>It was Prudencio Aguilar.</t1098> <t1099>When he finally identified him, amazed that even the dead aged, José Arcadio Buendía was shaken by nostalgia.</t1099> <t1100>“Prudencio,” he exclaimed, “how did you end up so far away!”</t1100> <t1101>After many years of death, the longing for the living was so intense, the need for company so urgent, the proximity of the other death that existed within death so terrifying, that Prudencio Aguilar had ended up loving his worst enemy.</t1101> <t1102>He had been looking for him for a long time.</t1102> <t1103>He asked the dead from Riohacha about him, the dead who arrived from the Valley of Upar, those who came from the swamp, and no one gave him an answer, because Macondo was an unknown town to the dead until Melquíades arrived and marked it with a black dot on the colorful maps of death.</t1103> <t1104>José Arcadio Buendía talked with Prudencio Aguilar until dawn.</t1104> <t1105>A few hours later, exhausted from the vigil, he entered Aureliano's workshop and asked him, “What day is today?”</t1105> <t1106>Aureliano replied that it was Tuesday.</t1106> <t1107>“That's what I thought,” said José Arcadio Buendía.</t1107> <t1108>“But suddenly I realized that it is still Monday like yesterday.</t1108> <t1109>Look at the sky, look at the walls, look at the begonias.</t1109> <t1110>Today is also Monday.”</t1110> <t1111>Used to his quirks, Aureliano paid no attention to him.</t1111> <t1112>The next day, Wednesday, José Arcadio Buendía returned to the workshop.</t1112> <t1113>“This is a disaster,” he said.</t1113> <t1114>“Look at the air, hear the hum of the sun, just like yesterday and the day before.</t1114> <t1115>Today is also Monday.”</t1115> <t1116>That night, Pietro Crespi found him in the corridor, crying with the graceless sob of the old, crying for Prudencio Aguilar, for Melquíades, for Rebeca's parents, for his father and mother, for all those he could remember who were then alone in death.</t1116> <t1117>He gave him a wind-up bear that walked on two legs on a wire, but he couldn't distract him from his obsession.</t1117> <t1118>He asked him what had happened to the project he had explained to him days before, about the possibility of building a pendulum machine that would allow man to fly, and he replied that it was impossible because the pendulum could lift anything into the air but could not lift itself.</t1118> <t1119>On Thursday he reappeared in the workshop with a painful look of scorched earth.</t1119> <t1120>“The time machine has broken down,” he almost sobbed, “and Úrsula and Amaranta are so far away!”</t1120> <t1121>Aureliano scolded him like a child, and he adopted a submissive air.</t1121> <t1122>He spent six hours examining things, trying to find a difference from the way they looked the day before, eager to discover in them some change that would reveal the passage of time.</t1122> <t1123>He spent the whole night in bed with his eyes open, calling out to Prudencio Aguilar, to Melquíades, to all the dead, to come and share his unease.</t1123> <t1124>But no one came.</t1124> <t1125>On Friday, before anyone got up, he went back to watch the appearance of nature until he had no doubt that it was still Monday.</t1125> <t1126>Then he grabbed a door bar and with the wild violence of his immense strength, he smashed the alchemy apparatus, the daguerreotype cabinet, the goldsmith's workshop into dust, shouting like a madman in a high-sounding and fluent but completely incomprehensible language.</t1126> <t1127>He was about to finish off the rest of the house when Aureliano asked the neighbors for help.</t1127> <t1128>It took ten men to knock him down, fourteen to tie him up, twenty to drag him to the chestnut tree in the yard, where they left him tied up, barking in a strange language and foaming green at the mouth.</t1128> <t1129>When Úrsula and Amaranta arrived, he was still tied hand and foot to the trunk of the chestnut tree, soaked by the rain and in a state of total innocence.</t1129> <t1130>They spoke to him, and he looked at them without recognizing them and said something incomprehensible.</t1130> <t1131>Úrsula untied his wrists and ankles, ulcerated by the pressure of the ropes, and left him tied only by the waist.</t1131> <t1132>Later they built him a palm shelter to protect him from the sun and rain.</t1132>
<t1133>Aureliano Buendía and Remedios Moscote were married on a Sunday in March before the altar that Father Nicanor Reyna had built in the visitors' room.</t1133><t1134>It was the culmination of four weeks of upheavals in the Moscote household, as little Remedios reached puberty before outgrowing the habits of childhood.</t1134><t1135>Despite her mother's lessons about the changes of adolescence, one February afternoon she burst into the room where her sisters were talking with Aureliano, shouting in alarm, and showed them her underwear smeared with a chocolaty paste.</t1135><t1136>A month was set for the wedding.</t1136><t1137>There was barely enough time to teach her to wash herself, dress herself, and understand the basic matters of running a household.</t1137><t1138>They made her urinate on hot bricks to correct her bed-wetting habit.</t1138><t1139>It was hard to convince her of the inviolability of marital secrets, because Remedios was so bewildered and at the same time so amazed by the revelation that she wanted to share the details of the wedding night with everyone.</t1139><t1140>It was an exhausting effort, but by the date set for the ceremony, the girl was as skilled in worldly matters as any of her sisters.</t1140><t1141>Don Apolinar Moscote walked her down the flower-adorned street, amidst the sound of fireworks and the music of several bands, and she waved and thanked with a smile those who wished her good luck from the windows.</t1141><t1142>Aureliano, dressed in black cloth, with the same patent leather boots with metal hooks that he would wear a few years later before the firing squad, was intensely pale and had a hard lump in his throat when he received his bride at the door of the house and led her to the altar.</t1142><t1143>She behaved with such naturalness and discretion that she did not lose her composure even when Aureliano dropped the ring while trying to put it on her.</t1143><t1144>Amidst the murmur and the beginning of confusion among the guests, she kept her arm raised with the lace mitten and remained with her ring finger ready, until her groom managed to stop the ring with his boot so it wouldn't roll to the door, and returned blushing to the altar.</t1144><t1145>Her mother and sisters were so anxious that the girl might make a mistake during the ceremony that in the end, they were the ones who committed the impropriety of lifting her up to give her a kiss.</t1145><t1146>From that day on, Remedios revealed the sense of responsibility, natural grace, and calm control that she would always have in the face of adverse circumstances.</t1146><t1147>It was she who, on her own initiative, set aside the best portion of the wedding cake and took it on a plate with a fork to José Arcadio Buendía.</t1147><t1148>Tied to the trunk of the chestnut tree, hunched on a wooden stool under the palm shelter, the enormous old man, discolored by the sun and rain, gave a vague smile of gratitude and ate the cake with his fingers, chewing an unintelligible psalm.</t1148><t1149>The only unhappy person at that boisterous celebration that lasted until dawn on Monday was Rebeca Buendía.</t1149><t1150>It was her frustrated party.</t1150><t1151>By agreement with Úrsula, her wedding was to be celebrated on the same date, but Pietro Crespi received a letter on Friday announcing the imminent death of his mother.</t1151><t1152>The wedding was postponed.</t1152><t1153>Pietro Crespi left for the provincial capital an hour after receiving the letter, and on the way, he crossed paths with his mother who arrived punctually on Saturday night and sang at Aureliano's wedding the sad aria she had prepared for her son's wedding.</t1153><t1154>Pietro Crespi returned at midnight on Sunday to sweep up the ashes of the party, after having exhausted five horses on the way trying to be on time for his wedding.</t1154><t1155>It was never discovered who wrote the letter.</t1155><t1156>Tormented by Úrsula, Amaranta cried in indignation and swore her innocence in front of the altar that the carpenters had not yet finished dismantling.</t1156>
<t1157>Father Nicanor Reyna —whom Don Apolinar Moscote had brought from the swamp to officiate the wedding— was an old man hardened by the ingratitude of his ministry.</t1157> <t1158>He had sad skin, almost just bones, a pronounced and round belly, and an expression of an old angel that was more of innocence than of kindness.</t1158> <t1159>He intended to return to his parish after the wedding, but he was appalled by the aridity of the inhabitants of Macondo, who thrived on scandal, adhered to natural law, without baptizing their children or sanctifying the holidays.</t1159> <t1160>Thinking that no land needed the seed of God as much, he decided to stay one more week to Christianize the circumcised and the gentiles, legalize concubinaries, and sacrament the dying.</t1160> <t1161>But no one paid him any attention.</t1161> <t1162>They replied that for many years they had been without a priest, dealing with the matters of the soul directly with God, and had lost the malice of mortal sin.</t1162> <t1163>Tired of preaching in the desert, Father Nicanor set out to build a temple, the largest in the world, with life-sized saints and colored glass in the walls, so that people would come from Rome to honor God in the center of impiety.</t1163> <t1164>He went everywhere begging for alms with a copper dish.</t1164> <t1165>They gave him a lot, but he wanted more, because the temple had to have a bell whose toll would bring the drowned to the surface.</t1165> <t1166>He pleaded so much that he lost his voice.</t1166> <t1167>His bones began to fill with noises.</t1167> <t1168>One Saturday, not having collected even the value of the doors, he let himself be overcome by despair.</t1168> <t1169>He improvised an altar in the square and on Sunday went around the town with a small bell, as in the times of insomnia, calling for an open-air mass.</t1169> <t1170>Many went out of curiosity.</t1170> <t1171>Others out of nostalgia.</t1171> <t1172>Others so that God would not take their disregard for his intermediary as a personal offense.</t1172> <t1173>So at eight in the morning, half the town was in the square, where Father Nicanor sang the gospels with a voice lacerated by supplication.</t1173> <t1174>At the end, when the attendees began to disperse, he raised his arms as a sign of attention.</t1174>
<t1175>—One moment —he said—.</t1175> <t1176>Now we are going to witness irrefutable proof of the infinite power of God.</t1176>
<t1177>The boy who had assisted at mass brought him a cup of thick, steaming chocolate which he drank without pausing for breath.</t1177> <t1178>Then he wiped his lips with a handkerchief he took from his sleeve, stretched out his arms, and closed his eyes.</t1178> <t1179>Then Father Nicanor rose twelve centimeters above the ground.</t1179> <t1180>It was a convincing demonstration.</t1180> <t1181>He spent several days going from house to house, repeating the levitation test through the stimulus of chocolate, while the altar boy collected so much money in a sack that in less than a month he began the construction of the temple.</t1181> <t1182>No one doubted the divine origin of the demonstration, except for José Arcadio Buendía, who observed without flinching the crowd that one morning gathered around the chestnut tree to witness the revelation once more.</t1182> <t1183>He barely stretched a little on the bench and shrugged when Father Nicanor began to rise from the ground along with the chair he was sitting on.</t1183>
<t1184>—This is the simplest thing —said José Arcadio Buendía—: this man has discovered the fourth state of matter.</t1184>
<t1185>Father Nicanor raised his hand and the four legs of the chair touched the ground at the same time.</t1185>
<t1186>—I deny it —he said—.</t1186> <t1187>This fact undoubtedly proves the existence of God.</t1187>
<t1188>That was how it was discovered that the devilish jargon of José Arcadio Buendía was Latin.</t1188> <t1189>Father Nicanor took advantage of being the only person who had been able to communicate with him to try to instill faith in his disturbed mind.</t1189> <t1190>Every afternoon he would sit next to the chestnut tree, preaching in Latin, but José Arcadio Buendía stubbornly refused to accept rhetorical twists or chocolate transmutations, and demanded as the only proof the daguerreotype of God.</t1190> <t1191>Father Nicanor then brought him medals and holy cards and even a reproduction of Veronica's veil, but José Arcadio Buendía rejected them as artisanal objects without scientific basis.</t1191> <t1192>He was so stubborn that Father Nicanor gave up his evangelization efforts and continued visiting him out of humanitarian feelings.</t1192> <t1193>But then it was José Arcadio Buendía who took the initiative and tried to break the priest's faith with rationalist tricks.</t1193> <t1194>On one occasion, when Father Nicanor brought a board and a box of pieces to the chestnut tree to invite him to play checkers, José Arcadio Buendía did not accept, saying that he could never understand the point of a contest between two opponents who agreed on the principles.</t1194> <t1195>Father Nicanor, who had never seen the game of checkers in that way, could not play it again.</t1195> <t1196>Increasingly astonished by José Arcadio Buendía's lucidity, he asked him how it was possible that they had him tied to a tree.</t1196>
<t1197>—That is the simplest —he replied—: because I am crazy.</t1197>
<t1198>Since then, worried about his own faith, the priest did not visit him again, and devoted himself entirely to hastening the construction of the temple.</t1198> <t1199>Rebeca felt hope revive.</t1199> <t1200>Her future was conditioned on the completion of the work, ever since a Sunday when Father Nicanor was having lunch at the house and the whole family, seated at the table, spoke of the solemnity and splendor that the religious ceremonies would have once the temple was built.</t1200> <t1201>“The most fortunate will be Rebeca,” said Amaranta.</t1201> <t1202>And since Rebeca did not understand what she meant, she explained it with an innocent smile:</t1202>
<t1203>—You're going to have to inaugurate the church with your wedding.</t1203>
<t1204>Rebeca tried to anticipate any comment.</t1204> <t1205>At the pace the construction was going, the temple would not be finished for another ten years.</t1205> <t1206>Father Nicanor did not agree: the growing generosity of the faithful allowed for more optimistic calculations.</t1206> <t1207>In the face of Rebeca's silent indignation, who could not finish her lunch, Úrsula celebrated Amaranta's idea and contributed a considerable amount to hasten the work.</t1207> <t1208>Father Nicanor considered that with another aid like that, the temple would be ready in three years.</t1208> <t1209>From then on, Rebeca did not speak to Amaranta again, convinced that her initiative had not had the innocence she had managed to appear.</t1209> <t1210>"It was the least serious thing I could do," Amaranta replied in the virulent argument they had that night.</t1210> <t1211>"This way I won't have to kill you in the next three years."</t1211> <t1212>Rebeca accepted the challenge.</t1212>
<t1213>When Pietro Crespi learned of the new postponement, he suffered a crisis of disappointment, but Rebeca gave him a definitive proof of loyalty.</t1213><t1214>"We will elope whenever you decide," she said to him.</t1214><t1215>However, Pietro Crespi was not a man of adventures.</t1215><t1216>He lacked the impulsive character of his fiancée, and considered respect for one's word as a capital that could not be squandered.</t1216><t1217>Then Rebeca resorted to bolder methods.</t1217><t1218>A mysterious wind would extinguish the lamps in the visiting room, and Úrsula would catch the couple youth.</t1222><t1223>"Poor mom," Rebeca would say with mocking indignation, seeing Úrsula yawn in the drowsiness of the visits.</t1223><t1224>"When she dies, she'll haunt that rocking the temple.</t1225><t1226>Amaranta did not grow impatient.</t1226><t1227>While conversing with the friends who came every afternoon to embroider or knit in the corridor, she tried to conceive new that she did not dare to blame Amaranta.</t1232><t1233>There was less than a month left for the wedding, but Amparo Moscote committed to sewing a new dress in a week.</t1233><t1234>Amaranta felt faint channel of her spine.</t1235><t1236>For long months, she had trembled with fear awaiting that hour, because if she did not conceive the definitive obstacle to Rebeca's wedding, she was sure that at the last moment, when all the resources of her imagination had failed, she would have the courage to poison her.</t1236><t1237>That afternoon, while Rebeca suffocated from the heat inside the satin armor that Amparo Moscote was fitting to her body with a thousand pins and infinite patience, Amaranta made several mistakes in her crochet stitches and pricked her finger with the needle, but she decided with dreadful coldness that the date would be the last Friday before the wedding, and the method would be a dose of laudanum in the coffee.</t1237>
<t1238>A major obstacle, as insurmountable as it was unforeseen, forced a new and indefinite postponement.</t1238><t1239>A week before the scheduled wedding date, little Remedios woke up at midnight drenched in a hot broth that exploded in her insides with a kind of tearing burp, and died three days later poisoned by her own blood with a pair of twins lodged in her belly.</t1239><t1240>Amaranta suffered a crisis of conscience.</t1240><t1241>She had prayed to God with such fervor for something dreadful to happen so she wouldn't have to poison Rebeca, that she felt guilty for Remedios' death.</t1241><t1242>That was not the obstacle she had prayed so much for.</t1242><t1243>Remedios had brought a breath of joy to the house.</t1243><t1244>She had settled with her husband in a room near the workshop, which she decorated with the dolls and toys of her recent childhood, and her cheerful vitality overflowed the four walls of the room and swept like a gust of good health through the begonia corridor.</t1244><t1245>She sang from dawn.</t1245><t1246>She was the only person who dared to mediate in the disputes between Rebeca and Amaranta.</t1246><t1247>She took on the costly task of attending to José Arcadio Buendía.</t1247><t1248>She brought him food, assisted him in his daily needs, washed him with soap and scrub, kept his hair and beard free of lice and nits, maintained the palm shed in good condition, and reinforced it with waterproof tarps during stormy times.</t1248><t1249>In her last months, she had managed to communicate with him in rudimentary Latin phrases.</t1249><t1250>When the son of Aureliano and Pilar Ternera was born and brought to the house and baptized in an intimate ceremony with the name Aureliano José, Remedios decided he should be considered her eldest son.</t1250><t1251>Her maternal instinct surprised Úrsula.</t1251><t1252>Aureliano, for his part, found in her the justification he needed to live.</t1252><t1253>He worked all day in the workshop and Remedios brought him a bowl of unsweetened coffee mid-morning.</t1253><t1254>They both visited the Moscotes every night.</t1254><t1255>Aureliano played endless games of dominoes with his father-in-law, while Remedios conversed with her sisters or dealt with adult matters with her mother.</t1255><t1256>The bond with the Buendía family consolidated the authority of don Apolinar Moscote in the town.</t1256><t1257>In frequent trips to the provincial capital, he managed to get the government to build a school for Arcadio to run, who had inherited his grandfather's educational enthusiasm.</t1257><t1258>He persuaded most of the houses to be painted blue for the national independence celebration.</t1258><t1259>At the urging of Father Nicanor, he arranged for Catarino's shop to be moved to a remote street and closed several scandalous places that thrived in the town center.</t1259><t1260>Once he returned with six policemen armed with rifles to maintain order, without anyone remembering the original commitment not to have armed people in the town.</t1260><t1261>Aureliano was pleased with his father-in-law's efficiency.</t1261><t1262>"You're going to get as fat as he is," his friends told him.</t1262><t1263>But the sedentary lifestyle, which accentuated his cheekbones and concentrated the glow in his eyes, did not increase his weight nor alter the calmness of his character, and on the contrary, it hardened the straight line of solitary meditation and implacable decision on his lips.</t1263><t1264>So deep was the affection that he and his wife had managed to awaken in both families, that when Remedios announced she was going to have a child, even Rebeca and Amaranta made a truce to knit in blue wool, in case it was a boy, and in pink wool, in case it was a girl.</t1264><t1265>She was the last person Arcadio thought of, a few years later, in front of the firing squad.</t1265>
<t1266>Úrsula arranged a mourning period with doors and windows closed, no entry or exit for anyone except for indispensable matters; she forbade speaking out loud for a year, and placed the daguerreotype of Remedios in the spot where the body was laid out, with a black ribbon across it and an oil lamp lit forever.</t1266> <t1267>Future generations, who never let the lamp go out, would be puzzled by that girl with ruffled skirts, white boots, and an organdy bow in her hair, whom they could not reconcile with the academic image of a great-grandmother.</t1267> <t1268>Amaranta took charge of Aureliano José.</t1268> <t1269>She adopted him as a son who would share her solitude and relieve her of the involuntary laudanum that her frantic supplications had poured into Remedios' coffee.</t1269> <t1270>Pietro Crespi would tiptoe in at dusk, with a black ribbon on his hat, and pay a silent visit to a Rebeca who seemed to be bleeding out inside the black dress with sleeves down to her wrists.</t1270> <t1271>The mere idea of thinking about a new date for the wedding would have been so irreverent that the engagement turned into an eternal relationship, a love of exhaustion that no one cared for anymore, as if the lovers who once disassembled lamps to kiss had been abandoned to the whim of death.</t1271> <t1272>Lost and completely demoralized, Rebeca went back to eating earth.</t1272>
<t1273>Suddenly —when the mourning had lasted so long that the cross-stitch sessions had already resumed— someone pushed the street door at two in the afternoon, in the deadly silence of the heat, and the beams trembled with such force in the foundations that Amaranta and her friends embroidering in the corridor, Rebeca sucking her thumb in the bedroom, Úrsula in the kitchen, Aureliano in the workshop, and even José Arcadio Buendía under the solitary chestnut tree, had the impression that an earthquake was unsettling the house.</t1273><t1274>A colossal man was arriving.</t1274><t1275>His square shoulders barely fit through the doors.</t1275><t1276>He had a small medallion of the Virgin of Remedies hanging on his bison neck, his arms and chest completely embroidered with cryptic tattoos, and on his right wrist the tight copper bracelet of the children-on-the-cross.</t1276><t1277>His skin was tanned by the salt of the outdoors, his hair short and cropped like a mule's mane, his jaws ironclad, and his gaze sad.</t1277><t1278>He had a belt twice as thick as a horse's girth, boots with gaiters and spurs, and with ironed heels, and his presence gave the tremulous impression of a seismic shake.</t1278><t1279>He crossed the living room and the sitting room, carrying in his hand some half-destroyed saddlebags, and appeared like thunder in the corridor of begonias, where Amaranta and her friends were paralyzed with their needles in the air.</t1279><t1280>“Hello,” he said to them with a tired voice, and threw the saddlebags on the work table and passed by towards the back of the house.</t1280><t1281>“Hello,” he said to the frightened Rebeca who saw him pass by her bedroom door.</t1281><t1282>“Hello,” he said to Aureliano, who was with all five senses alert at the goldsmith's bench.</t1282><t1283>He did not linger with anyone.</t1283><t1284>He went directly to the kitchen, and there he stopped for the first time at the end of a journey that had started on the other side of the world.</t1284><t1285>“Hello,” he said.</t1285><t1286>Úrsula stayed with her mouth open for a fraction of a second, looked him in the eyes, screamed and jumped to his neck, shouting and crying with joy.</t1286><t1287>It was José Arcadio.</t1287><t1288>He returned as poor as he had left, to the extent that Úrsula had to give him two pesos to pay for the horse's rental.</t1288><t1289>He spoke Spanish mixed with sailor's jargon.</t1289><t1290>They asked him where he had been, and he answered: “Around.”</t1290><t1291>He hung the hammock in the room they assigned to him and slept for three days.</t1291><t1292>When he woke up, and after drinking sixteen raw eggs, he went straight to Catarino's store, where his monumental bulk provoked a panic of curiosity among the women.</t1292><t1293>He ordered music and liquor for everyone at his expense.</t1293><t1294>He arm-wrestled five men at the same time.</t1294><t1295>“It's impossible,” they said, convinced that they couldn't move his arm.</t1295><t1296>“He has children-on-the-cross.”</t1296><t1297>Catarino, who didn't believe in feats of strength, bet twelve pesos that he couldn't move the counter.</t1297><t1298>José Arcadio ripped it from its place, lifted it over his head, and put it out on the street.</t1298><t1299>It took eleven men to bring it back in.</t1299><t1300>In the heat of the party, he displayed on the counter his unbelievable masculinity, entirely tattooed with a blue and red tangle of inscriptions in various languages.</t1300><t1301>To the women who besieged him with their greed, he asked who paid more.</t1301><t1302>The one who had the most offered twenty pesos.</t1302><t1303>Then he proposed to raffle himself among all at ten pesos a number.</t1303><t1304>It was an exorbitant price, because the most sought-after woman earned eight pesos in a night, but they all accepted.</t1304><t1305>They wrote their names on fourteen slips of paper that they put in a hat, and each woman drew one.</t1305><t1306>When only two slips were left to draw, it was established to whom they belonged.</t1306>
<t1307>—Five more pesos each —proposed José Arcadio— and I'll split myself between both of you.</t1307>
<t1308>That was how he made a living.</t1308><t1309>He had circled the globe sixty-five times, enlisted in a crew of stateless sailors.</t1309><t1310>The women who slept with him that night in Catarino's tent took him naked to the dance hall so that everyone could see that he didn't have a millimeter of his body without a tattoo, front and back, from his neck to his toes.</t1310><t1311>He couldn't integrate into the family.</t1311><t1312>He slept all day and spent the night in the red-light district performing feats of strength.</t1312><t1313>On the rare occasions when Úrsula managed to get him to sit at the table, he showed a radiant charm, especially when recounting his adventures in distant lands.</t1313><t1314>He had shipwrecked and spent two weeks adrift in the Sea of Japan, feeding on the body of a companion who succumbed to sunstroke, whose salted and re-salted flesh, cooked in the sun, had a grainy and sweet taste.</t1314><t1315>On a radiant midday in the Bay of Bengal, his ship had defeated a sea dragon in whose belly they found the helmet, buckles, and weapons of a crusader.</t1315><t1316>He had seen in the Caribbean the ghost of Victor Hugues' corsair ship, with sails torn by the winds of death, the rigging gnawed by sea cockroaches, and forever lost on the course to Guadeloupe.</t1316><t1317>Úrsula cried at the table as if she were reading the letters that never arrived, in which José Arcadio recounted his exploits and misadventures.</t1317><t1318>“And so much house here, my son,” she sobbed.</t1318><t1319>“And so much food thrown to the pigs!”</t1319><t1320>But deep down, she couldn't conceive that the boy taken by the gypsies was the same brute who ate half a piglet at lunch and whose flatulence withered the flowers.</t1320><t1321>Something similar happened to the rest of the family.</t1321><t1322>Amaranta couldn't hide the disgust his bestial belches caused her at the table.</t1322><t1323>Arcadio, who never knew the secret of his parentage, barely answered the questions he asked with the evident purpose of winning his affections.</t1323><t1324>Aureliano tried to revive the times when they slept in the same room, tried to restore the complicity of childhood, but José Arcadio had forgotten them because the life at sea had saturated his memory with too many things to remember.</t1324><t1325>Only Rebeca succumbed to the first impact.</t1325><t1326>The afternoon she saw him pass in front of her bedroom, she thought Pietro Crespi was a dandy weakling compared to that prime male whose volcanic breath was felt throughout the house.</t1326><t1327>She sought his proximity with any excuse.</t1327><t1328>On one occasion, José Arcadio looked at her body with blatant attention and said, “You are very much a woman, little sister.”</t1328><t1329>Rebeca lost control of herself.</t1329><t1330>She went back to eating earth and lime from the walls with the avidity of old days, and sucked her thumb with such anxiety that a callus formed on it.</t1330><t1331>She vomited a green liquid with dead leeches.</t1331><t1332>She spent sleepless nights shivering with fever, fighting delirium, waiting until the house trembled with José Arcadio's return at dawn.</t1332><t1333>One afternoon, when everyone was napping, she couldn't resist any longer and went to his bedroom.</t1333><t1334>She found him in his underwear, awake, lying in the hammock he had hung from the beams with ship mooring cables.</t1334><t1335>His enormous tattooed nakedness impressed her so much that she felt the urge to retreat.</t1335><t1336>“Excuse me,” she apologized.</t1336><t1337>“I didn't know you were here.”</t1337><t1338>But she lowered her voice so as not to wake anyone.</t1338><t1339>“Come here,” he said.</t1339><t1340>Rebeca obeyed.</t1340><t1341>She stopped next to the hammock, sweating ice, feeling knots form in her stomach, while José Arcadio caressed her ankles with his fingertips, then her calves, and then her thighs, murmuring, “Oh, little sister; oh, little sister.”</t1341><t1342>She had to make a supernatural effort not to die when an astonishingly regulated cyclonic force lifted her by the waist, stripped her of her intimacy with three claw swipes, and dismembered her like a little bird.</t1342><t1343>She managed to thank God for being born before losing consciousness in the inconceivable pleasure of that unbearable pain, splashing in the steaming swamp of the hammock that absorbed the explosion of her blood like blotting paper.</t1343>
<t1344>Three days later they got married at the five o'clock mass.</t1344> <t1345>José Arcadio had gone to Pietro Crespi's shop the day before.</t1345> <t1346>He had found him giving a zither lesson and did not take him aside to talk to him.</t1346> <t1347>"I'm marrying Rebeca," he said to him.</t1347> <t1348>Pietro Crespi turned pale, handed the zither to one of his students, and ended the class.</t1348> <t1349>When they were alone in the room crowded with musical instruments and clockwork toys, Pietro Crespi said:</t1349>
<t1350>—It's his sister.</t1350>
<t1351>—I don't care —replied José Arcadio.</t1351>
<t1352>Pietro Crespi wiped his forehead with a lavender-scented handkerchief.</t1352>
<t1353>—It is against nature —he explained— and, moreover, the law prohibits it.</t1353>
<t1354>José Arcadio grew impatient not so much with the argument as with Pietro Crespi's paleness.</t1354>
<t1355>—I shit twice on nature —he said—.</t1355> <t1356>And I'm telling you this so you don't bother going to ask Rebeca anything.</t1356>
<t1357>But his brutal behavior was shattered upon seeing Pietro Crespi's eyes fill with tears.</t1357>
<t1358>—Now —he said to him in a different tone—, if what you like is family, then there's Amaranta for you.</t1358>
<t1359>Father Nicanor revealed in the Sunday sermon that José Arcadio and Rebeca were not siblings.</t1359> <t1360>Úrsula never forgave what she considered an inconceivable lack of respect, and when they returned from church, she forbade the newlyweds from ever setting foot in the house again.</t1360> <t1361>To her, it was as if they had died.</t1361> <t1362>So they rented a small house in front of the cemetery and settled in it with no more furniture than José Arcadio's hammock.</t1362> <t1363>On their wedding night, Rebeca was bitten on the foot by a scorpion that had crawled into her slipper.</t1363> <t1364>Her tongue went numb, but that did not prevent them from having a scandalous honeymoon.</t1364> <t1365>The neighbors were frightened by the screams that woke up the entire neighborhood up to eight times in one night, and up to three times during the siesta, and they prayed that such an unbridled passion would not disturb the peace of the dead.</t1365>
<t1366>Aureliano was the only one who cared about them.</t1366> <t1367>He bought them some furniture and provided them with money until José Arcadio regained his sense of reality and began to work the no-man's land that bordered the house's yard.</t1367> <t1368>Amaranta, on the other hand, never managed to overcome her resentment towards Rebeca, although life offered her a satisfaction she had never dreamed of: on Úrsula's initiative, who did not know how to repair the shame, Pietro Crespi continued to have lunch at the house on Tuesdays, overcoming the failure with serene dignity.</t1368> <t1369>He kept the black ribbon on his hat as a sign of appreciation for the family, and he delighted in showing his affection to Úrsula by bringing her exotic gifts: Portuguese sardines, Turkish rose jam, and on one occasion, a beautiful Manila shawl.</t1369> <t1370>Amaranta attended to him with affectionate diligence.</t1370> <t1371>She guessed his tastes, removed the loose threads from the cuffs of his shirt, and embroidered a dozen handkerchiefs with his initials for his birthday.</t1371> <t1372>On Tuesdays, after lunch, while she embroidered in the corridor, he kept her cheerful company.</t1372> <t1373>For Pietro Crespi, that woman whom he always considered and treated as a child, was a revelation.</t1373> <t1374>Although her type lacked grace, she had a rare sensitivity to appreciate the things of the world, and a secret tenderness.</t1374> <t1375>One Tuesday, when no one doubted that sooner or later it had to happen, Pietro Crespi asked her to marry him.</t1375> <t1376>She did not interrupt her work.</t1376> <t1377>She waited for the hot blush of her ears to pass and gave her voice a serene emphasis of maturity.</t1377>
<t1378>—Of course, Crespi —he said—, but when one knows oneself better.</t1378> <t1379>It is never good to rush things.</t1379>
<t1380>Úrsula became flustered.</t1380> <t1381>Despite the affection she had for Pietro Crespi, she couldn't determine whether his decision was good or bad from a moral standpoint, after the prolonged and noisy courtship with Rebeca.</t1381> <t1382>But she ended up accepting it as an unqualified fact, because no one shared her doubts.</t1382> <t1383>Aureliano, who was the man of the house, confused her even more with his enigmatic and definitive opinion:</t1383>
<t1384>—These are not the hours to be thinking about marriages.</t1384>
<t1385>That opinion, which Úrsula only understood some months later, was the only sincere one that Aureliano could express at that moment, not only regarding marriage but any matter that wasn't war.</t1385><t1386>He himself, in front of the firing squad, did not quite understand how the series of subtle but irrevocable coincidences that led him to that point had unfolded.</t1386><t1387>Remedios' death did not produce the shock he feared.</t1387><t1388>It was rather a dull feeling of rage that gradually dissolved into a solitary and passive frustration, similar to what he experienced in the times when he was resigned to living without a woman.</t1388><t1389>He plunged back into work, but kept the habit of playing dominoes with his father-in-law.</t1389><t1390>In a house silenced by mourning, the nightly conversations consolidated the friendship between the two men.</t1390><t1391>"Remarry, Aurelito," his father-in-law would say.</t1391><t1392>"I have six daughters to choose from."</t1392><t1393>On one occasion, on the eve of the elections, Don Apolinar Moscote returned from one of his frequent trips, worried about the country's political situation.</t1393><t1394>The liberals were determined to go to war.</t1394><t1395>Since Aureliano had very confused notions at that time about the differences between conservatives and liberals, his father-in-law gave him schematic lessons.</t1395><t1396>The liberals, he said, were masons; people of bad character, supporters of hanging priests, implementing civil marriage and divorce, recognizing equal rights for natural children as for legitimate ones, and tearing the country apart into a federal system that stripped the supreme authority of its powers.</t1396><t1397>The conservatives, on the other hand, who had received power directly from God, advocated for the stability of public order and family morals; they were the defenders of the faith of Christ, the principle of authority, and were not willing to allow the country to be dismembered into autonomous entities.</t1397><t1398>Out of humanitarian sentiments, Aureliano sympathized with the liberal stance regarding the rights of natural children, but still did not understand how it came to the point of waging a war over things that could not be touched with hands.</t1398><t1399>It seemed an exaggeration to him that his father-in-law had six soldiers armed with rifles, led by a sergeant, sent for the elections in a town without political passions.</t1399><t1400>Not only did they arrive, but they went from house to house confiscating hunting weapons, machetes, and even kitchen knives, before distributing the blue ballots with the names of the conservative candidates and the red ballots with the names of the liberal candidates among men over twenty-one years old.</t1400><t1401>On the eve of the elections, Don Apolinar Moscote himself read an edict that prohibited, from midnight on Saturday and for forty-eight hours, the sale of alcoholic beverages and the gathering of more than three people who were not from the same family.</t1401><t1402>The elections passed without incident.</t1402><t1403>From eight o'clock on Sunday morning, the wooden ballot box was set up in the square, guarded by the six soldiers.</t1403><t1404>Voting took place with complete freedom, as Aureliano himself could verify, who spent almost the entire day with his father-in-law ensuring that no one voted more than once.</t1404><t1405>At four in the afternoon, a drum roll in the square announced the end of the day, and Don Apolinar Moscote sealed the ballot box with a label crossed with his signature.</t1405><t1406>That night, while playing dominoes with Aureliano, he ordered the sergeant to break the label to count the votes.</t1406><t1407>There were almost as many red ballots as blue ones, but the sergeant left only ten red ones and made up the difference with blue ones.</t1407><t1408>Then they resealed the ballot box with a new label and the next day, first thing in the morning, they took it to the provincial capital.</t1408><t1409>"The liberals will go to war," said Aureliano.</t1409><t1410>Don Apolinar did not take his eyes off his domino pieces.</t1410><t1411>"If you say it because of the ballot changes, they won't," he said.</t1411><t1412>"Some red ones are left so there are no complaints."</t1412><t1413>Aureliano understood the disadvantages of the opposition.</t1413><t1414>"If I were a liberal," he said, "I would go to war over this ballot issue."</t1414><t1415>His father-in-law looked at him over the rim of his glasses.</t1415>
<t1416>—Oh, Aurelito —he said—, if you were a liberal, even if you were my son-in-law, you wouldn't have seen the ballot change.</t1416>
<t1417>What actually caused indignation among the people was not the election results, but the fact that the soldiers had not returned the weapons.</t1417> <t1418>A group of women spoke with Aureliano to get his father-in-law to return the kitchen knives.</t1418> <t1419>Don Apolinar Moscote explained to him, in strict confidence, that the soldiers had taken the confiscated weapons as proof that the liberals were preparing for war.</t1419> <t1420>The cynicism of the statement alarmed him.</t1420> <t1421>He made no comment, but one night when Gerineldo Márquez and Magnífico Visbal were talking with other friends about the knife incident, they asked him if he was a liberal or a conservative.</t1421> <t1422>Aureliano did not hesitate:</t1422>
<t1423>—If I had to be something, I would be a liberal —he said—, because conservatives are cheaters.</t1423>
<t1424>The next day, at the insistence of his friends, he went to visit Dr. Alirio Noguera to treat a supposed liver pain.</t1424> <t1425>He didn't even know what the point of the ruse was.</t1425> <t1426>Dr. Alirio Noguera had arrived in Macondo a few years earlier with a kit of tasteless globules and a medical motto that convinced no one: One nail drives out another.</t1426> <t1427>In reality, he was a fraud.</t1427> <t1428>Behind his innocent facade of an unprestigious doctor hid a terrorist who covered the scars on his ankles from five years in stocks with half-leg gaiters.</t1428> <t1429>Captured in the first federalist adventure, he managed to escape to Curaçao disguised in the outfit he most detested in this world: a cassock.</t1429> <t1430>After a prolonged exile, spurred on by the exalted news brought to Curaçao by exiles from all over the Caribbean, he boarded a smugglers' schooner and appeared in Riohacha with the vials of globules that were nothing more than refined sugar, and a diploma from the University of Leipzig forged by himself.</t1430> <t1431>He cried in disappointment.</t1431> <t1432>The federalist fervor, which the exiles described as a powder keg about to explode, had dissolved into a vague electoral illusion.</t1432> <t1433>Bitter from the failure, anxious for a safe place to await old age, the fake homeopath took refuge in Macondo.</t1433> <t1434>In the narrow room crammed with empty bottles that he rented beside the plaza, he lived for several years off hopeless patients who, after trying everything, consoled themselves with sugar globules.</t1434> <t1435>His agitator instincts remained dormant while Don Apolinar Moscote was a decorative authority.</t1435> <t1436>His time was spent remembering and fighting asthma.</t1436> <t1437>The proximity of the elections was the thread that allowed him to find the skein of subversion again.</t1437> <t1438>He established contact with the young people of the town, who lacked political training, and embarked on a stealthy campaign of instigation.</t1438> <t1439>The numerous red ballots that appeared in the ballot box, which Don Apolinar Moscote attributed to the novelty-seeking nature of youth, were part of his plan: he forced his disciples to vote to convince them that elections were a farce.</t1439> <t1440>"The only effective thing," he said, "is violence."</t1440> <t1441>Most of Aureliano's friends were enthusiastic about the idea of overthrowing the conservative order, but no one had dared to include him in the plans, not only because of his ties to the magistrate but also because of his solitary and evasive nature.</t1441> <t1442>It was also known that he had voted blue at his father-in-law's suggestion.</t1442> <t1443>So it was a simple coincidence that revealed his political feelings, and it was pure curiosity that got him involved in the whim of visiting the doctor to treat a pain he didn't have.</t1443> <t1444>In the cubbyhole smelling of camphorated cobwebs, he found a sort of dusty iguana whose lungs whistled as he breathed.</t1444> <t1445>Before asking him any questions, the doctor took him to the window and examined the inside of his lower eyelid.</t1445> <t1446>"It's not there," said Aureliano, as he had been instructed.</t1446> <t1447>He pressed his liver with his fingertips and added, "It's here where I have the pain that won't let me sleep."</t1447> <t1448>Then Dr. Noguera closed the window under the pretext that there was too much sun and explained in simple terms why it was a patriotic duty to kill the conservatives.</t1448> <t1449>For several days, Aureliano carried a small vial in his shirt pocket.</t1449> <t1450>He would take it out every two hours, put three globules in the palm of his hand, and throw them into his mouth to dissolve slowly on his tongue.</t1450> <t1451>Don Apolinar Moscote mocked his faith in homeopathy, but those in the plot recognized him as one of their own.</t1451> <t1452>Almost all the children of the founders were involved, although none knew concretely what the action they were plotting consisted of.</t1452> <t1453>However, the day the doctor revealed the secret to Aureliano, he backed out of the conspiracy.</t1453> <t1454>Although he was then convinced of the urgency of overthrowing the conservative regime, the plan horrified him.</t1454> <t1455>Dr. Noguera was a mystic of personal attack.</t1455> <t1456>His system was reduced to coordinating a series of individual actions that, in a masterstroke of national scope, would eliminate the regime's officials along with their respective families, especially the children, to exterminate conservatism at its root.</t1456> <t1457>Don Apolinar Moscote, his wife, and their six daughters were, of course, on the list.</t1457>
<t1458>—You are neither a liberal nor anything —Aureliano told him without getting upset—.</t1458> <t1459>You are nothing but a butcher.</t1459>
<t1460>—In that case —replied the doctor with equal calm— give me back the little bottle.</t1460> <t1461>You don't need it anymore.</t1461>
<t1462>Only six months later did Aureliano learn that the doctor had written him off as a man of action, deeming him a sentimentalist with no future, a passive character, and a defined solitary vocation.</t1462> <t1463>They tried to corner him, fearing he would denounce the conspiracy.</t1463> <t1464>Aureliano reassured them: he wouldn't say a word, but the night they went to kill the Moscote family, they would find him defending the door.</t1464> <t1465>He demonstrated such a convincing resolve that the plan was postponed indefinitely.</t1465> <t1466>It was around that time that Úrsula sought his opinion on the marriage of Pietro Crespi and Amaranta, and he replied that the times were not right for thinking about that.</t1466> <t1467>For a week, he had been carrying an archaic pistol under his shirt.</t1467> <t1468>He was watching his friends.</t1468> <t1469>In the afternoons, he would go for coffee with José Arcadio and Rebeca, who were starting to organize their house, and from seven o'clock, he played dominoes with his father-in-law.</t1469> <t1470>At lunchtime, he conversed with Arcadio, who was already a monumental adolescent, and found him increasingly excited about the imminence of war.</t1470> <t1471>At school, where Arcadio had students older than him mixed with children who were just beginning to speak, the liberal fever had taken hold.</t1471> <t1472>There was talk of executing Father Nicanor, converting the temple into a school, and implementing free love.</t1472> <t1473>Aureliano tried to temper his impulses.</t1473> <t1474>He recommended discretion and prudence.</t1474> <t1475>Deaf to his calm reasoning and sense of reality, Arcadio publicly reproached him for his weakness of character.</t1475> <t1476>Aureliano waited.</t1476> <t1477>Finally, at the beginning of December, Úrsula burst into the workshop, distraught.</t1477>
<t1478>—The war has broken out!</t1478>
<t1479>Indeed, it had broken out three months ago.</t1479> <t1480>Martial law prevailed throughout the country.</t1480> <t1481>The only one who knew in time was Don Apolinar Moscote, but he did not give the news even to his wife, while the army platoon that was to occupy the town by surprise was arriving.</t1481> <t1482>They entered silently before dawn, with two pieces of light artillery pulled by mules, and established the barracks in the school.</t1482> <t1483>A curfew was imposed at six in the evening.</t1483> <t1484>A more drastic search than the previous one was carried out, house by house, and this time they even took the farming tools.</t1484> <t1485>They dragged out Dr. Noguera, tied him to a tree in the square, and executed him without trial.</t1485> <t1486>Father Nicanor tried to impress the military authorities with the miracle of levitation, and a soldier smashed his head with a rifle butt.</t1486> <t1487>The liberal exaltation faded into a silent terror.</t1487> <t1488>Aureliano, pale and inscrutable, continued playing dominoes with his father-in-law.</t1488> <t1489>He understood that despite his current title of civil and military chief of the town, Don Apolinar Moscote was once again a decorative authority.</t1489> <t1490>The decisions were made by an army captain who every morning collected an extraordinary levy for the defense of public order.</t1490> <t1491>Four soldiers under his command took a woman who had been bitten by a rabid dog from her family and killed her with rifle butts in the middle of the street.</t1491> <t1492>One Sunday, two weeks after the occupation, Aureliano entered Gerineldo Márquez's house and with his usual calm asked for a bowl of coffee without sugar.</t1492> <t1493>When the two were alone in the kitchen, Aureliano gave his voice an authority that had never been known in him.</t1493> <t1494>“Prepare the boys,” he said.</t1494> <t1495>“We are going to war.”</t1495> <t1496>Gerineldo Márquez did not believe him.</t1496>
<t1497>—With what weapons? —he asked.</t1497>
<t1498>—With theirs —Aureliano replied.</t1498>
<t1499>On Tuesday at midnight, in a reckless operation, twenty-one men under thirty years old led by Aureliano Buendía, armed with table knives and sharpened irons, took the garrison by surprise, seized the weapons, and executed in the courtyard the captain and the four soldiers who had murdered the woman.</t1499>
<t1500>That same night, while the firing squad's volleys were heard, Arcadio was named the civil and military chief of the town.</t1500> <t1501>The married rebels barely had time to say goodbye to their wives, whom they left to fend for themselves.</t1501> <t1502>They left at dawn, acclaimed by the population freed from terror, to join the forces of the revolutionary general Victorio Medina, who according to the latest news was in the vicinity of Manaure.</t1502> <t1503>Before leaving, Aureliano pulled Don Apolinar Moscote out of a wardrobe.</t1503> <t1504>"You stay calm, father-in-law," he said to him.</t1504> <t1505>"The new government guarantees, on its word of honor, your personal safety and that of your family."</t1505> <t1506>Don Apolinar Moscote had difficulty identifying that conspirator with high boots and a rifle slung over his back with the man he had played dominoes with until nine o'clock at night.</t1506>
<t1507>—This is nonsense, Aurelito —he exclaimed.</t1507>
<t1508>—No nonsense —said Aureliano—.</t1508> <t1509>It's war.</t1509> <t1510>And don't call me Aurelito anymore, I am now Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1510>
<t1511>Colonel Aureliano Buendía promoted thirty-two armed uprisings and lost them all.</t1511> <t1512>He had seventeen male children by seventeen different women, who were exterminated one by one in a single night, before the oldest turned thirty-five years old.</t1512> <t1513>He escaped fourteen assassination attempts, seventy-three ambushes, and a firing squad.</t1513> <t1514>He survived a dose of strychnine in his coffee that would have been enough to kill a horse.</t1514> <t1515>He rejected the Order of Merit that the president of the republic awarded him.</t1515> <t1516>He became the general commander of the revolutionary forces, with jurisdiction and command from one border to the other, and the most feared man by the government, but he never allowed a photograph to be taken of him.</t1516> <t1517>He declined the lifetime pension offered to him after the war and lived to old age on the goldfish he made in his workshop in Macondo.</t1517> <t1518>Although he always fought at the front of his men, the only wound he received was self-inflicted after signing the capitulation of Neerlandia, which ended almost twenty years of civil wars.</t1518> <t1519>He shot himself in the chest with a pistol, and the bullet exited through his back without injuring any vital organs.</t1519> <t1520>The only thing that remained from all of that was a street with his name in Macondo.</t1520> <t1521>However, as he declared a few years before dying of old age, he did not even expect that on the morning he left with his twenty-one men to join the forces of General Victorio Medina.</t1521>
<t1522>—We leave Macondo to you —was all he said to Arcadio before leaving—.</t1522> <t1523>We leave it in good shape, make sure we find it better.</t1523>
<t1524>Arcadio gave a very personal interpretation to the recommendation.</t1524> <t1525>He invented a uniform with marshal's stripes and epaulets, inspired by the illustrations in one of Melquíades' books, and hung the saber with golden tassels of the executed captain at his waist.</t1525> <t1526>He placed the two artillery pieces at the entrance of the town, uniformed his former students, exacerbated by his incendiary proclamations, and let them wander armed through the streets to give outsiders an impression of invulnerability.</t1526> <t1527>It was a double-edged trick because the government did not dare attack the plaza for ten months, but when it did, it unleashed such a disproportionate force that it crushed the resistance in half an hour.</t1527> <t1528>From the first day of his mandate, Arcadio revealed his fondness for decrees.</t1528> <t1529>He read up to four daily to order and arrange everything that crossed his mind.</t1529> <t1530>He implemented mandatory military service from the age of eighteen, declared animals that roamed the streets after six in the evening to be of public utility, and imposed on adult men the obligation to wear a red armband.</t1530> <t1531>He confined Father Nicanor in the parish house under threat of execution and forbade him from saying mass or ringing the bells except to celebrate liberal victories.</t1531> <t1532>To ensure no one doubted the severity of his intentions, he ordered a firing squad to train in the public square by shooting at a scarecrow.</t1532> <t1533>At first, no one took him seriously.</t1533> <t1534>After all, they were just schoolboys playing at being grown-ups.</t1534> <t1535>But one night, when Arcadio entered Catarino's store, the band's trumpeter greeted him with a fanfare that provoked the laughter of the customers, and Arcadio had him executed for disrespecting authority.</t1535> <t1536>Those who protested were put on bread and water with their ankles in a stock he installed in a room of the school.</t1536> <t1537>"You are a murderer!" Úrsula would shout at him every time she learned of a new atrocity.</t1537> <t1538>"When Aureliano finds out, he will have you executed, and I will be the first to rejoice."</t1538> <t1539>But it was all in vain.</t1539> <t1540>Arcadio continued tightening the screws of unnecessary rigor until he became the cruelest ruler Macondo had ever known.</t1540> <t1541>"Now suffer the difference," said Don Apolinar Moscote on one occasion.</t1541> <t1542>"This is the liberal paradise."</t1542> <t1543>Arcadio found out.</t1543> <t1544>Leading a patrol, he stormed the house, destroyed the furniture, beat the daughters, and dragged Don Apolinar Moscote away.</t1544> <t1545>When Úrsula burst into the barracks courtyard, after crossing the town clamoring with shame and brandishing a tarred whip in rage, Arcadio himself was about to give the order to fire to the firing squad.</t1545>
<t1546>—Dare, bastard! —shouted Úrsula.</t1546>
<t1547>Before Arcadio had time to react, she delivered the first lash.</t1547> <t1548>"Dare, murderer," she shouted.</t1548> <t1549>"And kill me too, you son of a bad mother.</t1549> <t1550>That way I won't have eyes to cry over the shame of having raised a monster."</t1550> <t1551>Whipping him mercilessly, she chased him to the back of the yard, where Arcadio curled up like a snail.</t1551> <t1552>Don Apolinar Moscote was unconscious, tied to the post where they had previously kept the scarecrow, torn apart by training shots.</t1552> <t1553>The boys of the platoon scattered, fearful that Úrsula would end up venting her anger on them.</t1553> <t1554>But she didn't even look at them.</t1554> <t1555>She left Arcadio with his uniform dragging, roaring in pain and rage, and untied Don Apolinar Moscote to take him to his house.</t1555> <t1556>Before leaving the barracks, she released the prisoners from the stocks.</t1556>
<t1557>From then on, it was she who ruled the town.</t1557> <t1558>She reinstated Sunday mass, suspended the use of red armbands, and disqualified the arbitrary edicts.</t1558> <t1559>But despite her strength, she continued to mourn the misfortune of her fate.</t1559> <t1560>She felt so alone that she sought the useless company of her husband forgotten under the chestnut tree.</t1560> <t1561>"Look at what we've come to," she would say, while the June rains threatened to bring down the palm shelter.</t1561> <t1562>"Look at the empty house, our children scattered around the world, and the two of us alone again like at the beginning."</t1562> <t1563>José Arcadio Buendía, sunk in an abyss of unconsciousness, was deaf to her laments.</t1563> <t1564>At the onset of his madness, he would announce his daily urgencies with pressing Latin phrases.</t1564> <t1565>In fleeting moments of lucidity, when Amaranta brought him food, he would communicate his most bothersome grievances and would submit docilely to her cupping and mustard plasters.</t1565> <t1566>But by the time Úrsula went to lament by his side, he had lost all contact with reality.</t1566> <t1567>She would bathe him in parts while he sat on the little stool, giving him news of the family.</t1567> <t1568>"Aureliano has gone to war, it's been more than four months now, and we haven't heard from him," she would say, scrubbing his back with a soapy sponge.</t1568> <t1569>"José Arcadio came back, a big man taller than you and all embroidered in cross-stitch, but he only came to bring shame to our house."</t1569> <t1570>She thought she observed, however, that her husband grew sad with the bad news.</t1570> <t1571>So she opted to lie to him.</t1571> <t1572>"Don't believe what I'm telling you," she would say, while sprinkling ashes over his excrement to collect it with the shovel.</t1572> <t1573>"God wanted José Arcadio and Rebeca to get married, and now they are very happy."</t1573> <t1574>She became so sincere in the deceit that she herself ended up consoling herself with her own lies.</t1574> <t1575>"Arcadio is already a serious man," she would say, "and very brave, and very handsome in his uniform and with his saber."</t1575> <t1576>It was like talking to a dead man, because José Arcadio Buendía was already beyond the reach of any concern.</t1576> <t1577>But she insisted.</t1577> <t1578>She saw him so meek, so indifferent to everything, that she decided to untie him.</t1578> <t1579>He didn't even move from the little stool.</t1579> <t1580>He remained exposed to the sun and the rain, as if the ropes were unnecessary, because a force superior to any visible tie kept him bound to the trunk of the chestnut tree.</t1580> <t1581>By the month of August, when the winter began to seem eternal, Úrsula was finally able to give him news that seemed true.</t1581>
<t1582>—Notice that good luck keeps hounding us —he said to her—.</t1582> <t1583>Amaranta and the Italian from the pianola are going to get married.</t1583>
<t1584>Amaranta and Pietro Crespi, indeed, had deepened their friendship, sheltered by Úrsula's trust, who this time did not find it necessary to monitor the visits.</t1584><t1585>It was a twilight courtship.</t1585><t1586>The Italian would arrive at dusk, with a gardenia in his buttonhole, and translate Petrarch's sonnets for Amaranta.</t1586><t1587>They would stay in the corridor suffocated by room.</t1587><t1588>Amaranta's sensitivity, her discreet but enveloping tenderness, had woven around the suitor an invisible web that he had to physically push aside with his pale, ringless fingers to vignettes of pierced hearts and golden ribbons held by doves.</t1590><t1591>"I know this park in Florence," Pietro Crespi would say, going through the postcards.</t1591><t1592>"One extends a hand and the birds come down to eat."</t1592><t1593>Sometimes, before a watercolor of Venice, nostalgia would transform the smell of mud and rotten seafood from the canals into the warm aromas of cats among the ruins.</t1594><t1595>After crossing the ocean in search of it, after confusing it with passion in Rebeca's vehement caresses, Pietro Crespi had found love.</t1595><t1596>Happiness brought prosperity with it.</t1596><t1597>His store then occupied almost an entire block, and it was a fantasy greenhouse, with reproductions of the Florence bell tower that chimed the hour with a carillon concert, and musical boxes from Sorrento, and Chinese powder boxes that sang five-note tunes when opened, and all the musical instruments one could imagine and all the mechanical devices one could conceive.</t1597><t1598>Bruno Crespi, his younger brother, was in charge of the store, because he couldn't keep up with the demands of the music school.</t1598><t1599>Thanks to him, the Street of the Turks, with its dazzling display of trinkets, became a melodic haven to forget Arcadio's arbitrariness and the distant nightmare of the war.</t1599><t1600>When Úrsula arranged for the resumption of Sunday mass, Pietro Crespi donated a German harmonium to the church, organized a children's choir, and prepared a Gregorian repertoire that added a splendid note to Father Nicanor's taciturn ritual.</t1600><t1601>No one doubted that he would make Amaranta a happy wife.</t1601><t1602>Without rushing their feelings, letting themselves be carried by the natural flow of the heart, they reached a point where all that was left was to set the wedding date.</t1602><t1603>They would find no obstacles.</t1603><t1604>Úrsula privately blamed herself for having twisted Rebeca's fate with repeated postponements, and she was not willing to accumulate more remorse.</t1604><t1605>The rigor of mourning for Remedios' death had been relegated to a secondary place by the mortification of the embroidery basket from her lap and clasped her hand between his.</t1611><t1612>"I can't bear this wait any longer," he said to her.</t1612><t1613>"We'll get married next <t1616>—Don't be naive, Crespi —she smiled—, not even dead would I marry you.</t1616>
<t1617>Pietro Crespi lost control of himself.</t1617> <t1618>He cried shamelessly, almost breaking his fingers in desperation, but he could not break her.</t1618> <t1619>“Don't waste your time,” was all Amaranta said.</t1619> <t1620>“If you really love me that much, don't set foot in this house again.”</t1620> <t1621>Úrsula thought she would go mad with shame.</t1621> <t1622>Pietro Crespi the light in all the windows of the town, except in Amaranta's.</t1634> <t1635>On November second, the Day of the Dead, his brother opened the store and found all the lamps lit and all the music boxes basin of benzoin.</t1635>
<t1636>Úrsula arranged for him to be laid out in the house.</t1636> <t1637>Father Nicanor opposed religious services and burial in sacred ground.</t1637> <t1638>Úrsula confronted him.</t1638> <t1639>"In a way that neither you nor I can understand, that man was a saint," she said.</t1639> <t1640>"So I am going to bury him, against your will, next to Melquíades' grave."</t1640> <t1641>She did so, with the support of the entire town, in magnificent funerals.</t1641> <t1642>Amaranta did not leave the bedroom.</t1642> <t1643>From her bed, she heard Úrsula's weeping, the footsteps and murmurs of the crowd that invaded the house, the wails of the mourners, and then a deep silence smelling of trampled flowers.</t1643> <t1644>For a long time, she continued to feel the lavender breath of Pietro Crespi at dusk, but she had the strength not to succumb to delirium.</t1644> <t1645>Úrsula abandoned her.</t1645> <t1646>She did not even lift her eyes to pity her, the afternoon when Amaranta entered the kitchen and put her hand in the embers of the stove, until it hurt so much that she felt no more pain, only the stench of her own scorched flesh.</t1646> <t1647>It was a donkey's cure for remorse.</t1647> <t1648>For several days she walked around the house with her hand in a bowl of egg whites, and when the burns healed, it seemed as if the egg whites had also healed the ulcers of her heart.</t1648> <t1649>The only external mark the tragedy left on her was the black gauze bandage she wore on her burned hand, which she would wear until her death.</t1649>
<t1650>Arcadio gave a rare display of generosity by proclaiming an official mourning for the death of Pietro Crespi.</t1650> <t1651>Úrsula interpreted it as the return of the lost lamb.</t1651> <t1652>But she was mistaken.</t1652> <t1653>She had lost Arcadio, not since he donned the military uniform, but since always.</t1653> <t1654>She believed she had raised him like a son, as she had raised Rebeca, without privileges or discrimination.</t1654> <t1655>However, Arcadio was a lonely and frightened child during the insomnia plague, amidst Úrsula's utilitarian fever, José Arcadio Buendía's delusions, Aureliano's hermeticism, and the deadly rivalry between Amaranta and Rebeca.</t1655> <t1656>Aureliano taught him to read and write, thinking of something else, as a stranger would have done.</t1656> <t1657>He gave him his clothes, for Visitación to alter, when they were already about to be discarded.</t1657> <t1658>Arcadio suffered with his oversized shoes, his patched pants, and his womanly buttocks.</t1658> <t1659>He never managed to communicate with anyone better than he did with Visitación and Cataure in their language.</t1659> <t1660>Melquíades was the only one who truly cared for him, making him listen to his incomprehensible texts and giving him instructions on the art of daguerreotype.</t1660> <t1661>No one imagined how much he cried in secret over his death, and with what desperation he tried to revive him through the futile study of his papers.</t1661> <t1662>The school, where he was given attention and respect, and later power, with its decisive edicts and his uniform of glory, freed him from the weight of an old bitterness.</t1662> <t1663>One night, in Catarino's store, someone dared to say to him: "You don't deserve the surname you bear."</t1663> <t1664>Contrary to what everyone expected, Arcadio did not have him shot.</t1664>
<t1665>—With great pride —he said—, I am not a Buendía.</t1665>
<t1666>Those who knew the secret of his parentage thought from that reply that he was also aware, but in reality, he never was.</t1666> <t1667>Pilar Ternera, his mother, who had made his blood boil in the daguerreotype room, was for him an obsession as irresistible as she was first for José Arcadio and then for Aureliano.</t1667> <t1668>Despite having lost her charms and the splendor of her laughter, he sought her out and found her in the trail of her smoky scent.</t1668> <t1669>Shortly before the war, one midday when she went later than usual to pick up her youngest son from school, Arcadio was waiting for her in the room where he used to take his siesta, and where he later installed the stocks.</t1669> <t1670>While the child played in the yard, he waited in the hammock, trembling with anxiety, knowing that Pilar Ternera had to pass by there.</t1670> <t1671>She arrived.</t1671> <t1672>Arcadio grabbed her by the wrist and tried to pull her into the hammock.</t1672> <t1673>“I can't, I can't,” said Pilar Ternera, horrified.</t1673> <t1674>“You can't imagine how much I would like to please you, but God is my witness that I can't.”</t1674> <t1675>Arcadio grabbed her by the waist with his tremendous hereditary strength, and felt the world fade away at the touch of her skin.</t1675> <t1676>“Don't play the saint,” he said.</t1676> <t1677>“After all, everyone knows you're a whore.”</t1677> <t1678>Pilar overcame the disgust that her miserable fate inspired in her.</t1678>
<t1679>—The children will notice —he murmured—.</t1679> <t1680>It's better if you leave the door unlatched tonight.</t1680>
<t1681>Arcadio waited for her that night, shivering with fever in the hammock.</t1681><t1682>He waited without sleeping, listening to the restless crickets of the endless dawn and the relentless schedule of the curlews, increasingly convinced that he had been deceived.</t1682><t1683>Suddenly, when his anxiety had turned into rage, the door opened.</t1683><t1684>A few months later, in front of the firing squad, Arcadio would relive the lost steps in the classroom, the stumbles against the benches, and finally the density of a body in the darkness of the room and the beats of the air pumped by a heart that was not his own.</t1684><t1685>He extended his hand and found another hand with two rings on the same finger, which was about to sink into the darkness.</t1685><t1686>He felt the veins' texture, the pulse of his misfortune, and felt the moist palm with the lifeline cut at the base of the thumb by the claw of death.</t1686><t1687>Then he understood that this was not the woman he was waiting for, because she did not smell of smoke but of little flower pomade, and she had inflated and blind breasts with male nipples, and a stone-like and round sex like a nut, and the chaotic tenderness of exalted inexperience.</t1687><t1688>She was a virgin and had the unbelievable name of Santa Sofía de la Piedad.</t1688><t1689>Pilar Ternera had paid her fifty pesos, half of her lifetime
