parents' consent, to whom Pilar Ternera had paid the other half of her savings.</t1692><t1693>Later, when the government troops evicted them from the premises, they made love among the tins of lard and sacks of corn in the back room.</t1693><t1694>By the time Arcadio was appointed civil and military chief, they had a daughter.</t1694>
<t1695>The only relatives who found out were José Arcadio and Rebeca, with whom Arcadio maintained intimate relations, based not so much on kinship as on complicity.</t1695> <t1696>José Arcadio had bowed his head to the matrimonial yoke.</t1696> <t1697>Rebeca's firm character, the voracity of her womb, her tenacious ambition, absorbed the husband's colossal energy, who from a lazy womanizer turned into a huge work animal.</t1697> <t1698>They had a clean and orderly house.</t1698> <t1699>Rebeca would open it wide at dawn, and the wind from the graves would enter through the windows and exit through the patio doors, leaving the walls whitewashed and the furniture tanned by the saltpeter of the dead.</t1699> <t1700>The hunger for land, the cloc cloc of her parents' bones, the impatience of her blood in the face of Pietro Crespi's passivity were relegated to the attic of memory.</t1700> <t1701>All day she would embroider by the window, oblivious to the turmoil of the war, until the ceramic pots started to vibrate in the cupboard and she would get up to heat the food, long before the scrawny tracking dogs appeared and then the colossus with gaiters and spurs and a double-barreled shotgun, who sometimes carried a deer on his shoulder and almost always a string of rabbits or wild ducks.</t1701> <t1702>One afternoon, at the beginning of his rule, Arcadio went to visit them unexpectedly.</t1702> <t1703>They hadn't seen him since they left the house, but he was so affectionate and familiar that they invited him to share the stew.</t1703>
<t1704>Only when they were having coffee did Arcadio reveal the reason for his visit: he had received a complaint against José Arcadio.</t1704> <t1705>It was said that he started by plowing his yard and continued straight through the adjoining lands, knocking down fences and destroying ranches with his oxen, until he forcibly took over the best plots in the area.</t1705> <t1706>To the peasants he had not dispossessed, because he was not interested in their lands, he imposed a tax that he collected every Saturday with his hunting dogs and double-barreled shotgun.</t1706> <t1707>He did not deny it.</t1707> <t1708>He based his right on the fact that the usurped lands had been distributed by José Arcadio Buendía at the time of the founding, and he believed it possible to prove that his father had been crazy since then, as he had disposed of an estate that actually belonged to the family.</t1708> <t1709>It was an unnecessary argument because Arcadio had not come to seek justice.</t1709> <t1710>He simply offered to create a property registry office so that José Arcadio could legalize the titles of the usurped land, on the condition that he delegated to the local government the right to collect the taxes.</t1710> <t1711>They came to an agreement.</t1711> <t1712>Years later, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía examined the property titles, he found that all the lands visible from the hill of his yard to the horizon, including the cemetery, were registered in his brother's name, and that in the eleven months of his mandate, Arcadio had taken not only the tax money but also the money he charged the town for the right to bury the dead in José Arcadio's plots.</t1712>
<t1713>Úrsula took several months to learn what was already public knowledge, because people hid it from her to avoid increasing her suffering.</t1713> <t1714>She began to suspect it.</t1714> <t1715>"Arcadio is building a house," she confided to her husband with feigned pride, while trying to put a spoonful of totumo syrup into his mouth.</t1715> <t1716>However, she sighed involuntarily: "I don't know why all this smells bad to me."</t1716> <t1717>Later, when she found out that Arcadio had not only finished the house but had also ordered Viennese furniture, she confirmed her suspicion that he was using public funds.</t1717> <t1718>"You are the shame of our family name," she shouted at him one Sunday after mass, when she saw him in the new house playing cards with his officers.</t1718> <t1719>Arcadio paid no attention to her.</t1719> <t1720>Only then did Úrsula learn that he had a six-month-old daughter, and that Santa Sofía de la Piedad, with whom he was living without being married, was pregnant again.</t1720> <t1721>She resolved to write to Colonel Aureliano Buendía, wherever he might be, to inform him of the situation.</t1721> <t1722>But the events that unfolded in those days not only thwarted her plans but made her regret having conceived them.</t1722> <t1723>The war, which until then had been just a word to designate a vague and remote circumstance, became a dramatic reality.</t1723> <t1724>At the end of February, an elderly woman with an ashen appearance arrived in Macondo, riding a donkey loaded with brooms.</t1724> <t1725>She seemed so harmless that the patrols let her pass without questions, like one of the many vendors who often came from the swamp villages.</t1725> <t1726>She went directly to the barracks.</t1726> <t1727>Arcadio received her in the room that had once been the classroom, which had since been transformed into a sort of rear-guard camp, with hammocks rolled up and hung on the rings, and bedrolls piled in the corners, and rifles and carbines and even hunting shotguns scattered on the floor.</t1727> <t1728>The elderly woman stood at attention in a military salute before identifying herself:</t1728>
<t1729>—I am Colonel Gregorio Stevenson.</t1729>
<t1730>He brought bad news.</t1730> <t1731>The last pockets of liberal resistance, he said, were being exterminated.</t1731> <t1732>Colonel Aureliano Buendía, whom he had left retreating around the Riohacha area, entrusted him with the mission of speaking to Arcadio.</t1732> <t1733>He was to surrender the town without resistance, on the condition that the lives and properties of the liberals would be respected under a word of honor.</t1733> <t1734>Arcadio examined with a look of pity that strange messenger who could have been mistaken for a runaway grandmother.</t1734>
<t1735>—You, of course, bring some written paper —he said.</t1735>
<t1736>—Of course —replied the emissary—, I don't have it with me.</t1736> <t1737>It is easy to understand that under the current circumstances, one does not carry anything compromising.</t1737>
<t1738>While he was speaking, he took out from his bodice and placed on the table a little gold fish.</t1738> <t1739>“I think this will be enough,” he said.</t1739> <t1740>Arcadio verified that it was indeed one of the little fish made by Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1740> <t1741>But someone could have bought it before the war, or stolen it, and therefore it had no merit as a safe-conduct.</t1741> <t1742>The messenger went so far as to violate a war secret to prove his identity.</t1742> <t1743>He revealed that he was on a mission to Curaçao, where he hoped to recruit exiles from all over the Caribbean and acquire enough weapons and supplies to attempt a landing by the end of the year.</t1743> <t1744>Trusting in that plan, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was not in favor of making useless sacrifices at that moment.</t1744> <t1745>But Arcadio was inflexible.</t1745> <t1746>He had the messenger imprisoned while verifying his identity, and resolved to defend the place to the death.</t1746>
<t1747>He did not have to wait long.</t1747> <t1748>The news of the liberal failure became increasingly concrete.</t1748> <t1749>At the end of March, in the early hours of a premature rainy morning, the tense calm of the previous weeks was abruptly shattered by a desperate bugle call, followed by a cannon shot that destroyed the temple tower.</t1749> <t1750>In reality, Arcadio's will to resist was madness.</t1750> <t1751>He had no more than fifty poorly armed men, each with a maximum supply of twenty cartridges.</t1751> <t1752>But among them, his former students, excited by high-sounding proclamations, were determined to sacrifice their skins for a lost cause.</t1752> <t1753>Amid the clatter of boots, contradictory orders, cannon shots that made the ground tremble, hasty gunfire, and senseless bugle calls, the supposed Colonel Stevenson managed to speak with Arcadio.</t1753> <t1754>“Spare me the indignity of dying in the stocks with these women's rags,” he said.</t1754> <t1755>“If I am to die, let it be fighting.”</t1755> <t1756>He managed to convince him.</t1756> <t1757>Arcadio ordered that he be given a weapon with twenty cartridges and left him with five men defending the barracks while he went with his staff to lead the resistance.</t1757> <t1758>He did not reach the swamp road.</t1758> <t1759>The barricades had been torn apart and the defenders were fighting in the open in the streets, first as far as their rifle supplies would last, then with pistols against rifles, and finally hand to hand.</t1759> <t1760>Faced with imminent defeat, some women took to the streets armed with sticks and kitchen knives.</t1760> <t1761>In that confusion, Arcadio found Amaranta who was looking for him like a madwoman, in her nightgown, with two old pistols of José Arcadio Buendía.</t1761> <t1762>He gave his rifle to an officer who had been disarmed in the skirmish, and escaped with Amaranta down an adjacent street to take her home.</t1762> <t1763>Úrsula was at the door, waiting, indifferent to the gunfire that had opened a breach in the facade of the neighboring house.</t1763> <t1764>The rain was letting up, but the streets were slippery and soft like melted soap, and distances had to be guessed in the darkness.</t1764> <t1765>Arcadio left Amaranta with Úrsula and tried to confront two soldiers who fired a blind volley from the corner.</t1765> <t1766>The old pistols, stored for many years in a wardrobe, did not work.</t1766> <t1767>Protecting Arcadio with her body, Úrsula tried to drag him to the house.</t1767>
<t1768>—Come on, for God's sake —he shouted at him—.</t1768> <t1769>Enough of this madness!</t1769>
<t1770>The soldiers aimed at them.</t1770>
<t1771>—Let go of that man, ma'am —shouted one of them—, or we won't be responsible for what happens!</t1771>
<t1772>Arcadio pushed Úrsula towards the house and surrendered.</t1772> <t1773>Shortly after, the shooting stopped and the bells began to ring.</t1773> <t1774>The resistance had been annihilated in less than half an hour.</t1774> <t1775>Not a single one of Arcadio's men survived the assault, but before dying they took down three hundred soldiers.</t1775> <t1776>The last stronghold was the barracks.</t1776> <t1777>Before being attacked, the supposed Colonel Gregorio Stevenson set the prisoners free and ordered his men to go out and fight in the street.</t1777> <t1778>The extraordinary mobility and accurate aim with which he fired his twenty cartridges from different windows gave the impression that the barracks was well guarded, and the attackers blew it to pieces with cannon fire.</t1778> <t1779>The captain who led the operation was astonished to find the rubble deserted, and a single man in his underwear, dead, with an unloaded rifle still clutched by an arm that had been torn off.</t1779> <t1780>He had a thick mane of a woman's hair coiled at the nape with a comb, and around his neck a scapular with a little gold fish.</t1780> <t1781>When he turned him over with the tip of his boot to light up his face, the captain was perplexed.</t1781> <t1782>“Shit,” he exclaimed.</t1782> <t1783>Other officers approached.</t1783>
<t1784>—Look where this man turned up —the captain told them—.</t1784> <t1785>It's Gregorio Stevenson.</t1785>
<t1786>At dawn, after a summary court-martial, Arcadio was executed by firing squad against the cemetery wall.</t1786> <t1787>In the last two hours of his life, he could not understand why the fear that had tormented him since childhood had disappeared.</t1787> <t1788>Impassive, without even bothering to demonstrate his newfound courage, he listened to the endless charges of the accusation.</t1788> <t1789>He thought of Úrsula, who at that hour must be under the chestnut tree having coffee with José Arcadio Buendía.</t1789> <t1790>He thought of his eight-month-old daughter, who still did not have a name, and of the one who was to be born in August.</t1790> <t1791>He thought of Santa Sofía de la Piedad, whom he had left the night before salting a deer for Saturday's lunch, and he longed for her hair streaming over her shoulders and her eyelashes that seemed artificial.</t1791> <t1792>He thought of his people without sentimentality, in a severe reckoning with life, beginning to understand how much he really loved the people he had most hated.</t1792> <t1793>The president of the court-martial began his final speech, before Arcadio realized that two hours had passed.</t1793> <t1794>"Although the proven charges may not have had sufficient merit," said the president, "the irresponsible and criminal recklessness with which the accused pushed his subordinates to a useless death would be enough to deserve the death penalty."</t1794> <t1795>In the dilapidated school where he first experienced the security of power, a few meters from the room where he knew the uncertainty of love, Arcadio found the formality of death ridiculous.</t1795> <t1796>In reality, it was not death that mattered to him but life, and that is why the sensation he experienced when the sentence was pronounced was not one of fear but of nostalgia.</t1796> <t1797>He did not speak until he was asked what his last wish was.</t1797>
<t1798>—Tell my wife —he replied with a well-modulated voice— to name the girl Úrsula.</t1798> <t1799>—He paused and confirmed—: Úrsula, like the grandmother.</t1799> <t1800>And also tell her that if the one to be born is a boy, to name him José Arcadio, but not after the uncle, rather after the grandfather.</t1800>
<t1801>Before they took him to the wall, Father Nicanor tried to assist him.</t1801> <t1802>“I have nothing to repent for,” said Arcadio, and he put himself at the orders of the squad after having a cup of black coffee.</t1802> <t1803>The leader of the squad, a specialist in summary executions, had a name that was much more than a coincidence: Captain Roque Carnicero.</t1803> <t1804>On the way to the cemetery, under the persistent drizzle, Arcadio noticed that a radiant Wednesday was dawning on the horizon.</t1804> <t1805>Nostalgia faded with the fog and left in its place an immense curiosity.</t1805> <t1806>Only when he was ordered to turn his back to the wall did Arcadio see Rebeca with wet hair and a dress of pink flowers, opening the house wide open.</t1806> <t1807>He made an effort for her to recognize him.</t1807> <t1808>Indeed, Rebeca casually looked towards the wall and was paralyzed with astonishment, and could barely react to wave goodbye to Arcadio with her hand.</t1808> <t1809>Arcadio responded in the same way.</t1809> <t1810>At that moment, the smoky barrels of the rifles aimed at him, and he heard letter by letter the encyclicals sung by Melquíades, and felt the lost steps of Santa Sofía de la Piedad, virgin, in the classroom, and experienced in his nose the same icy hardness that had caught his attention in the nostrils of Remedios' corpse.</t1810> <t1811>“Ah, damn! —he managed to think—, I forgot to say that if it was a girl, they should name her Remedios.”</t1811> <t1812>Then, accumulated in a tearing claw, he felt again all the terror that had tormented him in life.</t1812> <t1813>The captain gave the order to fire.</t1813> <t1814>Arcadio barely had time to puff out his chest and lift his head, without understanding where the burning liquid that was scorching his thighs was flowing from.</t1814>
<t1815>—Bastards! —he shouted—.</t1815> <t1816>Long live the liberal party!</t1816>
<t1817>The war ended in May.</t1817> <t1818>Two weeks before the government made the official announcement, in a high-sounding proclamation that promised ruthless punishment for the promoters of the rebellion, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was captured when he was about to reach the western border disguised as an indigenous sorcerer.</t1818> <t1819>Of the twenty-one men who followed him to war, fourteen died in combat, six were wounded, and only one accompanied him at the moment of final defeat: Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t1819> <t1820>The news of the capture was announced in Macondo with an extraordinary decree.</t1820> <t1821>“He is alive,” Úrsula informed her husband.</t1821> <t1822>“Let us pray to God that his enemies have mercy.”</t1822> <t1823>After three days of weeping, one afternoon while she was stirring a milk candy in the kitchen, she clearly heard her son's voice very close to her ear.</t1823> <t1824>“It was Aureliano,” she shouted, running towards the chestnut tree to give the news to her husband.</t1824> <t1825>“I don't know how the miracle happened, but he is alive and we will see him very soon.”</t1825> <t1826>She took it for granted.</t1826> <t1827>She had the floors of the house washed and the furniture rearranged.</t1827> <t1828>A few weeks later, a rumor without origin that would not be supported by the decree dramatically confirmed the omen.</t1828> <t1829>Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been sentenced to death, and the sentence would be carried out in Macondo, as a warning to the population.</t1829>
<t1830>One Monday, at twenty past ten in the morning, Amaranta was dressing Aureliano José when she perceived a distant commotion and a trumpet call, a second before Úrsula burst into the room with a shout: "They're bringing him."</t1830> <t1831>The troops struggled to subdue the overflowing crowd with rifle butts.</t1831> <t1832>Úrsula and Amaranta ran to the corner, pushing their way through, and then they saw him.</t1832> <t1833>He looked like a beggar.</t1833> <t1834>His clothes were torn, his hair and beard tangled, and he was barefoot.</t1834> <t1835>He walked without feeling the scorching dust, his hands tied behind his back with a rope held by a mounted officer.</t1835> <t1836>Next to him, equally shabby and defeated, they were bringing Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t1836> <t1837>They were not sad.</t1837> <t1838>They seemed rather disturbed by the crowd that shouted all kinds of insults at the troops.</t1838>
<t1839>—My son! —shouted Úrsula amidst the commotion, and she slapped the soldier who tried to stop her.</t1839> <t1840>The officer's horse reared up.</t1840> <t1841>Then Colonel Aureliano Buendía stopped, trembling, dodged his mother's arms, and fixed a hard gaze on her eyes.</t1841>
<t1842>—Go home, mom —he said—.</t1842> <t1843>Ask the authorities for permission and come see me in jail.</t1843>
<t1844>He looked at Amaranta, who remained undecided two steps behind Úrsula, and smiled as he asked her: "What happened to your hand?".</t1844> <t1845>Amaranta raised her hand with the black bandage.</t1845> <t1846>"A burn," she said, and moved Úrsula aside so she wouldn't be trampled by the horses.</t1846> <t1847>The troop fired.</t1847> <t1848>A special guard surrounded the prisoners and trotted them to the barracks.</t1848>
<t1849>In the evening, Úrsula visited Colonel Aureliano Buendía in jail.</t1849> <t1850>She had tried to obtain permission through Don Apolinar Moscote, but he had lost all authority in the face of the omnipotence of the military.</t1850> <t1851>Father Nicanor was bedridden with a hepatic fever.</t1851> <t1852>The parents of Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, who was not condemned to death, had tried to see him and were turned away with rifle butts.</t1852> <t1853>Faced with the impossibility of finding intermediaries, convinced that her son would be executed at dawn, Úrsula made a bundle with the things she wanted to take him and went alone to the barracks.</t1853>
<t1854>—I am the mother of Colonel Aureliano Buendía —she announced.</t1854>
<t1855>The sentinels blocked her way.</t1855> <t1856>“I’m going in anyway,” Úrsula warned them.</t1856> <t1857>“So if you have orders to shoot, start right now.”</t1857> <t1858>She pushed one aside and entered the old classroom, where a group of naked soldiers were greasing their weapons.</t1858> <t1859>An officer in campaign uniform, rosy-cheeked, with very thick glasses and ceremonious gestures, signaled the sentinels to withdraw.</t1859>
<t1860>—I am the mother of Colonel Aureliano Buendía —Úrsula repeated.</t1860>
<t1861>—You mean to say —corrected the officer with a kind smile— that she is the mother of Mr. Aureliano Buendía.</t1861>
<t1862>Úrsula recognized in his affected way of speaking the languid cadence of the people from the highlands, the cachacos.</t1862>
<t1863>—As you say, sir —he admitted—, as long as you allow me to see it.</t1863>
<t1864>There were higher orders not to allow visits to those condemned to death, but the officer took responsibility for granting a fifteen-minute interview.</t1864> <t1865>Úrsula showed him what she carried in the bundle: a change of clean clothes, the boots her son wore for the wedding, and the dulce de leche she had saved for him since the day she sensed his return.</t1865> <t1866>She found Colonel Aureliano Buendía in the stocks room, lying on a cot with his arms open, because his armpits were studded with boils.</t1866> <t1867>They had allowed him to shave.</t1867> <t1868>The dense mustache with twisted ends accentuated the angularity of his cheekbones.</t1868> <t1869>To Úrsula, he seemed paler than when he left, a bit taller, and more solitary than ever.</t1869> <t1870>He was aware of the details of the house: Pietro Crespi's suicide, the arbitrariness and execution of Arcadio, José Arcadio Buendía's impassiveness under the chestnut tree.</t1870> <t1871>He knew that Amaranta had dedicated her virgin widowhood to raising Aureliano José, and that he was beginning to show very good judgment and could read and write at the same time he was learning to speak.</t1871> <t1872>From the moment she entered the room, Úrsula felt intimidated by her son's maturity, by his aura of dominance, by the glow of authority that radiated from his skin.</t1872> <t1873>She was surprised that he was so well-informed. "You know I'm a fortune teller," he joked.</t1873> <t1874>And he added seriously: "This morning, when they brought me, I had the impression that I had already gone through all this."</t1874> <t1875>In truth, while the crowd thundered as he passed, he was lost in his thoughts, amazed at how the town had aged in a year.</t1875> <t1876>The almond trees had broken leaves.</t1876> <t1877>The houses painted blue, then painted red, and then painted blue again, had ended up acquiring an indefinable color.</t1877>
<t1878>—What did you expect? —sighed Úrsula—.</t1878> <t1879>Time passes.</t1879>
<t1880>—That's right —admitted Aureliano—, but not so much.</t1880>
<t1881>In this way, the long-awaited visit, for which both had prepared questions and even anticipated the answers, turned out to be the same everyday conversation as always.</t1881> <t1882>When the sentinel announced the end of the interview, Aureliano pulled a roll of sweaty papers from under the mat of the cot.</t1882> <t1883>They were his verses.</t1883> <t1884>The ones inspired by Remedios, which he had taken with him when he left, and the ones written afterwards, in the hazardous pauses of the war.</t1884> <t1885>“Promise me that no one will read them,” he said.</t1885> <t1886>“Tonight, burn them in the oven.”</t1886> <t1887>Úrsula promised and stood up to give him a farewell kiss.</t1887>
<t1888>—I brought you a revolver —he murmured.</t1888>
<t1889>Colonel Aureliano Buendía confirmed that the sentry was not in sight.</t1889> <t1890>“It's no use to me,” he replied in a low voice.</t1890> <t1891>“But give it to me, just in case they search you on the way out.”</t1891> <t1892>Úrsula took the revolver out of her bodice and he placed it under the cot's mat.</t1892> <t1893>“And now, don't say goodbye,” he concluded with calm emphasis.</t1893> <t1894>“Don't beg anyone or lower yourself before anyone.</t1894> <t1895>Consider that I was executed a long time ago.”</t1895> <t1896>Úrsula bit her lips to keep from crying.</t1896>
<t1897>—Put hot stones on the boils —he said.</t1897>
<t1898>He turned around and left the room.</t1898> <t1899>Colonel Aureliano Buendía remained standing, thoughtful, until the door closed.</t1899> <t1900>Then he lay back down with his arms spread out.</t1900> <t1901>Since the beginning of adolescence, when he began to be aware of his premonitions, he thought that death had to announce itself with a definite, unequivocal, irrevocable sign, but he was only a few hours away from dying, and the sign had not come.</t1901> <t1902>On one occasion, a very beautiful woman entered his camp in Tucurinca and asked the sentries to let her see him.</t1902> <t1903>They let her pass because they knew the fanaticism of some mothers who sent their daughters to the bedrooms of the most notable warriors, as they themselves said, to improve the race.</t1903> <t1904>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was that night finishing the poem of the man who had gotten lost in the rain when the girl entered the room.</t1904> <t1905>He turned his back to her to put the sheet in the locked drawer where he kept his verses.</t1905> <t1906>And then he felt it.</t1906> <t1907>He grabbed the pistol in the drawer without turning his face.</t1907>
<t1908>—Don't despair, please —he said.</t1908>
<t1909>When he turned with the gun cocked, the girl had lowered hers and didn't know what to do.</t1909> <t1910>That was how he had managed to evade four out of eleven ambushes.</t1910> <t1911>In contrast, someone who was never captured entered the revolutionary barracks of Manaure one night and stabbed to death his close friend, Colonel Magnífico Visbal, to whom he had given up his cot so he could sweat out a fever.</t1911> <t1912>A few meters away, sleeping in a hammock in the same room, he didn't notice anything.</t1912> <t1913>His efforts to systematize the omens were futile.</t1913> <t1914>They appeared suddenly, in a burst of supernatural lucidity, like an absolute and momentary conviction, but elusive.</t1914> <t1915>Sometimes they were so natural that he didn't identify them as omens until they came true.</t1915> <t1916>Other times they were definitive and didn't come true.</t1916> <t1917>Often they were nothing more than vulgar strokes of superstition.</t1917> <t1918>But when he was condemned to death and asked to express his last wish, he had no difficulty identifying the omen that inspired his response:</t1918>
<t1919>—I request that the sentence be carried out in Macondo —he said.</t1919>
<t1920>The president of the court became upset.</t1920>
<t1921>—Don't be clever, Buendía —he said to him—.</t1921> <t1922>It's a stratagem to buy time.</t1922>
<t1923>—If you don't fulfill it, that's your problem —said the colonel—, but that is my last will.</t1923>
<t1924>Since then, the omens had abandoned him.</t1924> <t1925>The day Úrsula visited him in jail, after much thought, he concluded that perhaps death would not announce itself that time, because it did not depend on chance but on the will of his executioners.</t1925> <t1926>He spent the night awake, tormented by the pain of the boils.</t1926> <t1927>Shortly before dawn, he heard footsteps in the corridor.</t1927> <t1928>“They are coming,” he said to himself, and thought without reason of José Arcadio Buendía, who at that moment was thinking of him, under the gloomy dawn of the chestnut tree.</t1928> <t1929>He did not feel fear, nor nostalgia, but an intestinal rage at the idea that this artificial death would not allow him to know the end of so many things he left unfinished.</t1929> <t1930>The door opened and the guard came in with a bowl of coffee.</t1930> <t1931>The next day at the same hour, he was still as he had been, raging with the pain in his armpits, and exactly the same thing happened.</t1931> <t1932>On Thursday, he shared the dulce de leche with the guards and put on the clean clothes, which were tight on him, and the patent leather boots.</t1932> <t1933>Even on Friday, he had not yet been executed.</t1933>
<t1934>In reality, they did not dare to carry out the sentence.</t1934> <t1935>The rebellion of the people made the military think that the execution of Colonel Aureliano Buendía would have serious political consequences not only in Macondo but throughout the entire swamp region, so they consulted the authorities of the provincial capital.</t1935> <t1936>On Saturday night, while they awaited the response, Captain Roque Carnicero went with other officers to Catarino's store.</t1936> <t1937>Only one woman, almost pressured with threats, dared to take him to the room.</t1937> <t1938>“They don't want to sleep with a man they know is going to die,” she confessed to him.</t1938> <t1939>“No one knows how it will happen, but everyone is saying that the officer who executes Colonel Aureliano Buendía, and all the soldiers in the squad, one by one, will be killed without fail, sooner or later, even if they hide at the end of the world.”</t1939> <t1940>Captain Roque Carnicero discussed it with the other officers, and they discussed it with their superiors.</t1940> <t1941>On Sunday, although no one had revealed it frankly, although no military act had disturbed the tense calm of those days, the whole town knew that the officers were willing to avoid the responsibility of the execution with all kinds of excuses.</t1941> <t1942>On Monday's mail came the official order: the execution had to be carried out within twenty-four hours.</t1942> <t1943>That night the officers put seven slips of paper with their names into a cap, and the unmerciful fate of Captain Roque Carnicero marked him with the winning slip.</t1943> <t1944>“Bad luck has no loopholes,” he said with deep bitterness.</t1944> <t1945>“I was born a son of a bitch and I will die a son of a bitch.”</t1945> <t1946>At five in the morning he chose the squad by lot, formed them in the courtyard, and woke the condemned man with a premonitory phrase:</t1946>
<t1947>—Let's go, Buendía —he said to him—.</t1947> <t1948>Our time has come.</t1948>
<t1949>—So this was it —replied the colonel—.</t1949> <t1950>I was dreaming that my boils had burst.</t1950>
<t1951>Rebeca Buendía would get up at three in the morning ever since she learned that Aureliano would be executed.</t1951> <t1952>She would stay in the dark bedroom, watching the cemetery wall through the half-open window, while the bed she was sitting on shook with José Arcadio's snores.</t1952> <t1953>She waited all week with the same hidden obstinacy with which she once waited for Pietro Crespi's letters.</t1953> <t1954>"They won't execute him here," José Arcadio would tell her.</t1954> <t1955>"They'll execute him at midnight in the barracks so that no one knows who formed the firing squad, and they'll bury him right there."</t1955> <t1956>Rebeca kept waiting.</t1956> <t1957>"They're so stupid that they'll execute him here," she would say.</t1957> <t1958>She was so sure that she had planned the way she would open the door to wave goodbye.</t1958> <t1959>"They won't bring him through the street," José Arcadio insisted, "with only six scared soldiers, knowing that people are ready for anything."</t1959> <t1960>Indifferent to her husband's logic, Rebeca continued at the window.</t1960>
<t1961>—You'll see that they are that dumb —he said.</t1961>
<t1962>On Tuesday at five in the morning, José Arcadio had taken his coffee and let the dogs out when Rebeca closed the window and grabbed the headboard of the bed to keep from falling.</t1962> <t1963>“Here he comes,” she sighed.</t1963> <t1964>“How beautiful he is.”</t1964> <t1965>José Arcadio looked out the window and saw him, trembling in the dawn light, wearing pants that had been his in his youth.</t1965> <t1966>He was already with his back to the wall and had his hands on his waist because the burning knots in his armpits prevented him from lowering his arms.</t1966> <t1967>“So much suffering,” murmured Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1967> <t1968>“So much suffering for six faggots to kill you without being able to do anything.”</t1968> <t1969>He repeated it with such rage that it almost seemed like fervor, and Captain Roque Carnicero was moved because he thought he was praying.</t1969> <t1970>When the squad aimed at him, the rage had materialized into a viscous and bitter substance that numbed his tongue and forced him to close his eyes.</t1970> <t1971>Then the aluminum glow of dawn disappeared, and he saw himself again, very young, in short pants and a bow around his neck, and saw his father on a splendid afternoon leading him inside the tent, and he saw the ice.</t1971> <t1972>When he heard the shout, he thought it was the squad's final order.</t1972> <t1973>He opened his eyes with a chilling curiosity, expecting to see the incandescent trajectory of the bullets, but he only found Captain Roque Carnicero with his arms raised, and José Arcadio crossing the street with his dreadful shotgun ready to fire.</t1973>
<t1974>—Don't shoot —the captain said to José Arcadio—.</t1974> <t1975>You are sent by Divine Providence.</t1975>
<t1976>There another war began.</t1976> <t1977>Captain Roque Carnicero and his six men went with Colonel Aureliano Buendía to liberate the revolutionary general Victorio Medina, sentenced to death in Riohacha.</t1977> <t1978>They thought they could save time by crossing the mountains via the route José Arcadio Buendía had taken to found Macondo, but within a week they were convinced it was an impossible endeavor.</t1978> <t1979>So they had to take the dangerous foothill route, with no more ammunition than that of the firing squad.</t1979> <t1980>They camped near the towns, and one of them, with a little gold fish in hand, would enter in disguise in broad daylight and make contact with the resting liberals, who the next morning would go out hunting and never return.</t1980> <t1981>When they sighted Riohacha from a bend in the mountains, General Victorio Medina had already been executed.</t1981> <t1982>Colonel Aureliano Buendía's men proclaimed him the leader of the revolutionary forces of the Caribbean coast, with the rank of general.</t1982> <t1983>He assumed the position but rejected the promotion, imposing on himself the condition not to accept it until the conservative regime was overthrown.</t1983> <t1984>Within three months they had managed to arm more than a thousand men, but they were exterminated.</t1984> <t1985>The survivors reached the eastern border.</t1985> <t1986>The next time they were heard from, they had landed at Cabo de la Vela, coming from the Antilles archipelago, and a government report, broadcast by telegraph and published in jubilant proclamations throughout the country, announced the death of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1986> <t1987>But two days later, a multiple telegram, which almost caught up with the previous one, announced another rebellion in the southern plains.</t1987> <t1988>Thus began the legend of the ubiquity of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1988> <t1989>Simultaneous and contradictory reports declared him victorious in Villanueva, defeated in Guacamayal, devoured by the Motilone Indians, dead in a village in the swamp, and once again uprising in Urumita.</t1989> <t1990>The liberal leaders, who at that time were negotiating a participation in parliament, labeled him an adventurer without party representation.</t1990> <t1991>The national government classified him as a bandit and put a price of five thousand pesos on his head.</t1991> <t1992>After sixteen defeats, Colonel Aureliano Buendía left La Guajira with two thousand well-armed indigenous people, and the garrison, surprised during their sleep, abandoned Riohacha.</t1992> <t1993>There he established his headquarters and declared total war against the regime.</t1993> <t1994>The first notification he received from the government was the threat to execute Colonel Gerineldo Márquez within forty-eight hours if he did not retreat with his forces to the eastern border.</t1994> <t1995>Colonel Roque Carnicero, who was then his chief of staff, handed him the telegram with a gesture of consternation, but he read it with unpredictable joy.</t1995>
<t1996>—How wonderful! —he exclaimed—.</t1996> <t1997>We now have a telegraph in Macondo.</t1997>
<t1998>His response was definitive.</t1998> <t1999>In three months, he expected to establish his headquarters in Macondo.</t1999> <t2000>If by then he did not find Colonel Gerineldo Márquez alive, he would summarily execute all the officers he had imprisoned at that moment, starting with the generals, and would issue orders to his subordinates to proceed in the same manner until the end of the war.</t2000> <t2001>Three months later, when he entered Macondo victorious, the first embrace he received on the swamp road was from Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t2001>
<t2002>The house was full of children.</t2002> <t2003>Úrsula had taken in Santa Sofía de la Piedad, along with her eldest daughter and a pair of twins who were born five months after Arcadio's execution.</t2003> <t2004>Against the last will of the executed man, she baptized the girl with the name Remedios.</t2004> <t2005>«I am sure that this is what Arcadio meant to say», she argued.</t2005> <t2006>«We will not name her Úrsula, because one suffers a lot with that name».</t2006> <t2007>She named the twins José Arcadio Segundo and Aureliano Segundo.</t2007> <t2008>Amaranta took care of all of them.</t2008> <t2009>She placed small wooden seats in the living room and established a nursery with other children from neighboring families.</t2009> <t2010>When Colonel Aureliano Buendía returned, amidst the explosions of fireworks and the ringing of bells, a children's choir welcomed him at the house.</t2010> <t2011>Aureliano José, tall like his grandfather, dressed as a revolutionary officer, gave him military honors.</t2011>
<t2012>Not all the news was good.</t2012><t2013>A year after Colonel Aureliano Buendía's escape, José Arcadio and Rebeca went to live in the house built by Arcadio.</t2013><t2014>Nobody found out about his intervention to prevent the execution.</t2014><t2015>In the new house, located in the best corner of the square, in the shade of a privileged almond tree with three robin nests, with a large door for visitors and four windows for light, they established a hospitable home.</t2015><t2016>Rebeca's old friends, among them four Moscote sisters who remained single, resumed the embroidery sessions interrupted years before in the begonia corridor.</t2016><t2017>José Arcadio continued to enjoy the usurped lands, whose titles were recognized by the conservative government.</t2017><t2018>Every afternoon he was seen returning on horseback, with his hunting dogs and his double-barreled shotgun, and a string of rabbits hanging on the saddle.</t2018><t2019>One September afternoon, under the threat of a storm, he returned home earlier than usual.</t2019><t2020>He greeted Rebeca in the dining room, tied the dogs in the yard, hung the rabbits in the kitchen to salt them later, and went to the bedroom to change clothes.</t2020><t2021>Rebeca later declared that when her husband entered the bedroom, she locked herself in the bathroom and didn't notice anything.</t2021><t2022>It was a hard-to-believe version, but there was no more plausible one, and no one could conceive of a motive for Rebeca to murder the man who had made her happy.</t2022><t2023>That was perhaps the only mystery that was never solved in Macondo.</t2023><t2024>As soon as José Arcadio closed the bedroom door, the blast of a gunshot echoed through the house.</t2024><t2025>A thread of blood came out from under the door, crossed the living room, went out to the street, followed a direct course along the uneven sidewalks, descended steps and climbed curbs, passed by the Street of the Turks, turned a corner to the right and another to the left, turned at a right angle in front of the Buendía house, passed under the closed door, crossed the living room sticking to the walls to avoid staining the tapestries, continued through the other room, avoided the dining table in a wide curve, advanced through the begonia corridor and passed unseen under Amaranta's chair who was giving an arithmetic lesson to Aureliano José, and entered the pantry and appeared in the kitchen where Úrsula was about to crack thirty-six eggs for the bread.</t2025>
<t2026>—Hail Mary Most Pure! —shouted Úrsula.</t2026>
<t2027>He followed the trail of blood in the opposite direction, and in search of its origin, he crossed the barn, passed through the corridor of begonias where Aureliano José was singing that three and three are six and six and three are nine, and crossed the dining room and the rooms and continued in a straight line down the street, then turned right and then left until he reached the Street of the Turks, not remembering that he was still wearing his baking apron and house slippers, and went out to the square and entered a house where he had never been before, and pushed open the door of the bedroom and almost choked on the smell of burnt gunpowder, and found José Arcadio lying face down on the floor over the gaiters he had just taken off, and saw the original end of the blood trail that had stopped flowing from his right ear.</t2027> <t2028>They found no wound on his body nor could they locate the weapon.</t2028> <t2029>Nor was it possible to remove the penetrating smell of gunpowder from the corpse.</t2029> <t2030>First, they washed him three times with soap and a scrubbing brush, then they rubbed him with salt and vinegar, then with ash and lemon, and finally they put him in a barrel of lye and let him soak for six hours.</t2030> <t2031>They scrubbed him so much that the arabesques of the tattoo began to fade.</t2031> <t2032>When they conceived the desperate measure of seasoning him with pepper and cumin and bay leaves and boiling him for an entire day over a slow fire, he had already begun to decompose and they had to bury him in a hurry.</t2032> <t2033>They sealed him hermetically in a special coffin two meters and thirty centimeters long and one meter and ten centimeters wide, reinforced inside with iron plates and screwed with steel bolts, and even so, the smell was noticeable on the streets through which the funeral procession passed.</t2033> <t2034>Father Nicanor, with his liver swollen and tense like a drum, blessed him from his bed.</t2034> <t2035>Although in the following months they reinforced the tomb with superimposed walls and filled the gaps with compacted ash, sawdust, and quicklime, the cemetery continued to smell of gunpowder for many years, until the engineers of the banana company covered the grave with a concrete shell.</t2035> <t2036>As soon as they removed the corpse, Rebeca closed the doors of her house and buried herself alive, covered with a thick crust of disdain that no earthly temptation could break.</t2036> <t2037>She went out into the street on one occasion, very old, with shoes the color of antique silver and a hat with tiny flowers, around the time when the Wandering Jew passed through the town and caused such intense heat that birds broke through the window screens to die in the bedrooms.</t2037> <t2038>The last time anyone saw her alive was when she killed a thief with a precise shot as he tried to force the door of her house.</t2038> <t2039>Except for Argénida, her maid and confidante, no one had contact with her since then.</t2039> <t2040>At one time, it was known that she wrote letters to the bishop, whom she considered her first cousin, but it was never said that she received a reply.</t2040> <t2041>The town forgot her.</t2041>
<t2042>Despite his triumphant return, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was not enthusiastic about appearances.</t2042> <t2043>The government troops abandoned the plazas without resistance, and this created an illusion of victory among the liberal population that was not convenient to dispel, but the revolutionaries knew the truth, and more than anyone, Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2043> <t2044>Although at that moment he had more than five thousand men under his command and dominated two coastal states, he was aware of being cornered against the sea, and caught in such a confusing political situation that when he ordered the restoration of the church tower shattered by an army cannon shot, Father Nicanor commented from his sickbed: "This is nonsense: the defenders of the faith of Christ destroy the temple and the Masons order it to be repaired."</t2044> <t2045>Seeking an escape route, he spent hours and hours in the telegraph office, conferencing with the leaders of other plazas, and each time he came out with the more defined impression that the war was stagnant.</t2045> <t2046>When news of new liberal triumphs was received, they were proclaimed with jubilant decrees, but he measured their true extent on the maps, and understood that his forces were penetrating the jungle, defending themselves from malaria and mosquitoes, advancing in the opposite direction to reality.</t2046> <t2047>"We are wasting time," he complained to his officers.</t2047> <t2048>"We will be wasting time as long as the bastards of the party are begging for a seat in congress."</t2048> <t2049>On sleepless nights, lying on his back in the hammock that hung in the same room where he had been condemned to death, he evoked the image of the lawyers dressed in black who left the presidential palace in the icy early morning with their coat collars turned up to their ears, rubbing their hands, whispering, taking refuge in the gloomy cafes of dawn, to speculate on what the president meant when he said yes, or what he meant when he said no, and even to suppose what the president was thinking when he said something entirely different, while he swatted mosquitoes at thirty-five degrees of temperature, feeling the dreadful dawn approaching when he would have to give his men the order to throw themselves into the sea.</t2049>
<t2050>One night of uncertainty when Pilar Ternera was singing in the courtyard with the troop, he asked her to read his future in the cards.</t2050> <t2051>"Take care of your mouth," was all that Pilar Ternera could make out after spreading and gathering the cards three times.</t2051> <t2052>"I don't know what it means, but the sign is very clear: take care of your mouth."</t2052> <t2053>Two days later, someone gave an orderly a bowl of coffee without sugar, and the orderly passed it to another, and this one to another, until it went from hand to hand to Colonel Aureliano Buendía's office.</t2053> <t2054>He hadn't asked for coffee, but since it was there, the colonel drank it.</t2054> <t2055>It had enough nux vomica to kill a horse.</t2055> <t2056>When they took him home, he was stiff and arched, and his tongue was split between his teeth.</t2056> <t2057>Úrsula wrestled him from death.</t2057> <t2058>After cleaning his stomach with emetics, she wrapped him in warm blankets and gave him egg whites for two days, until his ravaged body regained its normal temperature.</t2058> <t2059>On the fourth day, he was out of danger.</t2059> <t2060>Against his will, pressured by Úrsula and the officers, he stayed in bed for another week.</t2060> <t2061>Only then did he learn that his verses had not been burned.</t2061> <t2062>"I didn't want to rush," Úrsula explained to him.</t2062> <t2063>"That night, when I was about to light the oven, I told myself it was better to wait until they brought the corpse."</t2063> <t2064>In the haze of convalescence, surrounded by Remedios's dusty dolls, Colonel Aureliano Buendía evoked in the reading of his verses the decisive moments of his existence.</t2064> <t2065>He began to write again.</t2065> <t2066>For many hours, apart from the shocks of a war without a future, he resolved his experiences on the brink of death into rhymed verses.</t2066> <t2067>Then his thoughts became so clear that he could examine them inside out.</t2067> <t2068>One night he asked Colonel Gerineldo Márquez:</t2068>
<t2069>—Tell me something, buddy: why are you fighting?</t2069>
<t2070>—Why should it be, compadre —answered Colonel Gerineldo Márquez—: for the great Liberal Party.</t2070>
<t2071>—Lucky you who knows it —he replied—.</t2071> <t2072>As for me, I am just now realizing that I am fighting out of pride.</t2072>
<t2073>—That is bad —said Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t2073>
<t2074>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was amused by his alarm.</t2074> <t2075>"Naturally," he said.</t2075> <t2076>"But in any case, it's better than not knowing why one fights."</t2076> <t2077>He looked him in the eyes and added, smiling:</t2077>
<t2078>—What is it to fight like you for something that means nothing to anyone.</t2078>
<t2079>His pride had prevented him from making contact with the armed groups in the interior of the country, as long as the party leaders did not publicly retract their statement that he was a bandit.</t2079> <t2080>He knew, however, that as soon as he set aside those scruples, he would break the vicious circle of war.</t2080> <t2081>Convalescence allowed him to reflect.</t2081> <t2082>Then he managed to get Úrsula to give him the rest of the buried inheritance and her substantial savings; he appointed Colonel Gerineldo Márquez as the civil and military chief of Macondo, and went to establish contact with the rebel groups in the interior.</t2082>
<t2083>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was not only Colonel Aureliano Buendía's most trusted man, but Úrsula also received him as a member of the family.</t2083> <t2084>Fragile, timid, with a natural good education, he was nevertheless better suited for war than for governance.</t2084> <t2085>His political advisors easily entangled him in theoretical labyrinths.</t2085> <t2086>But he managed to impose in Macondo the rural peace environment that Colonel Aureliano Buendía dreamed of to die old making little gold fishes.</t2086> <t2087>Although he lived in his parents' house, he had lunch at Úrsula's two or three times a week.</t2087> <t2088>He initiated Aureliano José in the handling of firearms, gave him premature military instruction, and for several months took him to live in the barracks, with Úrsula's consent, so that he would become a man.</t2088> <t2089>Many years before, almost a child, Gerineldo Márquez had declared his love to Amaranta.</t2089> <t2090>She was then so infatuated with her solitary passion for Pietro Crespi that she laughed at him.</t2090> <t2091>Gerineldo Márquez waited.</t2091> <t2092>On one occasion, he sent Amaranta a note from jail, asking her the favor of embroidering a dozen batiste handkerchiefs with his father's initials.</t2092> <t2093>He sent her the money.</t2093> <t2094>A week later, Amaranta brought him the dozen embroidered handkerchiefs to the jail, along with the money, and they spent several hours talking about the past.</t2094> <t2095>“When I get out of here, I will marry you,” Gerineldo Márquez said to her as he bid farewell.</t2095> <t2096>Amaranta laughed, but she continued thinking about him while teaching the children to read, and wished to revive for him her youthful passion for Pietro Crespi.</t2096> <t2097>On Saturdays, the day for visiting prisoners, she would stop by Gerineldo Márquez’s parents' house and accompany them to the jail.</t2097> <t2098>One of those Saturdays, Úrsula was surprised to see her in the kitchen, waiting for the biscuits to come out of the oven so she could choose the best ones and wrap them in a napkin she had embroidered for the occasion.</t2098>
<t2099>—Marry him —he said to her—.</t2099> <t2100>You'll hardly find another man like him.</t2100>
<t2101>Amaranta pretended a reaction of disgust.</t2101>
<t2102>—I don't need to go around hunting men —she replied—.</t2102> <t2103>I'm taking these biscuits to Gerineldo because I feel sorry for him; sooner or later they're going to shoot him.</t2103>
<t2104>He said it without thinking, but it was around that time that the government made public the threat to execute Colonel Gerineldo Márquez if the rebel forces did not surrender Riohacha.</t2104> <t2105>The visits were suspended.</t2105> <t2106>Amaranta locked herself up to cry, overwhelmed by a feeling of guilt similar to the one that tormented her when Remedios died, as if once again her thoughtless words were responsible for a death.</t2106> <t2107>Her mother consoled her.</t2107> <t2108>She assured her that Colonel Aureliano Buendía would do something to prevent the execution, and promised that she herself would take care of attracting Gerineldo Márquez, once the war was over.</t2108> <t2109>She fulfilled the promise before the expected time.</t2109> <t2110>When Gerineldo Márquez returned to the house invested with his new dignity as civil and military chief, she received him like a son, conceived exquisite flattery to retain him, and prayed with all her heart that he would remember his intention to marry Amaranta.</t2110> <t2111>Her pleas seemed certain.</t2111> <t2112>The days when he went to have lunch at the house, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez would stay the afternoon in the begonia corridor playing Chinese checkers with Amaranta.</t2112> <t2113>Úrsula would bring them coffee with milk and biscuits and take care of the children so they wouldn't be disturbed.</t2113> <t2114>Amaranta, in reality, made an effort to rekindle in her heart the forgotten ashes of her youthful passion.</t2114> <t2115>With an anxiety that became intolerable, she awaited the lunch days, the afternoons of Chinese checkers, and time flew by in the company of that warrior with a nostalgic name whose fingers trembled imperceptibly when moving the pieces.</t2115> <t2116>But the day Colonel Gerineldo Márquez reiterated his intention to marry her, she rejected him.</t2116>
<t2117>—I will not marry anyone —she told him—, but least of all you.</t2117> <t2118>You love Aureliano so much that you are going to marry me because you cannot marry him.</t2118>
<t2119>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was a patient man.</t2119> <t2120>“I will insist again,” he said.</t2120> <t2121>“Sooner or later I will convince you.”</t2121> <t2122>He continued visiting the house.</t2122> <t2123>Locked in the bedroom, biting back secret tears, Amaranta put her fingers in her ears to avoid hearing the voice of the suitor who was telling Úrsula the latest news of the war, and despite dying to see him, she found the strength not to go out to meet him.</t2123>
<t2124>Colonel Aureliano Buendía then had time to send a detailed report to Macondo every two weeks.</t2124><t2125>But only once, almost eight months after he had left, did he write to Úrsula.</t2125><t2126>A special emissary brought a sealed envelope to the house, inside of which was a paper written in the colonel's meticulous handwriting: Take good care of father because he is going to die.</t2126><t2127>Úrsula was alarmed.</t2127><t2128>"If Aureliano says so, Aureliano knows," she said.</t2128><t2129>And she asked for help to take José Arcadio Buendía to his bedroom.</t2129><t2130>Not only was he as heavy as ever, but during his prolonged stay under the chestnut tree, he had developed the ability to increase his weight voluntarily, to the point that seven men could not lift him and had to drag him to the bed.</t2130><t2131>A stench of tender mushrooms, wood flower, and ancient, concentrated exposure to the elements filled the air of the bedroom when the colossal old man, macerated by the sun and rain, began to breathe it.</t2131><t2132>The next day he was not in bed at dawn.</t2132><t2133>After searching all the rooms, Úrsula found him again under the chestnut tree.</t2133><t2134>Then they tied him to the bed.</t2134><t2135>Despite his intact strength, José Arcadio Buendía was not in a condition to fight.</t2135><t2136>Everything was the same to him.</t2136><t2137>If he returned to the chestnut tree, it was not by his will but by a habit of the body.</t2137><t2138>Úrsula took care of him, fed him, and brought him news of Aureliano.</t2138><t2139>But in reality, the only person he could have contact with for a long time was Prudencio Aguilar.</t2139><t2140>Almost pulverized by the profound decrepitude of death, Prudencio Aguilar went twice a day to talk with him.</t2140><t2141>They talked about roosters.</t2141><t2142>They promised to establish a breeding farm of magnificent animals, not so much to enjoy victories that they would no longer need, but to have something to distract themselves with on the tedious Sundays of death.</t2142><t2143>It was Prudencio Aguilar who cleaned him, fed him, and brought him splendid news of an unknown man named Aureliano who was a colonel in the war.</t2143><t2144>When he was alone, José Arcadio Buendía consoled himself with the dream of infinite rooms.</t2144><t2145>He dreamed that he got out of bed, opened the door, and passed into another identical room, with the same wrought-iron headboard bed, the same wicker chair, and the same little picture of the Virgin of Remedies on the back wall.</t2145><t2146>From that room, he passed into another exactly the same, whose door he opened to pass into another exactly the same, and then another exactly the same, to infinity.</t2146><t2147>He liked to go from room to room, as in a gallery of parallel mirrors, until Prudencio Aguilar touched his shoulder.</t2147><t2148>Then he would return <t2156>—I have come to the king's funeral.</t2156>
<t2157>Then they entered José Arcadio Buendía's room, shook him with all their might, shouted in his ear, put a mirror in front of his nostrils, but they couldn't wake him up.</t2157> <t2158>Shortly after, when the carpenter was taking measurements for the coffin, they saw through the window that a drizzle of tiny yellow flowers was falling.</t2158> <t2159>They fell all night over the town in a silent storm, covering the roofs and clogging the doors, and suffocating the animals that slept outdoors.</t2159> <t2160>So many flowers fell from the sky that the streets dawned covered with a compact blanket, and they had to clear them with shovels and rakes so that the burial could pass.</t2160>
<t2161>Sitting in the wicker rocking chair, with her work interrupted on her lap, Amaranta watched Aureliano José with his chin smeared with foam, sharpening the barber's razor on the leaf to shave for the first time.</t2161> <t2162>He nicked his shins, cut his upper lip trying to shape a mustache of blonde fuzz, and after all that, he looked the same as before, but the laborious process left Amaranta with the impression that at that moment he had begun to age.</t2162>
<t2163>—You look just like Aureliano did when he was your age —he said—.</t2163> <t2164>You're already a man.</t2164>
<t2165>It had been so for a long time, since that distant day when Amaranta believed he was still a child and continued undressing in the bathroom in front of him, as she had always done, as she had become accustomed to doing since Pilar Ternera handed him over to her to finish raising him.</t2165><t2166>The first time he saw her, the only thing that caught his attention was the deep depression in contemplation, just as her skin bristled at the touch of the water.</t2169><t2170>Since he was very young, he had the habit of leaving his hammock to wake up in Amaranta's bed, whose touch had the palliative for her loneliness.</t2176><t2177>Then they not only slept together, naked, exchanging exhausting caresses, but they chased each other around the corners of the house and locked themselves in the bedrooms at any hour, in a permanent state of unrelieved exaltation.</t2177><t2178>They were almost caught by Úrsula one afternoon when she entered the barn just as they were beginning to <t2187>Shortly thereafter, contradictory news about the war began to arrive.</t2187> <t2188>While the government itself admitted the progress of the rebellion, the officers in Macondo had confidential reports of the imminence of a negotiated peace.</t2188> <t2189>In early April, a special emissary identified himself to Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t2189> <t2190>He confirmed that, indeed, the party leaders had established contacts with rebel leaders in the interior, and were on the verge of negotiating an armistice in exchange for three ministries for the liberals, a minority representation in parliament, and a general amnesty for the rebels who laid down their arms.</t2190> <t2191>The emissary carried a highly confidential order from Colonel Aureliano Buendía, who disagreed with the terms of the armistice.</t2191> <t2192>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was to select five of his best men and prepare to leave the country with them.</t2192> <t2193>The order was carried out with the utmost secrecy.</t2193> <t2194>A week before the agreement was announced, and amid a storm of contradictory rumors, Colonel Aureliano Buendía and ten trusted officers, including Colonel Roque Carnicero, stealthily arrived in Macondo after midnight, dispersed the garrison, buried the weapons, and destroyed the records.</t2194> <t2195>By dawn, they had left the town with Colonel Gerineldo Márquez and his five officers.</t2195> <t2196>It was such a swift and confidential operation that Úrsula only learned of it at the last moment, when someone tapped on her bedroom window and whispered: "If you want to see Colonel Aureliano Buendía, look out the door right now."</t2196> <t2197>Úrsula jumped out of bed and went to the door in her nightclothes, barely catching a glimpse of the galloping horses leaving the town in a silent cloud of dust.</t2197> <t2198>It was only the next day that she learned Aureliano José had left with his father.</t2198>
<t2199>Ten days after a joint statement from the government and the opposition announced the end of the war, news arrived of the first armed uprising by Colonel Aureliano Buendía on the western border.</t2199> <t2200>His scarce and poorly armed forces were dispersed in less than a week.</t2200> <t2201>But over the course of that year, while liberals and conservatives tried to make the country believe in reconciliation, he attempted seven other uprisings.</t2201> <t2202>One night he shelled Riohacha from a schooner, and in retaliation, the garrison pulled the fourteen most well-known liberals in the town out of their beds and executed them by firing squad.</t2202> <t2203>He occupied a border customs post for more than fifteen days, and from there he issued a call to the nation for a general war.</t2203> <t2204>Another of his expeditions got lost for three months in the jungle, in a foolish attempt to cross more than fifteen hundred kilometers of virgin territories to proclaim war in the suburbs of the capital.</t2204> <t2205>On one occasion, he was less than twenty kilometers from Macondo, and was forced by government patrols to retreat into the mountains very close to the enchanted region where his father had found the fossil of a Spanish galleon many years before.</t2205>
<t2206>During that time, Visitación died.</t2206> <t2207>She had the pleasure of dying a natural death, after having renounced a throne for fear of insomnia, and her last wish was for the salary she had saved for more than twenty years to be dug up from under her bed and sent to Colonel Aureliano Buendía so he could continue the war.</t2207> <t2208>But Úrsula did not bother to retrieve that money, because at that time there were rumors that Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been killed in a landing near the provincial capital.</t2208> <t2209>The official announcement—the fourth in less than two years—was believed to be true for almost six months, as nothing more was heard of him.</t2209> <t2210>Suddenly, when Úrsula and Amaranta had already added a new mourning to the previous ones, an unusual piece of news arrived.</t2210> <t2211>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was alive, but apparently he had given up harassing the government of his country and had joined the triumphant federalism in other Caribbean republics.</t2211> <t2212>He appeared under different names, each time farther from his homeland.</t2212> <t2213>It was later learned that the idea that then motivated him was the unification of the federalist forces of Central America, to sweep away the conservative regimes from Alaska to Patagonia.</t2213> <t2214>The first direct news Úrsula received from him, several years after he had left, was a crumpled and blurry letter that reached her hand to hand from Santiago de Cuba.</t2214>
<t2215>—We have lost him forever —exclaimed Úrsula upon reading it—.</t2215> <t2216>Christmas will pass that way at the end of the world.</t2216>
<t2217>The person to whom he said it, who was the first to whom he showed the letter, was the conservative general José Raquel Moncada, mayor of Macondo since the war ended.</t2217><t2218>"This Aureliano," commented General Moncada, "it's a pity he's not a conservative."</t2218><t2219>He truly admired him.</t2219><t2220>Like many conservative civilians, José Raquel Moncada had fought the war was anti-militarist.</t2221><t2222>He considered military people to be lazy, unprincipled, intriguing, and ambitious, experts in pitting civilians against each other to thrive in disorder.</t2222><t2223>Intelligent, likable, sanguine, a man of good appetite and a fan of cockfights, he had at one point been the most formidable adversary of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2223><t2224>He managed to impose his authority over career military officers in a large sector of the coast.</t2224><t2225>Once, when he was forced by strategic conveniences to abandon a <t2246>When Amaranta saw him enter, without him having said anything, she immediately knew why he had returned.</t2246><t2247>At the table, they did not dare to look each other in the <t2268>—Can one marry an aunt? —he asked, astonished.</t2268>
<t2269>—Not only is it possible —a soldier replied to him— but we are fighting this war against the priests so that one can marry his own mother.</t2269>
<t2270>Fifteen days later he deserted.</t2270> <t2271>He found Amaranta more withered than in his memory, more melancholic and prudish, and already truly rounding the last bend of maturity, but more feverish than ever in the darkness of the bedroom and more defiant than ever in the aggressiveness of her resistance: "You are a brute," Amaranta would say, harassed by her watchdogs.</t2271> <t2272>"It's not true that one can do this to a poor aunt, unless with a special dispensation from the Pope."</t2272> <t2273>Aureliano José promised to go to Rome, promised to travel across Europe on his knees, and kiss the sandals of the Supreme Pontiff just so she would lower her drawbridges.</t2273>

<t2278>One early morning, overcome by the unbearable pain of repressed manhood, he went to Catarino's store.</t2278> <t2279>He found a woman with flaccid breasts, affectionate and cheap, who soothed his belly for a while.</t2279> <t2280>He tried to apply the treatment of disdain to Amaranta.</t2280> <t2281>He saw her in the corridor, sewing on a hand-crank machine that she had learned to operate with admirable skill, and he didn't even speak to her.</t2281> <t2282>Amaranta felt liberated from a burden, and she herself didn't understand why she then began to think of Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, why she nostalgically recalled the afternoons of Chinese checkers, and why she even came to desire him as a bedroom man.</t2282> <t2283>Aureliano José did not realize how much ground he had lost, the night he could no longer resist the pretense of indifference, and returned to Amaranta's room.</t2283> <t2284>She rejected him with inflexible, unequivocal determination, and forever bolted the bedroom door.</t2284>
<t2285>A few months after Aureliano José's return, an exuberant woman, scented with jasmine, arrived at the house with a child of about five years old.</t2285> <t2286>She claimed that he was the son of Colonel Aureliano Buendía and had brought him for Úrsula to baptize.</t2286> <t2287>No one doubted the origin of that nameless child: he was just like the colonel when they took him to see the ice.</t2287> <t2288>The woman said that he was born with his eyes open, looking at people with the judgment of an adult, and that his way of staring at things without blinking frightened her.</t2288> <t2289>"He is identical," said Úrsula.</t2289> <t2290>"The only thing missing is for him to make the chairs move just by looking at them."</t2290> <t2291>They baptized him with the name Aureliano, and with his mother's surname, because the law did not allow him to carry his father's surname unless he recognized him.</t2291> <t2292>General Moncada served as godfather.</t2292> <t2293>Although Amaranta insisted on keeping him to finish raising him, the mother refused.</t2293>
<t2294>Úrsula was unaware at the time of the custom of sending maidens to the bedrooms of warriors, much like fine roosters are given hens, but over the course of that year, she found out: nine more <t2309>—Don't worry, comadre —said General Moncada enigmatically—.</t2309> <t2310>He will come sooner than you imagine.</t2310>
<t2311>What General Moncada knew, and did not want to reveal at lunch, was that Colonel Aureliano Buendía was already on his way to lead the longest, most radical, and bloodiest rebellion attempted until then.</t2311>
<t2312>The situation became as tense as it was in the months leading up to the first war.</t2312> <t2313>The cockfights, encouraged by the mayor himself, were suspended.</t2313> <t2314>Captain Aquiles <t2335>—I am happy knowing that people are happy in bed.</t2335>
<t2336>She never charged for the service.</t2336> <t2337>She never refused a favor, just as she did not refuse it to the countless men who sought her even in the twilight of her maturity, without providing her with money or love, and only sometimes pleasure.</t2337> <t2338>Her five daughters, inheritors of a burning seed, lost themselves in the twists and turns of life since adolescence.</t2338> <t2339>Of the two sons she managed to raise, one died fighting in the ranks of Colonel Aureliano Buendía and the other was wounded and captured at fourteen, when he tried to steal a crate of chickens in a swamp village.</t2339> <t2340>In a way, Aureliano José was the tall, dark man who for half a century announced the king of cups to her, and like all the messengers of the cards, he reached her heart when it was already marked by the sign of death.</t2340> <t2341>She saw him in the cards.</t2341>
<t2342>—Don't go out tonight —he told her—.</t2342> <t2343>Stay here and sleep, because Carmelita Montiel has been begging me to let her into your room.</t2343>
<t2344>Aureliano José did not grasp the profound sense of supplication that offer had.</t2344>
<t2345>—Tell him to wait for me at midnight —he said.</t2345>

<t2354>—Bastards! —he managed to shout—.</t2354> <t2355>I wish I were Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2355>
<t2356>Carmelita Montiel, a twenty-year-old virgin, had just bathed with orange blossom water and was sprinkling rosemary leaves on Pilar Ternera's bed when the shot rang out.</t2356> <t2357>Aureliano José was destined to find with her the happiness that Amaranta had denied him, to have seven children, and to die of old age in her arms, but the rifle bullet that entered through his back and shattered his chest was directed by a misinterpretation of the cards.</t2357> <t2358>Captain Aquiles Ricardo, who was actually the one destined to die that night, indeed died four hours before Aureliano José.</t2358> <t2359>As soon as the shot rang out, he was struck down by two simultaneous bullets, the origin of which was never established, and a multitude of cries shook the night.</t2359>
<t2360>—Long live the Liberal Party!</t2360> <t2361>Long live Colonel Aureliano Buendía!</t2361>
<t2362>At twelve o'clock, when Aureliano José had finished bleeding out and Carmelita Montiel found the cards of her future blank, more than four hundred men had marched in front of the theater and had unloaded their revolvers into the abandoned corpse of Captain Aquiles Ricardo.</t2362> <t2363>A patrol was needed to put the lead-laden body, which was falling apart like a soaked loaf of bread, into a wheelbarrow.</t2363>
<t2364>Upset by the impertinences of the regular army, General José Raquel Moncada mobilized his political influences, donned the uniform again, and assumed the civil and military leadership of Macondo.</t2364> <t2365>He did not expect, however, that his conciliatory attitude could prevent the inevitable.</t2365> <t2366>The news in September was contradictory.</t2366> <t2367>While the government announced that it maintained control throughout the country, the liberals received secret reports of armed uprisings in the interior.</t2367> <t2368>The regime did not admit the state of war until it was proclaimed in a decree that Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been court-martialed in absentia and sentenced to death.</t2368> <t2369>The order was to carry out the sentence by the first garrison that captured him.</t2369> <t2370>"This means he has returned," Úrsula rejoiced before General Moncada.</t2370> <t2371>But he himself was unaware of it.</t2371>
<t2372>In reality, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been in the country for more than a month.</t2372> <t2373>Preceded by contradictory rumors, supposedly in the most remote places at the same time, General Moncada himself did not believe in his return until it was officially announced that he had taken over two coastal states.</t2373> <t2374>“I congratulate you, comadre,” he said to Úrsula, showing her the telegram.</t2374> <t2375>“You will have him here very soon.”</t2375> <t2376>Úrsula then became worried for the first time.</t2376> <t2377>“And what will you do, compadre?” she asked.</t2377> <t2378>General Moncada had asked himself that question many times.</t2378>
<t2379>—The same as him, comadre —he replied—: to fulfill my duty.</t2379>
<t2380>On the first of October, at dawn, Colonel Aureliano Buendía with a thousand well-armed men attacked Macondo, and the garrison received the order to resist until the end.</t2380> <t2381>At noon, while General Moncada was having lunch with Úrsula, a rebel cannon shot that echoed throughout the town pulverized the facade of the municipal treasury.</t2381> <t2382>«They are as well-armed as we are,» sighed General Moncada, «but they also fight with more determination.»</t2382> <t2383>At two in the afternoon, while the ground trembled with cannon shots from both sides, he said goodbye to Úrsula with the certainty that he was fighting a lost battle.</t2383>
<t2384>—I pray to God that Aureliano is not in the house tonight —he said—.</t2384> <t2385>If he is, give him a hug from me, because I do not expect to see him ever again.</t2385>
<t2386>That night he was captured while trying to escape from Macondo, after writing an extensive letter to Colonel Aureliano Buendía, in which he reminded him of their common goals of humanizing the <t2408>In the dim light of the house, the lonely widow who was once the confidante of his repressed loves, and whose obstinacy saved his life, was a specter of the past.</t2408> <t2409>Clad in black up to her wrists, with a heart turned to ashes, she barely had any news of the war.</t2409> <t2410>Colonel Aureliano Buendía had the impression that the phosphorescence of her bones pierced her skin, and that she moved through an atmosphere of will-o'-the-wisps, in stagnant air where a faint smell of gunpowder still lingered.</t2410> <t2411>He began by advising her to moderate the rigor of her mourning, to air out the house, to forgive the world for the death of José Arcadio.</t2411> <t2412>But Rebeca was already beyond all vanity.</t2412> <t2413>After searching for her in vain in the taste of the earth, in the perfumed letters of Pietro Crespi, in the tempestuous bed of her husband, she had found peace in that house where memories materialized by the force of relentless evocation, and wandered like human beings through the closed rooms.</t2413> <t2414>Stretched out in her wicker rocker, looking at Colonel Aureliano Buendía as if he were the one who seemed like a specter of the past, Rebeca was not even moved by the news that the lands usurped by José Arcadio would be returned to their rightful owners.</t2414>
<t2415>—It will be done as you decide, Aureliano —she sighed—.</t2415> <t2416>I always believed, and I confirm it now, that you are a renegade.</t2416>
<t2417>The review of property titles was completed at the same time as the summary trials, presided over by Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, which concluded with the execution by firing squad of all the regular army officers who were prisoners of the revolutionaries.</t2417> <t2418>The last court-martial was that of General José Raquel Moncada.</t2418> <t2419>Úrsula intervened.</t2419> <t2420>“He is the best ruler we have had in Macondo,” she told Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2420> <t2421>“I don’t even need to tell you about his good heart, about the affection he has for us, because you know him better than anyone.”</t2421> <t2422>Colonel Aureliano Buendía fixed a reproachful gaze on her:</t2422>
