<t2425>Úrsula not only did it, but she also brought in all the mothers of the revolutionary officers living in Macondo to testify.</t2425> <t2426>One by one, the old founders of the town, several of whom had participated in the daring crossing of the mountains, extolled the virtues of General Moncada.</t2426> <t2427>Úrsula was the last in the parade.</t2427> <t2428>Her mournful dignity, the weight of her name, the convincing vehemence of her statement momentarily shook the balance of justice.</t2428> <t2429>“You have taken this dreadful game very seriously, and you have done well, because you are fulfilling your duty,” she said to the members of the tribunal.</t2429> <t2430>“But do not forget that as long as God gives us life, we will continue to be mothers, and no matter how revolutionary you are, we have the right to pull down your pants and give you a whipping at the first sign of disrespect.”</t2430> <t2431>The jury withdrew to deliberate while these words still echoed in the converted school-turned-barracks.</t2431> <t2432>At midnight, General José Raquel Moncada was sentenced to death.</t2432> <t2433>Colonel Aureliano Buendía, despite Úrsula's violent recriminations, refused to commute the sentence.</t2433> <t2434>Shortly before dawn, he visited the condemned man in the stockade room.</t2434>
<t2435>—Remember, compadre —he said to him—, that it is not I who am executing you.</t2435> <t2436>The revolution is executing you.</t2436>
<t2437>The general Moncada didn't even get up from the cot when he saw him enter.</t2437>
<t2438>—Go to hell, buddy —he replied.</t2438>
<t2439>Until that moment, since his return, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had not allowed himself the opportunity to see him with his heart.</t2439> <t2440>He was astonished at how much he had aged, at the tremor in his hands, at the somewhat routine acceptance with which he awaited death, and then he experienced a deep contempt for himself that he confused with a beginning of mercy.</t2440>
<t2441>—You know better than I do —he said— that every court-martial is a farce, and that you truly have to pay for the crimes of others, because this time we are going to win the war at any cost.</t2441> <t2442>If you were in my place, wouldn't you have done the same?</t2442>
<t2443>The general Moncada stood up to clean his thick tortoiseshell glasses with the tail of his shirt.</t2443> <t2444>"Probably," he said.</t2444> <t2445>"But what worries me is not that you will shoot me, because after all, for people like us this is the natural death."</t2445> <t2446>He placed the glasses on the bed and took off his pocket watch.</t2446> <t2447>"What worries me," he added, "is that from hating the military so much, from fighting them so much, from thinking about them so much, you have ended up being just like them.</t2447> <t2448>And there is no ideal in life that deserves such abjection."</t2448> <t2449>He took off his wedding ring and the medal of the Virgin of Remedies and placed them along with the glasses and the watch.</t2449>
<t2450>—At this rate —he concluded— not only will you become the most despotic and bloodthirsty dictator in our history, but you will also execute my friend Úrsula in an attempt to appease your conscience.</t2450>
<t2451>Colonel Aureliano Buendía remained impassive.</t2451> <t2452>General Moncada then handed him the glasses, the medal, the watch, and the ring, and changed his tone.</t2452>

<t2459>When he stepped into the blue misty air, his face became moist as it had in another dawn of the past, and only then did he understand why he had arranged for the sentence to be carried out in the courtyard, and not at the cemetery wall.</t2459> <t2460>The squad, formed up in front of the door, gave him the honors of a head of state.</t2460>
<t2461>—You can bring him now —he ordered.</t2461>
<t2462>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was the first to perceive the emptiness of the war.</t2462> <t2463>In his capacity as the civil and military chief of Macondo, he held telegraphic conversations twice a week with Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2463> <t2464>At the beginning, those interviews determined the course of a flesh-and-blood war whose perfectly defined contours allowed one to establish at any moment the exact point it was at, and to foresee its future directions.</t2464> <t2465>Although he never allowed himself to be drawn into confidences, not even by his closest friends, Colonel Aureliano Buendía then maintained the familiar tone that allowed him to be identified at the other end of the line.</t2465> <t2466>Many times he prolonged the conversations beyond the scheduled end and let them drift into comments of a domestic nature.</t2466> <t2467>Little by little, however, and as the war intensified and spread, his image began to fade into a universe of unreality.</t2467> <t2468>The dots and dashes of his voice became increasingly remote and uncertain, and they joined and combined to form words that gradually lost all meaning.</t2468> <t2469>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez then limited himself to listening, overwhelmed by the impression of being in telegraphic contact with a stranger from another world.</t2469>
<t2470>—Understood, Aureliano —he concluded on the manipulator—.</t2470> <t2471>Long live the Liberal Party!</t2471>
<t2472>He ended up losing all contact with the war.</t2472> <t2473>What had once been a real activity, an irresistible passion of his youth, became for him a remote reference: a void.</t2473> <t2474>His only refuge was Amaranta's sewing room.</t2474> <t2475>He visited her every afternoon.</t2475> <t2476>He liked to watch her hands while she curled foam lace on the hand-cranked machine that Remedios the Beauty would turn.</t2476> <t2477>They would spend many hours without speaking, content with each other's company, but while Amaranta secretly delighted in keeping the fire of his devotion alive, he was unaware of the secret designs of that inscrutable heart.</t2477> <t2478>When the news of his return became known, Amaranta had been overwhelmed with anxiety.</t2478> <t2479>But when she saw him enter the house, mingled with the noisy escort of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, and saw him battered by the harshness of exile, aged by time and forgetfulness, dirty with sweat and dust, smelling of livestock, ugly, with his left arm in a sling, she felt faint with disappointment.</t2479> <t2480>"My God," she thought, "this is not the one I was waiting for."</t2480> <t2481>The next day, however, he returned to the house shaved and clean, with his mustache scented with lavender water and without the bloodstained sling.</t2481> <t2482>He brought her a breviary with mother-of-pearl covers.</t2482>
<t2483>—How strange men are —she said, because she couldn't find anything else to say—.</t2483> <t2484>They spend their lives fighting against priests and give away prayer books.</t2484>
<t2485>Since then, even on the most critical days of the war, he visited her every afternoon.</t2485> <t2486>Many times, when Remedios the Beauty was not present, it was he who turned the wheel of the sewing machine.</t2486> <t2487>Amaranta felt disturbed by the perseverance, loyalty, and submission of that man vested with so much authority, who nevertheless disarmed himself in the living room to enter defenseless into the sewing room.</t2487> <t2488>But for four years he reiterated his love, and she always found a way to reject him without hurting him, because although she couldn't love him, she could no longer live without him.</t2488> <t2489>Remedios the Beauty, who seemed indifferent to everything and was thought to be mentally retarded, was not insensitive to such devotion and intervened in favor of Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t2489> <t2490>Amaranta suddenly discovered that the girl she had raised, who was just entering adolescence, was already the most beautiful creature ever seen in Macondo.</t2490> <t2491>She felt the resurgence in her heart of the resentment she once felt against Rebeca, and praying to God not to be driven to the extent of wishing her death, she banished her from the sewing room.</t2491> <t2492>It was around that time that Colonel Gerineldo Márquez began to feel the weariness of the war.</t2492> <t2493>He appealed to his reserves of persuasion, to his immense and repressed tenderness, willing to renounce for Amaranta a glory that had cost him the sacrifice of his best years.</t2493> <t2494>But he could not convince her.</t2494> <t2495>One August afternoon, overwhelmed by the unbearable weight of her own obstinacy, Amaranta locked herself in the bedroom to cry her loneliness to death, after giving her persistent suitor the final answer.</t2495>
<t2496>—Let's forget about each other forever —he said to her—, we are too old for these things now.</t2496>
<t2497>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez attended that afternoon to a telegraphic call from Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2497> <t2498>It was a routine conversation that would not open any breach in the stagnant war.</t2498> <t2499>At the end, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez contemplated the desolate streets, the crystallized water on the almond trees, and found himself lost in solitude.</t2499>
<t2500>—Aureliano —he said sadly on the manipulator—, it's raining in Macondo.</t2500>
<t2501>There was a long silence on the line.</t2501> <t2502>Suddenly, the devices jumped with the ruthless signs of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2502>
<t2503>—Don't be an idiot, Gerineldo —said the signs—.</t2503> <t2504>It's natural for it to be raining in August.</t2504>
<t2505>They had gone so long without seeing each other that Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was taken aback by the aggressiveness of that reaction.</t2505> <t2506>However, two months later, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía returned to Macondo, the bewilderment turned into astonishment.</t2506> <t2507>Even Úrsula was surprised at how much he had changed.</t2507> <t2508>He arrived quietly, without an escort, wrapped in a blanket despite the heat, and with three lovers whom he installed in the same house, where he spent most of his time lying in a hammock.</t2508> <t2509>He barely read the telegraphic dispatches that reported routine operations.</t2509> <t2510>On one occasion, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez asked him for instructions on the evacuation of a border town that threatened to become an international conflict.</t2510>
<t2511>—Don't bother me with trivialities —he ordered—.</t2511> <t2512>Consult it with Divine Providence.</t2512>
<t2513>It was perhaps the most critical moment of the war.</t2513> <t2514>The liberal landowners, who initially supported the revolution, had entered into secret alliances with the conservative landowners to prevent the review of property titles.</t2514> <t2515>The politicians who were capitalizing on the war from exile had publicly repudiated the drastic determinations of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, but even that disavowal seemed not to concern him.</t2515> <t2516>He had not returned to reading his verses, which occupied more than five volumes and remained forgotten at the bottom of the trunk.</t2516> <t2517>At night, or during siesta time, he would call one of his women to the hammock and obtain from her a rudimentary satisfaction, and then he would sleep with a stone-like slumber that was not disturbed by the slightest hint of worry.</t2517> <t2518>Only he knew then that his bewildered heart was forever condemned to uncertainty.</t2518> <t2519>At the beginning, intoxicated by the glory of his return, by the unbelievable victories, he had peered into the abyss of greatness.</t2519> <t2520>He took pleasure in keeping the Duke of Marlborough, his great master in the arts of war, at his right hand, whose attire of furs and tiger claws elicited the respect of adults and the astonishment of children.</t2520> <t2521>It was then that he decided that no human being, not even Úrsula, should approach him within three meters.</t2521> <t2522>In the center of the chalk circle that his aides would draw wherever he arrived, and into which only he could enter, he would decide the fate of the world with brief and unappealable orders.</t2522> <t2523>The first time he was in Manaure after the execution of General Moncada, he hastened to fulfill his victim's last wish, and the widow received the glasses, the medal, the watch, and the ring, but she did not allow him to go beyond the door.</t2523>
<t2524>—Don't come in, Colonel —he said to him—.</t2524> <t2525>You may command in your war, but I command in my house.</t2525>
<t2526>Colonel Aureliano Buendía showed no signs of resentment, but his spirit only found peace when his personal guard looted and burned the widow's house to ashes.</t2526> <t2527>"Take care of your heart, Aureliano," Colonel Gerineldo Márquez would say to him then.</t2527> <t2528>"You're rotting alive."</t2528> <t2529>During that time, he convened a second assembly of the main rebel commanders.</t2529> <t2530>He found all sorts: idealists, ambitious men, adventurers, social resentfuls, and even common criminals.</t2530> <t2531>There was even a former conservative official who had taken refuge in the revolt to escape a trial for embezzlement.</t2531> <t2532>Many didn't even know why they were fighting.</t2532> <t2533>Amid that motley crowd, whose differences in opinion were on the verge of causing an internal explosion, a sinister authority stood out: General Teófilo Vargas.</t2533> <t2534>He was a pure, wild, illiterate Indian, endowed with a taciturn malice and a messianic vocation that incited a mad fanaticism in his men.</t2534> <t2535>Colonel Aureliano Buendía organized the meeting with the purpose of unifying the rebel command against the maneuvers of the politicians.</t2535> <t2536>General Teófilo Vargas preempted his intentions: in a few hours, he dismantled the coalition of the best-qualified commanders and seized central command.</t2536> <t2537>"He's a beast to be wary of," Colonel Aureliano Buendía told his officers.</t2537> <t2538>"For us, that man is more dangerous than the Minister of War."</t2538> <t2539>Then a very young captain, who had always been distinguished by his shyness, raised a cautious index finger.</t2539>
<t2540>—It's very simple, Colonel —he proposed—: we have to kill him.</t2540>

<t2542>—Don't expect me to give that order —he said.</t2542>

felt scattered, repeated, and more solitary than ever.</t2553> <t2554>He was convinced that his own officers were lying to him.</t2554> <t2555>He quarreled with the Duke of Marlborough.</t2555> <t2556>"The best friend," he used to say then, "is the one who has just died."</t2556> <t2557>He grew tired of the uncertainty, the vicious circle of that eternal war that always found him in the same place, only each time older, more worn out, more without knowing why, or how, or until when.</t2557> <t2558>There was always someone outside the chalk circle.</t2558> <t2559>Someone who needed money, <t2563>—Take them to the whores —he said.</t2563>
<t2564>They were six lawyers in frock coats and top hats who endured the fierce November sun with hard stoicism.</t2564> <t2565>Úrsula hosted them in the house.</t2565> <t2566>They spent most of the day locked in the bedroom in hermetic consultations, and at nightfall, they would ask for an escort and a set of accordions and take over Catarino's store.</t2566> <t2567>"Do not bother them," ordered Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2567> <t2568>"After all, I know what they want."</t2568> <t2569>At the beginning of December, the long-awaited interview, which many had predicted would be an endless discussion, was resolved in less than an hour.</t2569>

<t2575>—It means —smiled Colonel Aureliano Buendía when he finished reading— that we are only fighting for power.</t2575>
<t2576>—They are tactical reforms —replied one of the delegates—.</t2576> <t2577>For now, the essential thing is to broaden the popular base of the war.</t2577> <t2578>We will see afterwards.</t2578>
<t2579>One of Colonel Aureliano Buendía's political advisors hurried to intervene.</t2579>
<t2580>—It is a contradiction —he said—.</t2580> <t2581>If these reforms are good, it means that the conservative regime is good.</t2581> <t2582>If with them we will manage to broaden the popular base of the war, as you say, it means that the regime has a wide popular base.</t2582> <t2583>It means, in summary, that for almost twenty years we have been fighting against the sentiments of the nation.</t2583>
<t2584>He was going to continue, but Colonel Aureliano Buendía interrupted him with a gesture.</t2584> <t2585>"Don't waste your time, doctor," he said.</t2585> <t2586>"The important thing is that from this moment on, we only fight for power."</t2586> <t2587>Without stopping his smile, he took the documents handed to him by the delegates and prepared to sign.</t2587>
<t2588>—Since that is the case —he concluded—, we have no objection to accepting.</t2588>
<t2589>His men looked at each other in dismay.</t2589>
<t2590>—Excuse me, Colonel —said Colonel Gerineldo Márquez softly—, but this is a betrayal.</t2590>
<t2591>Colonel Aureliano Buendía halted the inked pen in mid-air and unleashed the full weight of his authority upon him.</t2591>
<t2592>—Hand over your weapons —he ordered.</t2592>
<t2593>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez stood up and placed the weapons on the table.</t2593>
<t2594>—Present yourself at the barracks —ordered Colonel Aureliano Buendía—.</t2594> <t2595>You are at the disposal of the revolutionary courts.</t2595>
<t2596>Then he signed the declaration and handed the documents to the emissaries, saying to them:</t2596>
<t2597>—Gentlemen, here are your papers.</t2597> <t2598>Make good use of them.</t2598>
<t2599>Two days later, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, accused of high treason, was sentenced to death.</t2599> <t2600>Collapsed in his hammock, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was indifferent to the pleas for clemency.</t2600> <t2601>The day before the execution, disobeying the order not to disturb him, Úrsula visited him in the bedroom.</t2601> <t2602>Dressed in black, invested with a rare solemnity, she stood for the three minutes of the interview.</t2602> <t2603>"I know you will execute Gerineldo," she said calmly, "and I can do nothing to prevent it.</t2603> <t2604>But I warn you of one thing: as soon as I see the corpse, I swear by the bones of my father and mother, by the memory of José Arcadio Buendía, I swear before God, that I will drag you out from wherever you hide and I will kill you with my own hands."</t2604> <t2605>Before leaving the room, without waiting for any reply, she concluded:</t2605>
<t2606>—It's the same thing I would have done if you had been born with a pig's tail.</t2606>
<t2607>That endless night, while Colonel Gerineldo Márquez recalled his dead afternoons in Amaranta's sewing room, Colonel Aureliano Buendía scratched for many hours, trying to break it, the hard shell of his solitude.</t2607> <t2608>His only happy moments, since the distant afternoon when his father took him to see the ice, had passed in the silver workshop, where he spent his time assembling little gold fish.</t2608> <t2609>He had had to wage thirty-two wars, and had had to break all his pacts with death and wallow like a pig in the mire of glory, to discover almost forty years late the privileges of simplicity.</t2609>
<t2610>At dawn, ravaged by the stormy vigil, he appeared in the stocks room an hour before the execution.</t2610> <t2611>"The farce is over, compadre," he said to Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t2611> <t2612>"Let's get out of here before the mosquitoes finish you off."</t2612> <t2613>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez could not repress the contempt that this attitude inspired in him.</t2613>
<t2614>—No, Aureliano —he replied—.</t2614> <t2615>It's better to be dead than to see you turned into a bully.</t2615>
<t2616>—You won't see me —said Colonel Aureliano Buendía—.</t2616> <t2617>Put on your shoes and help me finish this shitty war.</t2617>
<t2618>When he said it, he did not imagine that it was easier to start a war than to end one.</t2618> <t2619>It took almost a year of bloody rigor to force the government to propose peace conditions favorable to the rebels, and another year to persuade his supporters of the convenience of accepting them.</t2619> <t2620>He reached inconceivable extremes of cruelty to quell the rebellions of his own officers, who resisted giving up the victory, and ended up relying on enemy forces to finally subdue them.</t2620>
<t2621>Never was there a better warrior than then.</t2621> <t2622>The certainty that he was finally fighting for his own liberation, and not for abstract ideals, for slogans that politicians could twist and turn according to circumstances, infused him with a fiery enthusiasm.</t2622> <t2623>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, who fought for failure with as much conviction and loyalty as he had previously fought for victory, reproached him for his useless recklessness.</t2623> <t2624>"Don't worry," he smiled.</t2624> <t2625>"Dying is much harder than one thinks."</t2625> <t2626>In his case, it was true.</t2626> <t2627>The certainty that his day was marked endowed him with a mysterious immunity, a fixed-term immortality that made him invulnerable to the risks of war, and finally allowed him to achieve a defeat that was much more difficult, much bloodier, and costlier than victory.</t2627>
<t2628>In almost twenty years of war, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been in the house many times, but the state of urgency in which he always arrived, the military apparatus that accompanied him everywhere, the aura of legend that gilded his presence and to which even Úrsula herself was not insensitive, ended up making him a stranger.</t2628> <t2629>The last time he was in Macondo, and took a house for his three concubines, he was seen in his own home only two or three times, when he had time to accept invitations to eat.</t2629> <t2630>Remedios, the beautiful, and the twins, born in the midst of the war, barely knew him.</t2630> <t2631>Amaranta could not reconcile the image of the brother who spent his adolescence making little gold fishes with that of the mythical warrior who had placed a distance of three meters between himself and the rest of humanity.</t2631> <t2632>But when the proximity of the armistice became known and it was thought that he was returning once again as a human being, finally rescued for the hearts of his own, the family affections dormant for so long were reborn with more strength than ever.</t2632>
<t2633>—At last —said Úrsula— we will have a man in the house again.</t2633>
<t2634>Amaranta was the first to suspect that they had lost him forever.</t2634> <t2635>A week before the armistice, when he entered the house without an escort, preceded by two barefoot orderlies who deposited the mule's gear and the trunk of verses, the only remnant of his former imperial baggage, she saw him pass in front of the sewing room and called him.</t2635> <t2636>Colonel Aureliano Buendía seemed to have difficulty recognizing her.</t2636>
<t2637>—I am Amaranta —she said in good spirits, happy about her return, and showed him the hand with the black bandage—.</t2637> <t2638>Look.</t2638>
<t2639>Colonel Aureliano Buendía gave her the same smile as the first time he saw her with the bandage, that distant morning when he returned to Macondo sentenced to death.</t2639>
<t2640>—What a horror —he said—, how time flies!</t2640>
<t2641>The regular army had to protect the house.</t2641> <t2642>He arrived harassed, spat upon, accused of having intensified the war only to sell it at a higher price.</t2642> <t2643>He was trembling with fever and cold and once again had his armpits full of boils.</t2643> <t2644>Six months earlier, when she heard about the armistice, Úrsula had opened and swept the bridal chamber, and had burned myrrh in the corners, thinking that he would return ready to grow old slowly among Remedios's moldy dolls.</t2644> <t2645>But in reality, in the last two years, he had paid his final dues to life, including that of aging.</t2645> <t2646>As he passed in front of the silver workshop, which Úrsula had prepared with special diligence, he did not even notice that the keys were in the lock.</t2646> <t2647>He did not perceive the tiny and heartbreaking damages that time had done to the house, and that after such a prolonged absence would have seemed a disaster to any man who kept his memories alive.</t2647> <t2648>The peeling lime on the walls did not hurt him, nor the dirty cottons of cobwebs in the corners, nor the dust on the begonias, nor the termite veins in the beams, nor the moss on the doorframes, nor any of the insidious traps that nostalgia laid for him.</t2648> <t2649>He sat in the corridor, wrapped in the blanket and without taking off his boots, as if just waiting for the rain to stop, and spent the whole afternoon watching it rain over the begonias.</t2649> <t2650>Úrsula then understood that she would not have him in the house for long.</t2650> <t2651>"If it's not the war," she thought, "it can only be death."</t2651> <t2652>It was such a clear, convincing supposition that she identified it as a premonition.</t2652>
<t2653>That night, at dinner, the supposed Aureliano Segundo crumbled the bread with his right hand and took the soup with his left.</t2653> <t2654>His twin brother, the supposed José Arcadio Segundo, crumbled the bread with his left hand and took the soup with his right.</t2654> <t2655>The coordination of their movements was so precise that they did not seem like two brothers sitting across from each other, but rather an artifice of mirrors.</t2655> <t2656>The spectacle that the twins had conceived since they became aware of being identical was repeated in honor of the newcomer.</t2656> <t2657>But Colonel Aureliano Buendía did not notice it.</t2657> <t2658>He seemed so oblivious to everything that he didn't even notice Remedios the Beauty, who walked naked towards the bedroom.</t2658> <t2659>Úrsula was the only one who dared to disturb his abstraction.</t2659>
<t2660>—If you have to leave again —he told her halfway through dinner—, at least try to remember how we were tonight.</t2660>
<t2661>Then Colonel Aureliano Buendía realized, without surprise, that Úrsula was the only human being who had managed to unravel his misery, and for the first time in many years, he dared to look her in the face.</t2661> <t2662>She had cracked skin, decayed teeth, withered and colorless hair, and an astonished look.</t2662> <t2663>He compared her with the oldest memory he had of her, the afternoon when he had the premonition that a pot of boiling broth was going to fall off the table, and he found her shattered.</t2663> <t2664>In an instant, he discovered the scratches, welts, bruises, ulcers, and scars that more than half a century of daily life had left on her, and he confirmed that these ravages did not even evoke a feeling of pity in him.</t2664> <t2665>He then made one last effort to search in his heart for the place where his affections had rotted away, and he could not find it.</t2665> <t2666>In another time, at least he experienced a confused feeling of shame when he detected Úrsula's smell on his own skin, and on more than one occasion, he felt his thoughts interfered by hers.</t2666> <t2667>But all that had been swept away by the war.</t2667> <t2668>Even Remedios, his wife, was at that moment the blurred image of someone who could have been his daughter.</t2668> <t2669>The countless women he had known in the desert of love, and who had scattered his seed all along the coast, had left no trace in his feelings.</t2669> <t2670>Most of them entered the room in the dark and left before dawn, and the next day they were barely a bit of tedium in his bodily memory.</t2670> <t2671>The only affection that prevailed against time and war was the one he felt for his brother José Arcadio when they were both children, and it was not founded on love, but on complicity.</t2671>
<t2672>—Excuse me —he apologized at Úrsula's request—.</t2672> <t2673>It's just that this war has ended everything.</t2673>
<t2674>In the following days, he occupied himself with destroying every trace of his passage through the world.</t2674> <t2675>He simplified the silversmith workshop until only impersonal objects were left, gave his clothes to the orderlies, and buried his weapons in the yard with the same sense of penance with which his father buried the spear that killed Prudencio Aguilar.</t2675> <t2676>He only kept one pistol, and with a single bullet.</t2676> <t2677>Úrsula did not intervene.</t2677> <t2678>The only time she dissuaded him was when he was about to destroy the daguerreotype of Remedios that was kept in the living room, illuminated by an eternal lamp.</t2678> <t2679>"That portrait stopped belonging to you a long time ago," she told him.</t2679> <t2680>"It is a family relic."</t2680> <t2681>On the eve of the armistice, when there was not a single object left in the house that could remind anyone of him, he took the trunk with the verses to the bakery at the moment when Santa Sofía de la Piedad was preparing to light the oven.</t2681>
<t2682>—Light it with this —he said, handing him the first roll of yellowed papers—.</t2682> <t2683>It burns better because they are very old things.</t2683>
<t2684>Santa Sofía de la Piedad, the silent one, the accommodating one, the one who never contradicted even her own children, had the impression that this was a forbidden act.</t2684>
<t2685>—They are important papers —he said.</t2685>
<t2686>—None of that —said the colonel—.</t2686> <t2687>These are things that are written for oneself.</t2687>
<t2688>—Then —she said— burn them yourself, Colonel.</t2688>
<t2689>Not only did he do it, but he also smashed the trunk with a hatchet and threw the splinters into the fire.</t2689> <t2690>Hours earlier, Pilar Ternera had come to visit him.</t2690> <t2691>After so many years of not seeing her, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was astonished at how much she had aged and gained weight, and how much the splendor of her laughter had faded, but he was also amazed at the depth she had achieved in reading the cards.</t2691> <t2692>"Take care of your mouth," she told him, and he wondered if the last time she said it, at the height of his glory, it had been a surprisingly foresighted vision of his destiny.</t2692> <t2693>Shortly after, when his personal doctor finished removing the boils, he asked him without showing particular interest where exactly the heart was located.</t2693> <t2694>The doctor examined him and then drew a circle on his chest with a cotton swab stained with iodine.</t2694>
<t2695>The armistice Tuesday dawned warm and rainy.</t2695> <t2696>Colonel Aureliano Buendía appeared in the kitchen before five and took his usual coffee without sugar.</t2696> <t2697>“A day like this you came into the world,” Úrsula told him.</t2697> <t2698>“Everyone was scared by your open eyes.”</t2698> <t2699>He paid no attention to her, because he was focused on the troop preparations, the bugle calls, and the commands that spoiled the dawn.</t2699> <t2700>Although after so many years of war they should have seemed familiar to him, this time he experienced the same weakness in his knees and the same tingling of the skin that he had felt in his youth in the presence of a naked woman.</t2700> <t2701>He thought confusedly, finally caught in a trap of nostalgia, that perhaps if he had married her he would have been a man without war and without glory, an anonymous craftsman, a happy animal.</t2701> <t2702>This late shudder, which was not in his plans, soured his breakfast.</t2702> <t2703>At seven in the morning, when Colonel Gerineldo Márquez came to fetch him accompanied by a group of rebel officers, he found him more taciturn than ever, more thoughtful and solitary.</t2703> <t2704>Úrsula tried to throw a new blanket over his shoulders.</t2704> <t2705>“What will the government think,” she said to him.</t2705> <t2706>“They will imagine that you have surrendered because you no longer had even enough to buy a blanket.”</t2706> <t2707>But he did not accept it.</t2707> <t2708>Already at the door, seeing that the rain continued, he let them put an old felt hat of José Arcadio Buendía on him.</t2708>
<t2709>—Aureliano —Úrsula then said to him—, promise me that if you ever encounter a bad moment out there, you will think of your mother.</t2709>
<t2710>He gave her a distant smile, raised his hand with all his fingers extended, and without saying a word, left the house and faced the shouts, insults, and blasphemies that would pursue him until he left the town.</t2710> <t2711>Úrsula bolted the door, determined not to remove it for the rest of her life.</t2711> <t2712>“We will rot in here,” she thought.</t2712> <t2713>“We will turn to ashes in this house without men, but we will not give this miserable town the pleasure of seeing us cry.”</t2713> <t2714>She spent the whole morning looking for a memory of her son in the most secret corners, and she could not find it.</t2714>
<t2715>The event was held twenty kilometers from Macondo, in the shadow of a gigantic ceiba tree around which the town of Neerlandia would later be founded.</t2715> <t2716>The government and party delegates, and the rebel commission that surrendered their weapons, were served by a bustling group of novices in white habits, who looked like a flutter of doves frightened by the rain.</t2716> <t2717>Colonel Aureliano Buendía arrived on a mud-splattered mule.</t2717> <t2718>He was unshaven, more tormented by the pain of his boils than by the immense failure of his dreams, for he had reached the end of all hope, beyond glory and the nostalgia for glory.</t2718> <t2719>In accordance with his own instructions, there was no music, no fireworks, no joyful bells, no cheers, nor any other manifestation that could alter the mournful character of the armistice.</t2719> <t2720>A traveling photographer, who took the only portrait of him that could have been preserved, was forced to destroy the plates without developing them.</t2720>
<t2721>The act lasted just long enough for the signatures to be affixed.</t2721> <t2722>Around the rustic table placed in the center of a patched circus tent, where the delegates sat, were the last officers who remained loyal to Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2722> <t2723>Before taking the signatures, the personal delegate of the president of the republic tried to read the act of surrender aloud, but Colonel Aureliano Buendía objected.</t2723> <t2724>"Let's not waste time on formalities," he said, and proceeded to sign the documents without reading them.</t2724> <t2725>One of his officers then broke the soporific silence of the tent.</t2725>
<t2726>—Colonel —he said—, do us the favor of not being the first to sign.</t2726>
<t2727>Colonel Aureliano Buendía agreed.</t2727> <t2728>When the document had made a full round of the table, in the midst of a silence so clear that the signatures could have been deciphered by the scratching of the pen on the paper, the first spot was still blank.</t2728> <t2729>Colonel Aureliano Buendía prepared to occupy it.</t2729>
<t2730>—Colonel —said then another of his officers—, you still have time to make things right.</t2730>
<t2731>Without flinching, Colonel Aureliano Buendía signed the first copy.</t2731> <t2732>He had not finished signing the last one when a rebel colonel appeared at the tent door, leading a mule loaded with two trunks by the halter.</t2732> <t2733>Despite his extreme youth, he had a harsh appearance and a patient expression.</t2733> <t2734>He was the treasurer of the revolution in the Macondo district.</t2734> <t2735>He had made a painful six-day journey, dragging the starving mule, to arrive in time for the armistice.</t2735> <t2736>With exasperating slowness, he unloaded the trunks, opened them, and began placing on the table, one by one, seventy-two gold bricks.</t2736> <t2737>No one remembered the existence of that fortune.</t2737> <t2738>In the disorder of the last year, when the central command fell apart and the revolution degenerated into a bloody rivalry of warlords, it was impossible to determine any responsibility.</t2738> <t2739>The gold of the rebellion, melted into blocks that were later covered with baked clay, was beyond any control.</t2739> <t2740>Colonel Aureliano Buendía had the seventy-two gold bricks included in the surrender inventory and closed the act without allowing any speeches.</t2740> <t2741>The scrawny adolescent remained in front of him, looking into his eyes with his serene honey-colored eyes.</t2741>
<t2742>—Anything else? —Colonel Aureliano Buendía asked him.</t2742>
<t2743>The young colonel clenched his teeth.</t2743>
<t2744>—The receipt —he said.</t2744>
<t2745>Colonel Aureliano Buendía handed it over in his own handwriting.</t2745> <t2746>Then he took a glass of lemonade and a piece of cake that the novices distributed, and retired to a tent that had been prepared for him in case he wanted to rest.</t2746> <t2747>There he took off his shirt, sat on the edge of the cot, and at three fifteen in the afternoon he shot himself with a pistol in the circle of iodine that his personal doctor had painted on his chest.</t2747> <t2748>At that hour, in Macondo, Úrsula uncovered the pot of milk on the stove, puzzled that it was taking so long to boil, and found it full of worms.</t2748>
<t2749>—They have killed Aureliano! —he exclaimed.</t2749>
<t2750>He looked toward the courtyard, following a habit born of his solitude, and then he saw José Arcadio Buendía soaked, sad from the rain, and much older than when he died.</t2750> <t2751>"They killed him treacherously," Úrsula specified, "and no one did him the kindness of closing his eyes."</t2751> <t2752>At dusk, through his tears, he saw the swift and luminous orange discs that crossed the sky like a breath, and he thought they were a sign of death.</t2752> <t2753>He was still under the chestnut tree, sobbing on his husband's knees, when they brought Colonel Aureliano Buendía wrapped in the stiff blanket of dried blood and with eyes wide open in rage.</t2753>
<t2754>He was out of danger.</t2754> <t2755>The projectile followed such a clean trajectory that the doctor inserted a cord soaked in iodine through his chest and pulled it out through his back.</t2755> <t2756>“This is my masterpiece,” he said, satisfied.</t2756> <t2757>“It was the only spot where a bullet could pass without harming any vital center.”</t2757> <t2758>Colonel Aureliano Buendía found himself surrounded by merciful nuns who chanted desperate psalms for the eternal rest of his soul, and then he regretted not having shot himself in the palate as he had planned, just to defy Pilar Ternera's prediction.</t2758>
<t2759>—If I still had any authority left —he told the doctor—, I would have him shot without a trial.</t2759> <t2760>Not to save my life, but to make me look ridiculous.</t2760>
<t2761>The failure of death restored his lost prestige in a few hours.</t2761><t2762>The same people who invented the lie that he had sold the war for a room whose walls were made of gold bricks, defined the suicide attempt as an act of honor, and proclaimed him a martyr.</t2762><t2763>Later, when he rejected the Order of Merit awarded to him by the president of the republic, even his most bitter rivals paraded through his room asking him to disregard the terms of the armistice and promote a new war.</t2763><t2764>The house was filled with gifts of reparation.</t2764><t2765>Belatedly impressed by the massive support of his former comrades-in-arms, Colonel Aureliano Buendía did not rule out the possibility of pleasing them.</t2765><t2766>On the contrary, at one point he seemed so enthusiastic about the idea of a new war that Colonel Gerineldo Márquez thought he was only waiting for a pretext to proclaim it.</t2766><t2767>The pretext was indeed offered to him when the president of the republic refused to assign war pensions to former combatants, whether liberal or conservative, unless each file was reviewed by a special commission and the allocation law approved by
