rocking chair that Úrsula had bought for his convalescence, and pacing around the room, he dictated a decisive message to the president of the republic.</t2770><t2771>In that telegram, which was never published, he denounced the first violation of the Treaty of Neerlandia and threatened to proclaim a war to the death if the allocation of pensions was not resolved within fifteen days.</t2771><t2772>His stance was so just that it even allowed for the expectation of support from former conservative combatants.</t2772><t2773>But the only response from the government was the reinforcement of the military guard that had been placed at the door of the house, under the pretext of protecting it, and the prohibition of all kinds of visits.</t2773><t2774>Similar measures were adopted throughout the country with other troublesome leaders.</t2774><t2775>It was such a timely, drastic, and effective operation that two months after the armistice, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía was discharged, his most determined instigators were either dead, exiled, or had been permanently assimilated into the public administration.</t2775>
<t2776>Colonel Aureliano Buendía left the room in December, and it only took a glance at the corridor for him to never think about the war again.</t2776> <t2777>With a vitality that seemed impossible for her age, Úrsula had rejuvenated the house once more.</t2777> <t2778>“Now you will see who I am,” she said when she learned that her son would live.</t2778> <t2779>“There won't be a better house, nor one more open to everyone, than this house of crazies.”</t2779> <t2780>She had it washed and painted, changed the furniture, restored the garden and planted new flowers, and opened doors and windows so that the dazzling summer light would reach even the bedrooms.</t2780> <t2781>She decreed the end of the numerous overlapping mourning periods, and she herself changed the old strict outfits for youthful clothes.</t2781> <t2782>The music of the player piano once again brought joy to the house.</t2782> <t2783>Upon hearing it, Amaranta remembered Pietro Crespi, his twilight gardenia and lavender scent, and in the depths of her withered heart, a clean resentment, purified by time, blossomed.</t2783> <t2784>One afternoon, while trying to bring order to the living room, Úrsula asked the soldiers guarding the house for help.</t2784> <t2785>The young commander of the guard granted them permission.</t2785> <t2786>Little by little, Úrsula assigned them new tasks.</t2786> <t2787>She invited them to eat, gave them clothes and shoes, and taught them to read and write.</t2787> <t2788>When the government suspended the surveillance, one of them stayed living in the house and served her for many years.</t2788> <t2789>On New Year's Day, driven mad by the slights of Remedios the Beauty, the young commander of the guard was found dead from love by her window at dawn.</t2789>
<t2790>Years later, on his deathbed, Aureliano Segundo would remember the rainy June afternoon when he entered the bedroom to meet his first child.</t2790> <t2791>Although he was languid and crying, without any features of a Buendía, he did not have to think twice to name him.</t2791>
<t2792>—He will be called José Arcadio —he said.</t2792>
<t2793>Fernanda del Carpio, the beautiful woman he had married the previous year, agreed.</t2793> <t2794>On the other hand, Úrsula could not hide a vague feeling of unease.</t2794> <t2795>In the long history of the family, the persistent repetition of names had allowed her to draw conclusions that seemed definitive to her.</t2795> <t2796>While the Aurelianos were withdrawn but of clear mind, the José Arcadios were impulsive and enterprising, but they were marked by a tragic sign.</t2796> <t2797>The only cases of impossible classification were those of José Arcadio Segundo and Aureliano Segundo.</t2797> <t2798>They were so similar and mischievous during childhood that not even Santa Sofía de la Piedad herself could distinguish them.</t2798> <t2799>On the day of the baptism, Amaranta put bracelets on them with their respective names and dressed them in different colored clothes marked with each one's initials, but when they started attending school, they opted to switch clothes and bracelets and call themselves by the crossed names.</t2799> <t2800>Teacher Melchor Escalona, accustomed to knowing José Arcadio Segundo by his green shirt, lost his temper when he discovered that this one had Aureliano Segundo's bracelet, and that the other claimed to be Aureliano Segundo despite wearing the white shirt and the bracelet marked with the name José Arcadio Segundo.</t2800> <t2801>From then on, it was not known for certain who was who.</t2801> <t2802>Even when they grew up and life made them different, Úrsula kept wondering if they themselves had made a mistake at some point in their intricate game of confusions and had remained switched forever.</t2802> <t2803>Until the beginning of adolescence, they were two synchronous mechanisms.</t2803> <t2804>They woke up at the same time, felt the need to go to the bathroom at the same hour, suffered the same health disorders, and even dreamed the same things.</t2804> <t2805>In the house, where it was believed they coordinated their actions simply to confuse, no one realized the reality until one day when Santa Sofía de la Piedad gave one a glass of lemonade, and he no sooner tasted it than the other said it lacked sugar.</t2805> <t2806>Santa Sofía de la Piedad, who had indeed forgotten to put sugar in the lemonade, told Úrsula about it.</t2806> <t2807>“That's how they all are,” she said, without surprise.</t2807> <t2808>“Crazy from birth.”</t2808> <t2809>Time finished disorganizing things.</t2809> <t2810>The one who, in the games of confusion, kept the name Aureliano Segundo became monumental like the grandfather, and the one who kept the name José Arcadio Segundo became bony like the colonel, and the only thing they retained in common was the solitary air of the family.</t2810> <t2811>Perhaps it was this crossing of statures, names, and characters that made Úrsula suspect they had been switched since childhood.</t2811>
<t2812>The decisive difference was revealed in the midst of the war when José Arcadio Segundo asked Colonel Gerineldo Márquez to take him to see the executions.</t2812> <t2813>Against Úrsula's opinion, his wishes were granted.</t2813> <t2814>Aureliano Segundo, on the other hand, shuddered at the mere idea of witnessing an execution.</t2814> <t2815>He preferred the house.</t2815> <t2816>At twelve years old, he asked Úrsula what was in the locked room.</t2816> <t2817>"Papers," she replied.</t2817> <t2818>"They are Melquíades' books and the strange things he wrote in his later years."</t2818> <t2819>The answer, instead of calming him, increased his curiosity.</t2819> <t2820>He insisted so much, promised so earnestly not to damage anything, that Úrsula gave him the keys.</t2820> <t2821>No one had entered the room again since they had taken out Melquíades' corpse and put a lock on the door whose pieces had fused with rust.</t2821> <t2822>But when Aureliano Segundo opened the windows, a familiar light entered that seemed accustomed to illuminating the room every day, and there was not the slightest trace of dust or cobwebs; everything was swept and clean, better swept and cleaner than on the day of the burial, and the ink had not dried in the inkwell, nor had rust altered the shine of the metals, nor had the embers of the furnace where José Arcadio Buendía vaporized mercury extinguished.</t2822> <t2823>On the shelves were books bound in a cardboard-like material, pale like tanned human skin, and the manuscripts were intact.</t2823> <t2824>Despite the many years of confinement, the air seemed purer than in the rest of the house.</t2824> <t2825>Everything was so recent that several weeks later, when Úrsula entered the room with a bucket of water and a broom to wash the floors, she had nothing to do.</t2825> <t2826>Aureliano Segundo was absorbed in reading a book.</t2826> <t2827>Although it lacked covers and the title did not appear anywhere, the boy enjoyed the story of a woman who sat at the table and only ate grains of rice that she picked up with pins, and the story of the fisherman who borrowed a lead weight for his net from his neighbor and the fish with which he later repaid him had a diamond in its stomach, and the lamp that granted wishes and the flying carpets.</t2827> <t2828>Astonished, he asked Úrsula if all that was true, and she replied that yes, many years ago the gypsies brought wonderful lamps and flying rugs to Macondo.</t2828>
<t2829>—What happens —he sighed— is that the world is gradually ending and those things no longer come.</t2829>
<t2830>When he finished the book, many of whose stories were incomplete because pages were missing, Aureliano Segundo set about deciphering the manuscripts.</t2830> <t2831>It was impossible.</t2831> <t2832>The letters looked like clothes hung out to dry on a wire, and they resembled musical notation more than literary writing.</t2832> <t2833>One scorching midday, while he was scrutinizing the manuscripts, he felt he was not alone in the room.</t2833> <t2834>Against the glare of the window, sitting with his hands on his knees, was Melquíades.</t2834> <t2835>He was no more than forty years old.</t2835> <t2836>He wore the same anachronistic vest and the raven-winged hat, and the grease from his hair, melted by the heat, dripped down his pale temples, just as Aureliano and José Arcadio had seen when they were children.</t2836> <t2837>Aureliano Segundo recognized him immediately, because that hereditary memory had been passed down from generation to generation, and had reached him from his grandfather's memory.</t2837>
<t2838>—Cheers —said Aureliano Segundo.</t2838>
<t2839>—Greetings, young man —said Melquíades.</t2839>
<t2840>Since then, for several years, they saw each other almost every afternoon.</t2840> <t2841>Melquíades spoke to him about the world, tried to instill his old wisdom in him, but refused to translate the manuscripts.</t2841> <t2842>“No one should know their meaning until a hundred years have passed,” he explained.</t2842> <t2843>Aureliano Segundo kept the secret of those meetings forever.</t2843> <t2844>On one occasion he felt that his private world was collapsing, because Úrsula entered just as Melquíades was in the room.</t2844> <t2845>But she did not see him.</t2845>
<t2846>—Who are you talking to? —he asked her.</t2846>
<t2847>—With no one —said Aureliano Segundo.</t2847>
<t2848>—That was your great-grandfather —said Úrsula—.</t2848> <t2849>He also talked to himself.</t2849>
<t2850>José Arcadio Segundo, meanwhile, had fulfilled the illusion of witnessing an execution.</t2850> <t2851>For the rest of his life, he would remember the livid flash of the six simultaneous shots and the echo of the blast that shattered through the mountains, and the sad smile and perplexed eyes of the executed man, who remained upright while his shirt was soaked with blood, and who continued smiling even when they untied him from the post and put him in a box full of lime.</t2851> <t2852>“He is alive,” he thought.</t2852> <t2853>“They are going to bury him alive.”</t2853> <t2854>He was so impressed that from then on he detested military practices and war, not because of the executions but because of the dreadful habit of burying the executed alive.</t2854> <t2855>No one knew then at what moment he began to ring the bells in the tower, to assist Father Antonio Isabel, successor of El Cachorro, at mass, and to care for fighting cocks in the courtyard of the parish house.</t2855> <t2856>When Colonel Gerineldo Márquez found out, he harshly reprimanded him for learning trades despised by the liberals.</t2856> <t2857>“The thing is,” he replied, “it seems to me that I have turned out to be a conservative.”</t2857> <t2858>He believed it as if it were a determination of fate.</t2858> <t2859>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, scandalized, told Úrsula about it.</t2859>
<t2860>—Better —she approved—.</t2860> <t2861>I hope he becomes a priest, so that God finally enters this house.</t2861>
<t2862>Very soon it was known that Father Antonio Isabel was preparing him for his first communion.</t2862> <t2863>He taught him the catechism while shaving the necks of the roosters.</t2863> <t2864>He explained with simple examples, while placing the broody hens in their nests, how God thought on the second day of creation that chicks should form inside the egg.</t2864> <t2865>Since then, the parish priest showed the first symptoms of the senile delirium that led him to say, years later, that probably the devil had won the rebellion against God, and that it was he who was sitting on the heavenly throne, without revealing his true identity to trap the unwary.</t2865> <t2866>Fired up by the boldness of his preceptor, José Arcadio Segundo became in a few months as skilled in theological tricks to confuse the devil as he was in the tricks of the cockpit.</t2866> <t2867>Amaranta made him a linen suit with a collar and tie, bought him a pair of white shoes, and engraved his name in gold letters on the ribbon of the candle.</t2867> <t2868>Two nights before the first communion, Father Antonio Isabel locked himself up with him in the sacristy to confess him, with the help of a dictionary of sins.</t2868> <t2869>It was such a long list that the elderly parish priest, used to going to bed at six, fell asleep in the chair before finishing.</t2869> <t2870>The interrogation was a revelation for José Arcadio Segundo.</t2870> <t2871>He was not surprised that the father asked him if he had done bad things with women, and he honestly answered no, but he was puzzled by the question of whether he had done them with animals.</t2871> <t2872>On the first Friday of May, he took communion tortured by curiosity.</t2872> <t2873>Later he asked the question to Petronio, the sick sacristan who lived in the tower and who, according to rumors, fed on bats, and Petronio answered him: "It’s because there are corrupted Christians who do their things with donkeys."</t2873> <t2874>José Arcadio Segundo continued to show so much curiosity, asked so many questions, that Petronio lost his patience.</t2874>
<t2875>—I go on Tuesday nights —he confessed—.</t2875> <t2876>If you promise not to tell anyone, I'll take you next Tuesday.</t2876>
<t2877>On the following Tuesday, indeed, Petronio came down from the tower with a small wooden bench that no one had known what it was for until then, and took José Arcadio Segundo to a nearby orchard.</t2877> <t2878>The boy became so fond of those nocturnal excursions that a long time passed before he was seen again in Catarino's store.</t2878> <t2879>He became a rooster man.</t2879> <t2880>"Take those animals somewhere else," Úrsula ordered him the first time she saw him enter with his fine fighting animals.</t2880> <t2881>"Roosters have already brought too much bitterness to this house for you to bring us more now."</t2881> <t2882>José Arcadio Segundo took them away without argument, but continued raising them at Pilar Ternera's, his grandmother, who provided him with everything he needed in exchange for having him in the house.</t2882> <t2883>Soon he demonstrated in the cockfighting ring the wisdom that Father Antonio Isabel had instilled in him, and had enough money not only to enrich his breeds but also to procure himself the satisfactions of a man.</t2883> <t2884>At that time, Úrsula compared him to his brother and could not understand how the two twins, who had seemed like one person in their childhood, had ended up being so different.</t2884> <t2885>The perplexity did not last long, because very soon Aureliano Segundo began to show signs of laziness and dissipation.</t2885> <t2886>While he was locked up in Melquíades' room, he was a self-absorbed man, as Colonel Aureliano Buendía had been in his youth.</t2886> <t2887>But shortly before the Treaty of Neerlandia, a coincidence took him out of his self-absorption and confronted him with the reality of the world.</t2887> <t2888>A young woman, who was selling tickets for the raffle of an accordion, greeted him very familiarly.</t2888> <t2889>Aureliano Segundo was not surprised because it often happened that he was mistaken for his brother.</t2889> <t2890>But he did not clarify the misunderstanding, not even when the girl tried to soften his heart with whimpering, and ended up taking him to her room.</t2890> <t2891>She became so fond of him from that first encounter that she cheated in the raffle so that he would win the accordion.</t2891> <t2892>After two weeks, Aureliano Segundo realized that the woman had been sleeping alternately with him and his brother, believing they were the same man, and instead of clarifying the situation, he managed to prolong it.</t2892> <t2893>He did not return to Melquíades' room.</t2893> <t2894>He spent the afternoons in the courtyard, learning to play the accordion by ear, against Úrsula's protests, who at that time had banned music in the house because of mourning, and who also despised the accordion as an instrument fit for the vagabond heirs of Francisco the Man.</t2894> <t2895>However, Aureliano Segundo became a virtuoso of the accordion and remained so even after he got married, had children, and became one of the most respected men in Macondo.</t2895>
<t2896>For almost two months, the woman shared with her brother.</t2896> <t2897>She watched over him, disrupted his plans, and when she was sure that José Arcadio Segundo would not visit their common lover that night, she went to sleep with her.</t2897> <t2898>One morning, she discovered that he was sick.</t2898> <t2899>Two days later, she found her brother clinging to a beam in the bathroom, drenched in sweat and crying his heart out, and then she understood.</t2899> <t2900>Her brother confessed to her that the woman had rejected him for bringing her what she called a disease of the bad life.</t2900> <t2901>He also told her how Pilar Ternera was trying to cure him.</t2901> <t2902>Aureliano Segundo secretly submitted to the burning permanganate washes and diuretic waters, and both were cured separately after three months of secret suffering.</t2902> <t2903>José Arcadio Segundo never saw the woman again.</t2903> <t2904>Aureliano Segundo obtained her forgiveness and stayed with her until death.</t2904>
<t2905>Her name was Petra Cotes.</t2905> <t2906>She had arrived in Macondo in the midst of the war, with a casual husband who lived off raffles, and when the man died, she continued with the business.</t2906> <t2907>She was a clean and young mulatto woman, with yellow almond-shaped eyes that gave her face the ferocity of a panther, but she had a generous heart and a magnificent vocation for love.</t2907> <t2908>When Úrsula realized that José Arcadio Segundo was a cockfighter and Aureliano Segundo played the accordion at his concubine's noisy parties, she thought she would go mad with confusion.</t2908> <t2909>It was as if both had concentrated the family's defects and none of its virtues.</t2909> <t2910>Then she decided that no one would be named Aureliano or José Arcadio again.</t2910> <t2911>However, when Aureliano Segundo had his first child, she did not dare to contradict him.</t2911>
<t2912>—Agreed —said Úrsula—, but with one condition: I will take care of raising him.</t2912>
<t2913>Although she was already a centenarian and was about to go blind from cataracts, she retained her physical dynamism, integrity of character, and mental balance intact.</t2913> <t2914>No one better than her to shape the virtuous man who was to restore the family's prestige, a man who would never have heard of war, fighting cocks, women of ill repute, and delirious enterprises, four calamities that, according to Úrsula, had determined the decline of her lineage.</t2914> <t2915>"He will be a priest," she promised solemnly.</t2915> <t2916>"And if God gives me life, he will become Pope."</t2916> <t2917>Everyone laughed upon hearing her, not only in the bedroom but throughout the house, where Aureliano Segundo's boisterous friends were gathered.</t2917> <t2918>The war, relegated to the attic of bad memories, was momentarily evoked with the popping of champagne corks.</t2918>
<t2919>—To the health of the Pope —Aureliano Segundo toasted.</t2919>
<t2920>The guests toasted in unison.</t2920> <t2921>Then the host played the accordion, fireworks exploded, and drums of jubilation were ordered for the town.</t2921> <t2922>In the early morning, the guests, soaked in champagne, sacrificed six cows and put them in the street for the crowd to take.</t2922> <t2923>Nobody was scandalized.</t2923> <t2924>Since Aureliano Segundo took charge of the house, those festivities were commonplace, even if there wasn't a reason as just as the birth of a Pope.</t2924> <t2925>In a few years, without effort, purely by strokes of luck, he had accumulated one of the greatest fortunes in the swamp, thanks to the supernatural proliferation of his animals.</t2925> <t2926>His mares gave birth to triplets, the hens laid eggs twice a day, and the pigs fattened with such frenzy that no one could explain such chaotic fertility, except by magic arts.</t2926> <t2927>“Save now,” Úrsula would tell her scatterbrained great-grandson.</t2927> <t2928>“This luck won't last you a lifetime.”</t2928> <t2929>But Aureliano Segundo paid her no attention.</t2929> <t2930>The more champagne he uncorked to soak his friends, the more wildly his animals reproduced, and the more convinced he became that his good fortune was not due to his behavior but to the influence of Petra Cotes, his concubine, whose love had the virtue of exasperating nature.</t2930> <t2931>He was so persuaded that this was the origin of his fortune that he never kept Petra Cotes away from his offspring, and even when he married and had children, he continued living with her with Fernanda's consent.</t2931> <t2932>Solid, monumental like his grandparents, but with a vital joy and an irresistible charm that they did not have, Aureliano Segundo barely had time to oversee his herds.</t2932> <t2933>It was enough for him to take Petra Cotes to his breeding grounds and ride her on horseback through his lands for every animal marked with his brand to succumb to the irremediable plague of proliferation.</t2933>
<t2934>Like all the good things that happened to them in their long life, that unbridled fortune originated from chance.</t2934> <t2935>Until the end of the wars, Petra Cotes continued to support herself with the proceeds from her raffles, and Aureliano Segundo managed to occasionally raid Úrsula's piggy banks.</t2935> <t2936>They formed a frivolous couple, with no other concerns than going to bed every night, even on forbidden dates, and frolicking in bed until dawn.</t2936> <t2937>“That woman has been your downfall,” Úrsula would shout at her great-grandson when she saw him enter the house like a sleepwalker.</t2937> <t2938>“She has you so bewitched that one of these days I'll see you writhing in colic, with a toad inside your belly.”</t2938> <t2939>José Arcadio Segundo, who took a long time to discover the substitution, could not understand his brother's passion.</t2939> <t2940>He remembered Petra Cotes as a conventional woman, rather lazy in bed, and completely devoid of resources for love.</t2940> <t2941>Deaf to Úrsula's clamor and his brother's taunts, Aureliano Segundo only thought then of finding a job that would allow him to support a house for Petra Cotes, and die with her, on her, and under her, in a night of feverish excess.</t2941> <t2942>When Colonel Aureliano Buendía reopened the workshop, finally seduced by the peaceful charms of old age, Aureliano Segundo thought it would be a good business to dedicate himself to making little gold fishes.</t2942> <t2943>He spent many hours in the hot little room watching how the hard metal sheets, worked by the colonel with the inconceivable patience of disappointment, gradually turned into golden scales.</t2943> <t2944>The trade seemed so laborious to him, and the memory of Petra Cotes was so persistent and pressing, that after three weeks he disappeared from the workshop.</t2944> <t2945>It was during that time that Petra Cotes took to raffling rabbits.</t2945> <t2946>They reproduced and became adults so quickly that there was barely time to sell the raffle numbers.</t2946> <t2947>At first, Aureliano Segundo did not notice the alarming proportions of the proliferation.</t2947> <t2948>But one night, when no one in town wanted to hear about rabbit raffles anymore, he heard a crash against the patio wall.</t2948> <t2949>“Don't be scared,” said Petra Cotes.</t2949> <t2950>“It's the rabbits.”</t2950> <t2951>They could not sleep anymore, tormented by the hustle of the animals.</t2951> <t2952>At dawn, Aureliano Segundo opened the door and saw the patio paved with rabbits, blue in the glow of dawn.</t2952> <t2953>Petra Cotes, laughing her head off, could not resist the temptation to play a joke on him.</t2953>
<t2954>—These are the ones who were born last night —he said.</t2954>
<t2955>—What a horror! —he said—.</t2955> <t2956>Why don't you try with cows?</t2956>
<t2957>A few days later, trying to clear out her yard, Petra Cotes traded the rabbits for a cow, which two months later gave birth to triplets.</t2957> <t2958>That's how things started.</t2958> <t2959>Overnight, Aureliano Segundo became the owner of lands and livestock, and he barely had time to expand the overflowing stables and pigsties.</t2959> <t2960>It was a delirious prosperity that made him laugh, and he couldn't help but adopt extravagant attitudes to release his good humor.</t2960> <t2961>"Move aside, cows, life is short," he shouted.</t2961> <t2962>Úrsula wondered what kind of trouble he had gotten into, if he was stealing, if he had ended up becoming a cattle rustler, and every time she saw him popping champagne just for the pleasure of pouring the foam over his head, she would loudly reproach him for the waste.</t2962> <t2963>She bothered him so much that one day, when Aureliano Segundo woke up in an overflowing good mood, he appeared with a box of money, a can of paste, and a brush, and singing at the top of his lungs the old songs of Francisco the Man, he wallpapered the house inside and out, from top to bottom, with one-peso bills.</t2963> <t2964>The old mansion, painted white since the time they brought the pianola, took on the ambiguous appearance of a mosque.</t2964> <t2965>Amid the family's uproar, Úrsula's scandal, and the town's jubilation that crowded the street to witness the glorification of waste, Aureliano Segundo ended up wallpapering from the facade to the kitchen, including the bathrooms and bedrooms, and threw the leftover bills in the yard.</t2965>
<t2966>—Now —he finally said— I hope no one in this house talks to me about money again.</t2966>
<t2967>That's how it was.</t2967> <t2968>Úrsula had the bills removed from the large cakes of lime and repainted the house white.</t2968> <t2969>"My God," she pleaded.</t2969> <t2970>"Make us as poor as we were when we founded this town, lest in the afterlife you charge us for this wastefulness."</t2970> <t2971>Her pleas were heard in the opposite sense.</t2971> <t2972>Indeed, one of the workers who was removing the bills accidentally stumbled upon an enormous plaster Saint Joseph that someone had left in the house during the last years of the war, and the hollow image shattered on the ground.</t2972> <t2973>It was stuffed with gold coins. No one remembered who had brought that life-sized saint.</t2973> <t2974>"Three men brought it," explained Amaranta.</t2974> <t2975>"They asked me to keep it while the rain passed, and I told them to put it there, in the corner, where no one would trip over it, and they placed it there very carefully, and it has been there ever since because they never came back to claim it."</t2975> <t2976>In recent times, Úrsula had lit candles and prostrated herself before it, without suspecting that instead of a saint, she was worshipping almost two hundred kilograms of gold.</t2976> <t2977>The late realization of her involuntary paganism worsened her dismay.</t2977> <t2978>She spat on the spectacular pile of coins, put it into three canvas sacks, and buried it in a secret place, waiting for the three strangers to eventually come and claim it.</t2978> <t2979>Much later, in the difficult years of her decrepitude, Úrsula would often intervene in the conversations of the numerous travelers who then passed through the house, and she would ask them if during the war they had left a plaster Saint Joseph there to be kept while the rain passed.</t2979>
<t2980>These things, which so greatly dismayed Úrsula, were common at that time.</t2980> <t2981>Macondo was drowning in a miraculous prosperity.</t2981> <t2982>The mud and cane houses of the founders had been replaced by brick constructions, with wooden shutters and cement floors, which made the suffocating heat of two in the afternoon more bearable.</t2982> <t2983>From the old village of José Arcadio Buendía, only the dusty almond trees, destined to withstand the harshest circumstances, and the river with its clear waters whose prehistoric stones were pulverized by the crazed sledgehammers of José Arcadio Segundo when he insisted on clearing the channel to establish a navigation service, remained.</t2983> <t2984>It was a delirious dream, comparable only to those of his great-grandfather, because the rocky bed and the numerous obstacles of the current prevented transit from Macondo to the sea.</t2984> <t2985>But José Arcadio Segundo, in an unforeseen burst of recklessness, persisted with the project.</t2985> <t2986>Until then, he had shown no sign of imagination.</t2986> <t2987>Except for his precarious adventure with Petra Cotes, he had never been known to have a woman.</t2987> <t2988>Úrsula considered him the dullest specimen the family had ever produced, incapable of standing out even as a cockfight troublemaker, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía told him the story of the Spanish galleon stranded twelve kilometers from the sea, whose charred ribs he himself saw during the war.</t2988> <t2989>The story, which had seemed fantastic to so many people for so long, was a revelation for José Arcadio Segundo.</t2989> <t2990>He auctioned off his roosters to the highest bidder, recruited men, bought tools, and embarked on the colossal enterprise of breaking stones, digging canals, clearing obstacles, and even leveling waterfalls.</t2990> <t2991>“I already know this by heart,” Úrsula shouted.</t2991> <t2992>“It’s as if time were going in circles and we had returned to the beginning.”</t2992> <t2993>When he deemed the river navigable, José Arcadio Segundo gave his brother a detailed presentation of his plans, and his brother gave him the money he needed for his enterprise.</t2993> <t2994>He disappeared for a long time.</t2994> <t2995>It was said that his project to buy a boat was nothing more than a ruse to abscond with his brother’s money, when news spread that a strange vessel was approaching the town.</t2995> <t2996>The inhabitants of Macondo, who no longer remembered the colossal enterprises of José Arcadio Buendía, rushed to the riverbank and saw with eyes wide with incredulity the arrival of the first and last boat that ever docked in the town.</t2996> <t2997>It was nothing more than a raft of logs, dragged by thick cables by twenty men walking along the riverbank.</t2997> <t2998>At the prow, with a gleam of satisfaction in his eyes, José Arcadio Segundo directed the extravagant maneuver.</t2998> <t2999>Along with him came a group of splendid matrons who protected themselves from the scorching sun with colorful umbrellas, and wore precious silk shawls on their shoulders, colorful ointments on their faces, natural flowers in their hair, gold snakes on their arms, and diamonds in their teeth.</t2999> <t3000>The log raft was the only vehicle that José Arcadio Segundo managed to bring up to Macondo, and only once, but he never acknowledged the failure of his enterprise; instead, he proclaimed his feat as a victory of will.</t3000> <t3001>He gave scrupulous accounts to his brother and soon returned to the routine of the roosters.</t3001> <t3002>The only thing that remained of that unfortunate initiative was the breath of renewal brought by the matrons from France, whose magnificent arts changed the traditional methods of love, and whose sense of social well-being swept away the outdated store of Catarino and transformed the street into a bazaar of Japanese lanterns and nostalgic barrel organs.</t3002> <t3003>They were the promoters of the bloody carnival that for three days plunged Macondo into delirium, and whose only lasting consequence was giving Aureliano Segundo the opportunity to meet Fernanda del Carpio.</t3003>
<t3004>Remedios, the beautiful, was proclaimed queen.</t3004> <t3005>Úrsula, who trembled before the unsettling beauty of her great-granddaughter, could not prevent the election.</t3005> <t3006>Until then, she had managed to keep her from going out, except to attend mass with Amaranta, but she made her cover her face with a black mantilla.</t3006> <t3007>The less pious men, those who disguised themselves as priests to say sacrilegious masses in Catarino's shop, attended church with the sole purpose of catching even a glimpse of the face of Remedios, the beautiful, whose legendary beauty was spoken of with an overwhelming fervor throughout the swamp area.</t3007> <t3008>A long time passed before they succeeded, and they would have been better off if the occasion had never come, for most of them never regained the tranquility of sleep.</t3008> <t3009>The man who made it possible, a stranger, lost his serenity forever, got entangled in the quagmires of abjection and misery, and years later was torn apart by a night train when he fell asleep on the tracks.</t3009> <t3010>From the moment he was seen in the church, wearing a green velvet suit and an embroidered vest, no one doubted that he had come from very far away, perhaps from a distant city abroad, drawn by the magical allure of Remedios, the beautiful.</t3010> <t3011>He was so handsome, so gallant and composed, with such well-carried poise, that next to him Pietro Crespi would have seemed like a premature baby, and many women murmured with spiteful smiles that he was the one who truly deserved the mantilla.</t3011> <t3012>He did not mingle with anyone in Macondo.</t3012> <t3013>He appeared at dawn on Sunday, like a fairytale prince, on a horse with silver stirrups and velvet trappings, and left the town after mass.</t3013>
<t3014>Such was the power of his presence that from the first time he was seen in the church, everyone took it for granted that between him and Remedios, the beautiful, a silent and tense duel had been established, a secret pact, an irrevocable challenge whose culmination could not be only love but also death.</t3014> <t3015>On the sixth Sunday, the gentleman appeared with a yellow rose in his hand.</t3015> <t3016>He heard the mass standing, as he always did, and at the end, he intercepted Remedios, the beautiful, and offered her the solitary rose.</t3016> <t3017>She received it with a natural gesture, as if she had been prepared for that homage, and then she revealed her face for an instant and thanked him with a smile.</t3017> <t3018>That was all she did.</t3018> <t3019>But not only for the gentleman, but for all the men who had the unfortunate privilege of witnessing it, that was an eternal moment.</t3019>
<t3020>Since then, the gentleman had been setting up the band next to Remedios the Beauty's window, sometimes until dawn.</t3020> <t3021>Aureliano Segundo was the only one who felt a heartfelt compassion for him and tried to break his perseverance.</t3021> <t3022>"Don't waste any more time," he told him one night.</t3022> <t3023>"The women of this house are worse than mules."</t3023> <t3024>He offered him his friendship, invited him to bathe in champagne, tried to make him understand that the women in his family had hearts of stone, but he couldn't break his obstinacy.</t3024> <t3025>Exasperated by the endless nights of music, Colonel Aureliano Buendía threatened to cure his affliction with gunshots.</t3025> <t3026>Nothing made him desist, except his own deplorable state of demoralization.</t3026> <t3027>From handsome and impeccable, he became vile and ragged.</t3027> <t3028>It was rumored that he had abandoned power and fortune in his distant nation, although his origin was never truly known.</t3028> <t3029>He became a man of brawls, a barroom brawler, and was found at dawn wallowing in his own excrement in Catarino's store.</t3029> <t3030>The saddest part of his drama was that Remedios the Beauty didn't even notice him, not even when he showed up at the church dressed as a prince.</t3030> <t3031>She received the yellow rose without the slightest malice, rather amused by the extravagance of the gesture, and lifted her mantilla to see his face better, not to show him hers.</t3031>
<t3032>In reality, Remedios the Beautiful was not a being of this world.</t3032> <t3033>Until she was well into puberty, Santa Sofía de la Piedad had to bathe her and dress her, and even when she could fend for herself, she had to be watched to prevent her from painting little animals on the walls with a stick smeared with her own feces.</t3033> <t3034>She reached the age of twenty without learning to read or write, without using cutlery at the table, and walking naked around the house, because her nature resisted any kind of conventionalism.</t3034> <t3035>When the young commander of the guard declared his love for her, she rejected him simply because his frivolity astonished her.</t3035> <t3036>“Look how simple he is,” she said to Amaranta.</t3036> <t3037>“He says he is dying for me, as if I were a colic miserere.”</t3037> <t3038>When they indeed found him dead next to her window, Remedios the Beautiful confirmed her initial impression.</t3038>
<t3039>—You see —he commented—.</t3039> <t3040>It was completely simple.</t3040>
<t3041>It seemed as if a penetrating lucidity allowed her to see the reality of things beyond any formalism.</t3041> <t3042>That was at least the point of view of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, for whom Remedios the Beauty was by no means mentally retarded, as was believed, but quite the opposite.</t3042> <t3043>"It's as if she came back from twenty years of war," he used to say.</t3043> <t3044>Úrsula, for her part, thanked God for rewarding the family with a creature of exceptional purity, but at the same time, her beauty disturbed her, because it seemed like a contradictory virtue, a diabolical trap in the midst of candor.</t3044> <t3045>That was why she decided to keep her away from the world, to preserve her from all earthly temptation, not knowing that Remedios the Beauty, from her mother's womb, was already safe from any contagion.</t3045> <t3046>It never crossed her mind that she would be chosen as the queen of beauty in the pandemonium of a carnival.</t3046> <t3047>But Aureliano Segundo, excited by the whirlwind of dressing up as a tiger, brought Father Antonio Isabel to the house to convince Úrsula that the carnival was not a pagan festival, as she said, but a Catholic tradition.</t3047> <t3048>Finally convinced, though reluctantly, she gave her consent for the coronation.</t3048>
<t3049>The news that Remedios Buendía was going to be the queen of the festival spread beyond the limits of the swamp in a few hours, reaching distant territories where the immense prestige of her beauty was unknown, and arousing the unease of those who still considered her surname a symbol of subversion.</t3049><t3050>It was an unfounded unease.</t3050><t3051>If anyone was harmless at that time, it was the aged and disillusioned Colonel Aureliano Buendía, who had gradually lost all contact with the reality of the nation.</t3051><t3052>Locked in his workshop, his only connection with the rest of the world was the trade of little gold fishes.</t3052><t3053>One of the former soldiers who guarded his house in the early days of peace would sell them in the swamp towns and return loaded with coins and news.</t3053><t3054>That the conservative government, he said, with the support of the liberals, was reforming the calendar so that each president would stay in power for a hundred years.</t3054><t3055>That the concordat with the Holy See had finally been signed, and a cardinal had come from Rome with a diamond crown and on a solid gold throne, and the liberal ministers had had their pictures taken kneeling as they kissed his ring.</t3055><t3056>That the leading chorus girl of a Spanish company, passing through the capital, had been kidnapped in her dressing room by a group of masked men, and the following Sunday had danced naked at the president of the republic's summer house.</t3056><t3057>"Don't talk to me about politics," the colonel would say to him.</t3057><t3058>"Our business is to sell little fishes."</t3058><t3059>The public rumor that he wanted nothing to do with the country's situation because he was getting rich from his workshop made Úrsula laugh when it reached her ears.</t3059><t3060>With her terrible practical sense, she could not understand the colonel's business, who exchanged the little fishes for gold coins, and then turned the gold coins back into little fishes, and so on, so that he had to work more and more as he sold more, to satisfy an exasperating vicious circle.</t3060><t3061>In truth, what interested him was not the business but the work.</t3061><t3062>He needed so much concentration to link scales, inlay tiny rubies in the eyes, laminate gills, and mount rudders, that he had no empty space left to fill with the disillusionment of the war.</t3062><t3063>So absorbing was the attention demanded by the meticulousness of his craft that in a short time he aged more than in all the years of the war, and the position twisted his spine and the precision wore out his eyesight, but the relentless concentration rewarded him with peace of mind.</t3063><t3064>The last time he was seen attending to any matter related to the war was when a group of veterans from both parties sought his support for the approval of lifelong pensions, always promised and always at the starting point.</t3064><t3065>"Forget about that," he told them.</t3065><t3066>"You see that I rejected my pension to rid myself of the torture of waiting for it until death."</t3066><t3067>At first, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez would visit him in the evening, and they would both sit at the street door to recall the past.</t3067><t3068>But Amaranta could not bear the memories evoked by that tired man whose baldness precipitated him into the abyss of premature old age, and she tormented him with unjust slights, until he only returned on special occasions, and finally disappeared, paralyzed.</t3068><t3069>Taciturn, silent, insensible to the new breath of vitality that shook the house, Colonel Aureliano Buendía barely understood that the secret to a good old age is nothing more than an honest pact with solitude.</t3069><t3070>He would get up at five after a superficial sleep, take his eternal bowl of bitter coffee in the kitchen, lock himself in the workshop all day, and at four in the afternoon would pass through the corridor dragging a stool, without even noticing the blaze of the rose bushes, the brightness of the hour, or the impassiveness of Amaranta, whose melancholy made a perfectly perceptible pot noise in the evening, and sit at the street door until the mosquitoes allowed him.</t3070><t3071>Someone once dared to disturb his solitude.</t3071>
<t3072>—How are you, Colonel? —he said as he passed by.</t3072>
<t3073>—Here —he replied—.</t3073> <t3074>Waiting for my funeral to pass.</t3074>
<t3075>Thus, the unease caused by the public reappearance of their surname, regarding the reign of Remedios, the beautiful, lacked any real foundation.</t3075> <t3076>Many, however, did not believe so.</t3076> <t3077>Innocent of the tragedy that threatened them, the townspeople overflowed into the public square in a boisterous explosion of joy.</t3077> <t3078>The carnival had reached its highest level of madness; Aureliano Segundo had finally fulfilled his dream of dressing up as a tiger and wandered happily among the frenzied crowd, hoarse from so much roaring, when a large parade appeared on the swamp road carrying on golden litters the most fascinating woman that imagination could conceive.</t3078> <t3079>For a moment, the peaceful inhabitants of Macondo removed their masks to better see the dazzling creature with an emerald crown and ermine cape, who seemed invested with legitimate authority, and not simply a sovereignty of sequins and crepe paper.</t3079> <t3080>There was no shortage of those with enough clairvoyance to suspect that it was a provocation.</t3080> <t3081>But Aureliano Segundo immediately overcame his perplexity, declared the newcomers as guests of honor, and seated Remedios, the beautiful, and the intruding queen on the same pedestal, Solomon-like.</t3081> <t3082>Until midnight, the outsiders dressed as Bedouins participated in the delirium and even enriched it with sumptuous pyrotechnics and acrobatic virtues that made one think of the arts of the gypsies.</t3082> <t3083>Suddenly, at the paroxysm of the party, someone broke the delicate balance.</t3083>
<t3084>—Long live the Liberal Party! —he shouted—.</t3084> <t3085>Long live Colonel Aureliano Buendía!</t3085>
<t3086>The rifle volleys drowned out the splendor of the fireworks, and the screams of terror overpowered the music, and the jubilation was annihilated by panic.</t3086> <t3087>Many years later, it would still be claimed that the royal guard of the intrusive sovereign was a squadron of the regular army that hid regulation rifles beneath their rich robes.</t3087> <t3088>The government rejected the charge in an extraordinary decree and promised a thorough investigation of the bloody episode.</t3088> <t3089>But the truth was never clarified, and the version prevailed forever that the royal guard, without any kind of provocation, took combat positions at a signal from their commander and mercilessly fired upon the crowd.</t3089> <t3090>When calm was restored, not a single one of the false Bedouins remained in the town, and lying in the square, among the dead and wounded, were nine clowns, four columbines, seventeen playing card kings, a devil, three musicians, two Peers of France, and three Japanese empresses.</t3090> <t3091>In the confusion of the panic, José Arcadio Segundo managed to save Remedios the Beautiful, and Aureliano Segundo carried the intrusive sovereign to the house in his arms, with her dress torn and her ermine cape stained with blood.</t3091> <t3092>Her name was Fernanda del Carpio.</t3092> <t3093>She had been selected as the most beautiful among the five thousand most beautiful women in the country, and she had been brought to Macondo with the promise of being named queen of Madagascar.</t3093> <t3094>Úrsula took care of her as if she were a daughter.</t3094> <t3095>The town, instead of doubting her innocence, felt pity for her naivety.</t3095> <t3096>Six months after the massacre, when the wounded had recovered and the last flowers in the mass grave had withered, Aureliano Segundo went to fetch her from the distant city where she lived with her father, and married her in Macondo, in a raucous twenty-day party.</t3096>
<t3097>The marriage was on the verge of ending after two months because Aureliano Segundo, trying to make amends to Petra Cotes, had her take a portrait dressed as the queen of Madagascar.</t3097> <t3098>When Fernanda found out, she packed her newlywed trunks again and left Macondo without saying goodbye.</t3098> <t3099>Aureliano Segundo caught up with her on the swamp road.</t3099> <t3100>After many pleas and promises of amendment, he managed to bring her back to the house, and he abandoned the concubine.</t3100>
<t3101>Petra Cotes, aware of her strength, showed no signs of concern.</t3101> <t3102>She had made him a man.</t3102> <t3103>While he was still a child, she took him out of Melquíades' room, with his head full of fantastic ideas and no contact with reality, and gave him a place in the world.</t3103> <t3104>Nature had made him reserved and elusive, with tendencies towards solitary meditation, and she had shaped his character into the opposite: vital, expansive, uninhibited, and had instilled in him the joy of living and the pleasure of partying and extravagance, until she turned him, inside and out, into the man she had dreamed of for herself since adolescence.</t3104> <t3105>He had gotten married, as children eventually do.</t3105> <t3106>He did not dare to break the news to her in advance.</t3106> <t3107>He assumed such a childish attitude towards the situation that he pretended to have false grudges and imaginary resentments, looking for a way for Petra Cotes to provoke the breakup.</t3107> <t3108>One day, when Aureliano Segundo unfairly reproached her, she avoided the trap and set things straight.</t3108>
<t3109>"What happens," he said, "is that you want to marry the queen."</t3109>
<t3110>Aureliano Segundo, ashamed, pretended to have a fit of rage, declared himself misunderstood and outraged, and did not visit her again.</t3110> <t3111>Petra Cotes, without losing for a single moment her magnificent composure of a resting beast, heard the music and fireworks of the wedding, the wild bustle of the public party, as if all of it were nothing more than another prank by Aureliano Segundo.</t3111> <t3112>To those who pitied her fate, she reassured them with a smile.</t3112> <t3113>"Don't worry," she told them.</t3113> <t3114>"Queens run errands for me."</t3114> <t3115>To a neighbor who brought her candles to light up the portrait of her lost lover, she said with enigmatic confidence:</t3115>
<t3116>—The only candle that will make him come is always lit.</t3116>
<t3117>Just as she had foreseen, Aureliano Segundo returned home as soon as the honeymoon was over.</t3117> <t3118>He brought along his usual friends, a traveling photographer, and the blood-stained ermine cape and suit that Fernanda had worn during the carnival.</t3118> <t3119>In the heat of the party that ignited that afternoon, he dressed Petra Cotes as a queen, crowned her the absolute and lifelong sovereign of Madagascar, and distributed copies of the portrait among his friends.</t3119> <t3120>She not only went along with the game but also felt a deep pity for him, thinking that he must have been very scared when he conceived that extravagant reconciliation tactic.</t3120> <t3121>At seven in the evening, still dressed as a queen, she received him in bed.</t3121> <t3122>He had been married for only two months, but she immediately realized that things were not going well in the marital bed, and she experienced the delightful pleasure of fulfilled revenge.</t3122> <t3123>Two days later, however, when he did not dare to return but instead sent an intermediary to arrange the terms of the separation, she understood that she would need more patience than anticipated, because he seemed willing to sacrifice himself for appearances.</t3123> <t3124>Even then, she did not get upset.</t3124> <t3125>She once again made things easier with a submission that confirmed the widespread belief that she was a poor woman, and the only memory she kept of Aureliano Segundo was a pair of patent leather boots that, according to him, were the ones he wanted to wear in the coffin.</t3125> <t3126>She stored them wrapped in rags at the bottom of a trunk and prepared herself for a wait without despair.</t3126>
<t3127>—Sooner or later he has to come —he told himself—, even if it's just to put on these boots.</t3127>
<t3128>He didn't have to wait as long as he thought.</t3128> <t3129>In fact, Aureliano Segundo realized on his wedding night that he would return to Petra Cotes' house much sooner than he would need to put on his patent leather boots: Fernanda was a woman lost to the world.</t3129> <t3130>She was born and raised a thousand kilometers from the sea, in a gloomy city where the stone alleyways still rattled, on nights of fright, with the carriages of the viceroys.</t3130> <t3131>Thirty-two bell towers tolled for the dead at six in the evening.</t3131> <t3132>In the stately house paved with sepulchral slabs, the sun was never known.</t3132> <t3133>The air had died in the cypresses of the courtyard, in the pale hangings of the bedrooms, in the dripping arcades of the garden of tuberoses.</t3133> <t3134>Until puberty, Fernanda had no other news of the world than the melancholic piano exercises played in some neighboring house by someone who for years and years allowed themselves the whim of not taking a nap.</t3134> <t3135>In the room of her sick mother, green and yellow under the dusty light of the stained glass windows, she listened to the methodical, tenacious, disheartened scales, and thought that this music was in the world, while she wasted away weaving funeral palm crowns.</t3135> <t3136>Her mother, sweating the fever of five o'clock, spoke to her of the splendor of the past.</t3136> <t3137>As a very young girl, one moonlit night, Fernanda saw a beautiful woman dressed in white who crossed the garden towards the oratory.</t3137> <t3138>What most disturbed her about that fleeting vision was that she felt exactly like her, as if she had seen herself twenty years in advance.</t3138> <t3139>“She is your great-grandmother, the queen,” her mother told her in the pauses of her cough.</t3139> <t3140>“She died from a bad air that struck her while cutting a stalk of tuberoses.”</t3140> <t3141>Many years later, when she began to feel like her great-grandmother, Fernanda doubted the childhood vision, but her mother reproached her for her disbelief.</t3141>
<t3142>—We are immensely rich and powerful —he told her—.</t3142> <t3143>One day you will be queen.</t3143>
<t3144>She believed it, although they only occupied the long table with linen tablecloths and silverware to have a cup of chocolate with water and a sweet bread.</t3144><t3145>Until the day of the wedding, she dreamed of a legendary reign, despite the fact that her father, Don Fernando, had to mortgage the house to buy her trousseau.</t3145><t3146>It was neither naivety nor delusions of grandeur.</t3146><t3147>That was how she was raised.</t3147><t3148>Since she could remember, she had done her business in a golden chamber pot with the family coat of arms.</t3148><t3149>She left the house for the first time at twelve years old, in a horse-drawn carriage that only had to travel two blocks to take her to the convent.</t3149><t3150>Her classmates were surprised that she was kept apart, in a high-backed chair, and that she didn't even mix with them during recess.</t3150><t3151>"She is different," the nuns explained.</t3151><t3152>"She is going to be a queen."</t3152><t3153>Her classmates believed it, because even then she was the most beautiful, distinguished, and discreet maiden they had ever seen.</t3153><t3154>After eight years, having learned to versify in Latin, to play the clavichord, to converse about falconry with gentlemen and apologetics with archbishops, to discuss state matters with foreign rulers and matters of God with the Pope, she returned to her parents' house to weave funeral palms.</t3154><t3155>She found it ransacked.</t3155><t3156>Only the essential furniture, the candelabras, and the silverware remained, because the household items had been sold, one by one, to cover the costs of her education.</t3156><t3157>Her mother had succumbed to the five o'clock fever.</t3157><t3158>Her father, Don Fernando, dressed in black, with a laminated anyone.</t3160><t3161>She never heard about the wars that bled the country dry.</t3161><t3162>She never stopped hearing the piano exercises at three in the afternoon.</t3162><t3163>She was even beginning to lose the illusion of being a queen when two peremptory knocks sounded at the gate, and she opened it to a dashing military man, with ceremonious manners, who had a scar on his cheek and a gold medal on his chest.</t3163><t3164>He locked himself in the office with her father.</t3164><t3165>Two hours later, her father came to fetch her from the sewing room.</t3165><t3166>"Prepare your things," he said.</t3166><t3167>"You have to take a long trip."</t3167><t3168>That was how they took her to Macondo.</t3168><t3169>In a single day, with a brutal blow, life threw upon her all the trying to erase the burn of that unheard-of mockery.</t3170><t3171>She had promised herself not to leave the bedroom until death, when Aureliano Segundo came to find her.</t3171><t3172>It was an fierce determination with which José Arcadio Buendía crossed the mountains to found Macondo, with the blind pride with which Colonel Aureliano Buendía promoted his useless wars, with the insane tenacity with which Úrsula ensured the survival of the lineage, Aureliano Segundo searched for Fernanda, without a single moment of discouragement.</t3175><t3176>When he asked where they sold funeral palms, they took him from house to house to choose the best ones.</t3176><t3177>When he asked where the most beautiful woman on earth was, all the mothers brought their daughters to him.</t3177><t3178>He got lost through foggy ravines, through times reserved for oblivion, through labyrinths of disillusionment.</t3178><t3179>He crossed a yellow wasteland where the echo repeated thoughts and anxiety caused premonitory mirages.</t3179><t3180>After weeks of fruitless searching, he arrived in an unknown city where all the bells were tolling for the dead.</t3180><t3181>Although he had never seen them, nor had anyone described them to him, he immediately recognized the walls corroded by the lime of bones, the decrepit balconies of wood gutted by fungi, and nailed to the gate and almost erased by the rain, the saddest little sign in the world: Funeral palms for sale.</t3181><t3182>From then until the cold morning when Fernanda left the house in the care of the Mother Superior, there was barely enough time for the nuns to sew the trousseau and pack the candelabras, the silverware, the golden chamber pot, and the countless and useless remnants of a family catastrophe that had taken two centuries to consummate into six trunks.</t3182><t3183>Don Fernando declined the invitation to accompany them.</t3183><t3184>He promised to come later, when he finished settling his commitments, and from the moment he blessed his daughter, he locked himself back in the office to write her letters with mourning borders and the family coat of arms that would be the
