<t3716>—If you can't, don't eat any more —said The Elephant—.</t3716> <t3717>We're tied.</t3717>
<t3718>She said it from the heart, understanding that she too could not eat another bite due to the remorse of facilitating the death of the adversary.</t3718> <t3719>But Aureliano Segundo interpreted it as a new challenge, and he stuffed himself with turkey beyond his incredible capacity.</t3719> <t3720>He lost consciousness.</t3720> <t3721>He fell face-first into the plate of bones, foaming at the mouth like a dog, and choking on agonizing snores.</t3721> <t3722>In the midst of the darkness, he felt as if he were being thrown from the top of a tower into a bottomless abyss, and in a final flash of lucidity, he realized that at the end of that endless fall, death was waiting for him.</t3722>
<t3723>—Take me to Fernanda —he managed to say.</t3723>
<t3724>The friends who left him at the house believed that he had fulfilled his promise to his wife not to die in the concubine's bed.</t3724> <t3725>Petra Cotes had polished the patent leather boots that he wanted to wear in the coffin, and she was already looking for someone to take them when they came to tell her that Aureliano Segundo was out of danger.</t3725> <t3726>He recovered, in fact, in less than a week, and fifteen days later he was celebrating the event of his survival with an unprecedented party.</t3726> <t3727>He continued living in Petra Cotes' house, but he visited Fernanda every day and sometimes stayed to eat with the family, as if fate had reversed the situation, leaving him as the husband of the concubine and the lover of the wife.</t3727>
<t3728>It was a relief for Fernanda.</t3728> <t3729>In the tedium of abandonment, her only distractions were clavichord exercises during siesta time and letters from her children.</t3729> <t3730>In the detailed notes she sent them every fifteen days, there was not a single line of truth.</t3730> <t3731>She hid her sorrows from them.</t3731> <t3732>She concealed the sadness of a house that, despite the light over the begonias, despite the suffocation of two in the afternoon, despite the frequent bursts of festivity that came from the street, was increasingly resembling the colonial mansion of her parents.</t3732> <t3733>Fernanda wandered alone among three living ghosts and the dead ghost of José Arcadio Buendía, who sometimes sat with an inquisitive attention in the dimness of the living room while she played the clavichord.</t3733> <t3734>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was a shadow.</t3734> <t3735>Since the last time he went out to propose a futile war to Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, he barely left the workshop except to urinate under the chestnut tree.</t3735> <t3736>He received no other visitors than the barber every three weeks.</t3736> <t3737>He fed on whatever Úrsula brought him once a day, and although he continued making little gold fishes with the same passion as before, he stopped selling them when he found out that people were not buying them as jewelry but as historical relics.</t3737> <t3738>He had made a bonfire in the yard with Remedios' dolls, which had decorated his bedroom since the day of their marriage.</t3738> <t3739>The vigilant Úrsula realized what her son was doing, but she could not stop him.</t3739>
<t3740>—You have a heart of stone —he told her.</t3740>
<t3741>—This is not a matter of the heart —he said—.</t3741> <t3742>The room is filling up with moths.</t3742>
<t3743>Amaranta was weaving her shroud.</t3743> <t3744>Fernanda did not understand why she wrote occasional letters to Meme, and even sent her gifts, yet did not even want to talk about José Arcadio.</t3744> <t3745>“They will die without knowing why,” Amaranta answered when she asked her the question through Úrsula, and that response planted an enigma in her heart that she could never unravel.</t3745> <t3746>Tall, slender, haughty, always dressed in abundant frothy petticoats and with an air of distinction that resisted the years and bad memories, Amaranta seemed to bear the ash cross of virginity on her forehead.</t3746> <t3747>In reality, she bore it in her hand, in the black bandage that she did not remove even to sleep, and which she herself washed and ironed.</t3747> <t3748>Her life was spent embroidering the shroud.</t3748> <t3749>One might have said that she embroidered during the day and unembroidered at night, not with the hope of defeating loneliness in that way, but quite the opposite, to sustain it.</t3749>
<t3750>The greatest concern Fernanda had during her years of abandonment was that Meme would come home for her first vacation and not find Aureliano Segundo in the house.</t3750> <t3751>The congestion put an end to that fear.</t3751> <t3752>When Meme returned, her parents had agreed not only to make the girl believe that Aureliano Segundo was still a domesticated husband, but also to ensure she did not notice the sadness in the house.</t3752> <t3753>Every year, for two months, Aureliano Segundo played his role as an exemplary husband and organized parties with ice cream and cookies, which the cheerful and lively student enlivened with the clavichord.</t3753> <t3754>It was evident from then on that she had inherited very little of her mother's character.</t3754> <t3755>She seemed more like a second version of Amaranta, when she did not yet know bitterness and would stir up the house with her dance steps, at twelve, at fourteen years old, before the secret passion for Pietro Crespi definitively twisted the course of her heart.</t3755> <t3756>But unlike Amaranta, unlike everyone else, Meme did not yet reveal the solitary fate of the family, and seemed entirely content with the world, even when she locked herself in the living room at two in the afternoon to practice the clavichord with inflexible discipline.</t3756> <t3757>It was evident that she liked the house, that she spent the whole year dreaming of the commotion of teenagers that her arrival caused, and that she was not far from the festive vocation and hospitable excesses of her father.</t3757> <t3758>The first sign of that calamitous inheritance was revealed during the third vacation, when Meme appeared at the house with four nuns and sixty-eight classmates, whom she invited to spend a week with the family, on her own initiative and without any prior announcement.</t3758>
<t3759>—What a misfortune! —lamented Fernanda—.</t3759> <t3760>This child is as barbaric as her father!</t3760>
<t3761>It was necessary to ask the neighbors for beds and hammocks, establish nine meal shifts, set bath schedules, and borrow forty stools so that the girls in blue uniforms and men's boots wouldn't spend the whole day fluttering from one side to the other.</t3761> <t3762>The invitation was a failure because the noisy schoolgirls had barely finished breakfast when they already had to start the shifts for lunch, and then for dinner, and in the whole week they could only take one walk to the plantations.</t3762> <t3763>At dusk, the nuns were exhausted, unable to move, to give another order, and still the horde of tireless adolescents was in the courtyard singing dull school hymns.</t3763> <t3764>One day they almost ran over Úrsula, who insisted on being useful precisely where she was most in the way.</t3764> <t3765>Another day, the nuns raised a commotion because Colonel Aureliano Buendía urinated under the chestnut tree without worrying that the schoolgirls were in the courtyard.</t3765> <t3766>Amaranta almost caused a panic because one of the nuns entered the kitchen when she was salting the soup, and the only thing that occurred to her was to ask what those handfuls of white powder were.</t3766>
<t3767>—Arsenic —said Amaranta.</t3767>
<t3768>The night of their arrival, the students got so tangled up trying to go to the toilet before going to bed that by one in the morning, the last ones were still entering.</t3768> <t3769>Fernanda then bought seventy-two chamber pots, but she only managed to turn the nighttime problem into a morning problem, because from dawn there was a long line of girls in front of the toilet, each with her chamber pot in hand, waiting for their turn to wash it.</t3769> <t3770>Although some suffered from fevers and several had mosquito bites that got infected, the majority showed unbreakable resilience in the face of the most painful difficulties, and even during the hottest hours, they ran around in the garden.</t3770> <t3771>When they finally left, the flowers were destroyed, the furniture was broken, and the walls were covered with drawings and graffiti, but Fernanda forgave the damage in the relief of their departure.</t3771> <t3772>She returned the borrowed beds and stools and stored the seventy-two chamber pots in Melquíades' room.</t3772> <t3773>The sealed room, around which the spiritual life of the house once revolved, was known from then on as the chamber pot room.</t3773> <t3774>For Colonel Aureliano Buendía, that was the most appropriate name, because while the rest of the family continued to be amazed that Melquíades' room was immune to dust and destruction, he saw it turned into a dump.</t3774> <t3775>Anyway, he didn't seem to care who was right, and if he found out about the room's fate, it was because Fernanda spent an entire afternoon passing by and disturbing his work to store the chamber pots.</t3775>
<t3776>In those days, José Arcadio Segundo reappeared in the house.</t3776> <t3777>He would walk through the corridor without greeting anyone and lock himself in the workshop to converse with the colonel.</t3777> <t3778>Even though she couldn't see him, Úrsula analyzed the clicking of his foreman's boots and was surprised by the insurmountable distance that separated him from the family, even from his twin brother with whom he had played clever games of confusion in childhood and with whom he no longer shared any common traits.</t3778> <t3779>He was linear, solemn, and had a thoughtful demeanor, a Saracen's sadness, and a gloomy glow on his autumn-colored face.</t3779> <t3780>He was the one who most resembled his mother, Santa Sofía de la Piedad.</t3780> <t3781>Úrsula reproached herself for the tendency to forget about him when talking about the family, but when she felt him in the house again and noticed that the colonel admitted him into the workshop during working hours, she reexamined her old memories and confirmed the belief that at some point in childhood he had switched places with his twin brother, because it was he and not the other who should have been named Aureliano.</t3781> <t3782>Nobody knew the details of his life.</t3782> <t3783>At one time, it was known that he didn't have a fixed residence, that he raised roosters at Pilar Ternera's house, and that sometimes he stayed there to sleep, but that he almost always spent the night in the rooms of the French matrons.</t3783> <t3784>He wandered aimlessly, without affections, without ambitions, like a wandering star in Úrsula's planetary system.</t3784>
<t3785>In reality, José Arcadio Segundo was not a member of the family, nor would he ever be of another, since the distant dawn when Colonel Gerineldo Márquez took him to the barracks, not to witness an execution, but so that he would never forget for the rest of his life the sad and slightly mocking smile of the executed man.</t3785> <t3786>That was not only his oldest memory, but the only one from his childhood.</t3786> <t3787>The other, of an old man with an anachronistic vest and a hat with crow wings who told wonders in front of a dazzling window, he could not place in any time period.</t3787> <t3788>It was an uncertain memory, entirely devoid of lessons or nostalgia, unlike the memory of the executed man, which had actually defined the course of his life and returned to his mind increasingly clearer as he aged, as if the passage of time had brought it closer.</t3788> <t3789>Úrsula tried to make use of José Arcadio Segundo to get Colonel Aureliano Buendía to abandon his seclusion.</t3789> <t3790>“Convince him to go to the movies,” she would say.</t3790> <t3791>“Even if he doesn't like the films, at least he will have a chance to breathe fresh air.”</t3791> <t3792>But she soon realized that he was as insensitive to her pleas as the Colonel could have been, and that they were armored by the same impermeability to affections.</t3792> <t3793>Although she never knew, nor did anyone, what they talked about in the prolonged seclusions of the workshop, she understood that they were the only members of the family who seemed linked by affinities.</t3793>
<t3794>The truth is that not even José Arcadio Segundo could have gotten the colonel out of his seclusion.</t3794><t3795>The school invasion had exceeded the limits of his patience.</t3795><t3796>With the pretext that the bridal bedroom was at the mercy of moths despite the destruction of Remedios' appetizing dolls, he hung a hammock in the workshop, and then he only left it to go to the patio to <t3821>—What rain! —said Úrsula.</t3821>
<t3822>—October —he said.</t3822>
<t3823>When he said it, he did not lift his gaze from the first little fish of the day, because he was setting the rubies in its eyes.</t3823> <t3824>Only when he finished it and placed it with the others in the jar did he start to eat the soup.</t3824> <t3825>Then he ate, very slowly, the piece of meat stewed with onions, the white rice, and the slices of fried plantain, all together on the same plate.</t3825> <t3826>His appetite was unaffected by either the best or the harshest circumstances.</t3826> <t3827>At the end of lunch, he experienced the anxiety of idleness.</t3827> <t3828>Due to a kind of scientific superstition, he never worked, read, bathed, or made love before two hours of digestion had passed, and this belief was so ingrained that he had several times delayed military operations to avoid exposing the troops to the risks of congestion.</t3828> <t3829>So he lay down in the hammock, cleaning the wax from his ears with a penknife, and within a few minutes, he fell asleep.</t3829> <t3830>He dreamed that he was entering an empty house with white walls, and he was troubled by the burden of being the first human being to enter it.</t3830> <t3831>In the dream, he remembered that he had dreamed the same thing the night before and on many nights over the past years, and he knew that the image would have been erased from his memory upon waking, because that recurring dream had the virtue of being remembered only within the dream itself.</t3831> <t3832>A moment later, indeed, when the barber knocked on the workshop door, Colonel Aureliano Buendía woke up with the impression that he had involuntarily fallen asleep for a few seconds and had not had time to dream anything.</t3832>

<t3835>He had a three-day beard, speckled with white fuzz, but he didn't think it necessary to shave if he was going to get a haircut on Friday and could do it all at once.</t3835> <t3836>The sticky <t3852>Instead of going to the chestnut tree, Colonel Aureliano Buendía also went to the street door and mingled with the onlookers who were watching the parade.</t3852> <t3853>He saw a woman dressed in gold on the nape of an elephant.</t3853> <t3854>He saw a sad dromedary.</t3854> <t3855>He saw a bear dressed as a Dutch woman marking the rhythm of the music with a ladle and a saucepan.</t3855> <t3856>He saw the clowns doing somersaults at the tail end of the parade, and he saw the miserable face of his loneliness again when everything had passed, and only the luminous space on the street remained, and the air filled with flying ants, and a few onlookers peering into the abyss of uncertainty.</t3856> <t3857>Then he went to the chestnut tree, thinking about the circus, and while he urinated he tried to keep thinking about the circus, but he could no longer find the memory.</t3857> <t3858>He tucked his head between his shoulders, like a little chick, and remained motionless with his forehead resting on the trunk of the chestnut tree.</t3858> <t3859>The family did not find out until the next day, at eleven in the morning, when Santa Sofía de la Piedad went to throw out the trash in the backyard and was struck by the sight of the buzzards descending.</t3859>
<t3860>Meme's last vacation coincided with the mourning for the death of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t3860> <t3861>In the closed house, there was no room for parties.</t3861> <t3862>People spoke in whispers, ate in silence, prayed the rosary three times a day, and even the clavichord exercises in the heat of the siesta had a funeral resonance.</t3862> <t3863>Despite her secret hostility towards the colonel, it was Fernanda who imposed the rigor of that mourning, impressed by the solemnity with which the government exalted the memory of the dead enemy.</t3863> <t3864>Aureliano Segundo returned as usual to sleep in the house while his daughter's vacation lasted, and Fernanda must have done something to regain her privileges as a legitimate wife, because the following year Meme found a newborn little sister, who was baptized, against the mother's will, with the name Amaranta Úrsula.</t3864>
<t3865>Meme had finished her studies.</t3865><t3866>The diploma that certified her as a harpsichord concert performer was ratified by the virtuosity with which she played popular 17th-century themes at the party organized to celebrate the completion of her studies and with which the mourning period was ended.</t3866><t3867>The guests admired, more than her art, her rare duality.</t3867><t3868>Her frivolous and even somewhat childish character did not seem suitable for any serious activity, but when she sat at the harpsichord she transformed into a different girl, whose unexpected maturity gave <t3888>—What’s wrong? —he asked.</t3888>
<t3889>—Nothing —Meme replied—.</t3889> <t3890>That only now I discover how much I love them.</t3890>
<t3891>Amaranta was frightened by the evident load of hatred carried by the declaration.</t3891><t3892>But Fernanda was so moved that she thought she would go crazy when Meme woke up at midnight with lucid, was the only one who intuited the exact diagnosis.</t3895><t3896>"To me," she thought, "these are the same things that happen to drunkards."</t3896><t3897>But she not only rejected the idea, <t3925>Among Meme's friends, there were three young American women who broke through the electrified chicken coop fence and established friendships with the girls of Macondo.</t3925> <t3926>One of <t3938>Aureliano Segundo was so enthusiastic about his daughter's progress that he bought from a traveling salesman an English encyclopedia in six volumes with numerous color plates, which Meme read in her free time.</t3938> <t3939>Reading occupied the attention she previously devoted to lovers' gossip or experimental seclusions with her friends, not because she had imposed it as a discipline, Amaranta was embroidering her endless shroud, and Úrsula was being dragged by decrepitude into the depths of darkness, where the only visible thing was the specter of José Arcadio Buendía under the <t3951>It might have been said that in the weary mansion of the Buendías there was peace and routine happiness for a long time if Amaranta's untimely death had not caused a new scandal.</t3951> <t3952>It was an unexpected event.</t3952> <t3953>Although she was old and isolated from everyone, she still appeared firm and upright, with the stone-like health she had always had.</t3953> <t3954>Nobody knew her thoughts since the afternoon she definitively rejected Colonel Gerineldo Márquez and locked herself in to cry.</t3954> <t3955>When she came out, she had exhausted all her tears.</t3955> <t3956>She was not seen crying at the ascension of Remedios the Beauty, nor at the extermination of the Aurelianos, nor at the death of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, who was the person she loved most in this world, although she could only show it when they found his body under the chestnut tree.</t3956> <t3957>She helped lift the body.</t3957> <t3958>She dressed him in his warrior gear, shaved him, combed him, and glued his mustache better than he had ever done in his years of glory.</t3958> <t3959>Nobody thought there was love in that act, because they were used to Amaranta's familiarity with the rites of death.</t3959> <t3960>Fernanda was scandalized that she did not understand the relations of Catholicism with life, but only its relations with death, as if it were not a religion, but a prospectus of funeral conventions.</t3960> <t3961>Amaranta was too entangled in the thicket of her memories to understand those apologetic subtleties.</t3961> <t3962>She had reached old age with all her nostalgias alive.</t3962> <t3963>When she listened to Pietro Crespi's waltzes, she felt the same desire to cry that she had in her adolescence, as if time and lessons had served no purpose.</t3963> <t3964>The music rolls she herself had thrown away, under the pretext that they were rotting with humidity, kept spinning and striking hammers in her memory.</t3964> <t3965>She had tried to sink them in the swampy passion she allowed herself with her nephew Aureliano José, and had tried to take refuge in the serene and virile protection of Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, but she had not managed to defeat them even with the most desperate act of her old age, when she bathed little José Arcadio three years before he was sent to the seminary, and caressed him not as a grandmother would with a grandson, but as a woman would with a man, as it was said French matrons did, and as she had wanted to do with Pietro Crespi, at twelve, fourteen years old, when she saw him with his dancing pants and the magic wand with which he kept the metronome's beat.</t3965> <t3966>Sometimes it hurt her to have left that trail of misery in her wake, and sometimes she was so angry that she Colonel Aureliano Buendía thought about the war, unable to avoid it, Amaranta thought about Rebeca.</t3967> <t3968>But while her brother had managed to sterilize the memories, she had only managed to noticed the progress of the destruction, she was pleased with the idea that God was listening to her.</t3970> <t3971>One afternoon, while sewing in the corridor, she was struck by the certainty that letter, and it was true that at one time she would rip off buttons to sew them back on, so that idleness would not make the wait longer and more distressing.</t3972> <t3973>Nobody in the house noticed that Amaranta then wove a beautiful shroud for Rebeca.</t3973> <t3974>Later, when Aureliano Triste said he had seen her turned into an apparition, with cracked skin and a few yellowish strands on her death.</t3977> <t3978>The only thing she did not take into account in her tremendous plan was that, despite her pleas to God, she might die before Rebeca.</t3978> <t3979>And so it happened, indeed.</t3979> <t3980>But in the final moment, Amaranta did not feel frustrated, but on the contrary, liberated from all bitterness, because death granted her the privilege of announcing itself several years in advance.</t3980> <t3981>She saw it one scorching midday, sewing with her in the corridor, shortly after Meme had gone to school.</t3981> <t3982>She recognized it immediately, and to prolong the work beyond Rebeca's death, but the same concentration provided her with the calm she needed to accept the idea of frustration.</t3989> <t3990>It was then that she understood the vicious circle of Colonel Aureliano Buendía's goldfish.</t3990> <t3991>The world was reduced to the surface of her skin, and the interior was safe from all bitterness.</t3991> <t3992>It pained her not to have had that revelation many years earlier, when it was still possible to purify memories and rebuild the universe under a new light, and to evoke without shuddering the lavender scent of Pietro Crespi at dusk, and to rescue Rebeca from her mire of misery, not out of hatred or love, but out of boundless understanding of solitude.</t3992> <t3993>The hatred she noticed one night in Meme's words <t4001>The news that Amaranta Buendía was setting sail at dusk carrying the mail of death spread in Macondo before noon, and by three in the afternoon there was a box full of letters in the living <t4026>—Let no one have any illusions —she shouted, so that Fernanda could hear her—.</t4026> <t4027>Amaranta Buendía is leaving this world as she came.</t4027>
<t4028>She did not get up again.</t4028> <t4029>Reclining on cushions, as if she were truly ill, she braided her long hair and coiled it over her ears, just as death had told her she should be in the coffin.</t4029> <t4030>Then she asked Úrsula for a mirror, and for the first time in more than forty years, she saw her face ravaged by age and suffering, and she was surprised at how much it resembled the mental image she had of herself.</t4030> <t4031>Úrsula understood by the silence in the room that it had begun to darken.</t4031>

<t4035>Meme couldn't help but think of her when they turned on the lights of the makeshift stage and the second part of the program began.</t4035> <t4036>Halfway through the piece, someone whispered the news in her ear, and the act was suspended.</t4036> <t4037>When he arrived at the house, Aureliano Segundo had to push his way through the crowd to see the corpse of the old maiden, ugly and discolored, with the black bandage in her hand and wrapped in the exquisite shroud.</t4037> <t4038>It was displayed in the living room next to the mail chest.</t4038>
<t4039>Úrsula did not get up again after Amaranta's nine nights.</t4039> <t4040>Santa Sofía de la Piedad took care of her.</t4040> <t4041>She brought her food to the bedroom, and bija water for her to wash, and kept her informed of everything that happened in Macondo.</t4041> <t4042>Aureliano Segundo visited her frequently, and brought clothes that she placed near the bed, along with the most essential things for daily living, so that in a short time she had built a world within arm's reach.</t4042> <t4043>She managed to awaken a great affection in little Amaranta Úrsula, who was identical to her, and whom she taught to read.</t4043> <t4044>Her lucidity, the ability to fend for herself, made it seem that she was naturally overcome by the weight of a hundred years, but although it was evident that her eyesight was failing, no one suspected that she was completely blind.</t4044> <t4045>She then had so much time and so much inner silence to watch over the life of the house, that she was the first to notice Meme's quiet tribulation.</t4045>
<t4046>—Come here —he said to her—.</t4046> <t4047>Now that we are alone, confess to this poor old woman what is happening to you.</t4047>
<t4048>Meme avoided the conversation with a nervous laugh.</t4048> <t4049>Úrsula did not insist, but she confirmed her suspicions when Meme did not visit her again.</t4049> <t4050>She knew that Meme got ready earlier than usual, that she did not have a moment of peace while waiting to go out, that she spent entire nights tossing and turning in the adjacent bedroom, and that she was tormented by the fluttering of a butterfly.</t4050> <t4051>On one occasion, she heard her say that she was going to meet Aureliano Segundo, and Úrsula was surprised that Fernanda was so unimaginative that she did not suspect anything when her husband came to the house to ask about their daughter.</t4051> <t4052>It was too obvious that Meme was involved in secret matters, urgent commitments, and repressed anxieties, long before the night when Fernanda caused a commotion in the house because she found her kissing a man at the cinema.</t4052>
<t4053>Meme herself was so self-absorbed at the time that she accused Úrsula of having denounced her.</t4053> <t4054>In reality, she denounced herself.</t4054> <t4055>For a long time, she had been leaving a trail of clues that would have awakened even the most oblivious, and if Fernanda took so long to discover them, it was because she too was dazzled by her secret relationships with the invisible doctors.</t4055> <t4056>Even so, she eventually noticed the deep silences, the sudden startles, the mood swings, and the contradictions of her daughter.</t4056> <t4057>She committed herself to a discreet but relentless surveillance.</t4057> <t4058>She let her go out with her usual friends, helped her dress for the Saturday parties, and never asked her an impertinent question that might alert her.</t4058> <t4059>She already had many proofs that Meme did different things from what she announced, and still did not reveal her suspicions, waiting for the decisive moment.</t4059> <t4060>One night, Meme told her that she was going to the cinema with her father.</t4060> <t4061>Shortly after, Fernanda heard the fireworks of the party and the unmistakable accordion of Aureliano Segundo in the direction of Petra Cotes.</t4061> <t4062>Then she got dressed, went to the cinema, and in the dim light of the seats, she recognized her daughter.</t4062> <t4063>The overwhelming emotion of being right prevented her from seeing the man she was kissing, but she managed to perceive his trembling voice amidst the catcalls and deafening laughter of the audience.</t4063> <t4064>“I'm sorry, love,” she heard him say, and she took Meme out of the hall without saying a word, subjected her to the shame of walking her through the bustling Calle de los Turcos, and locked her in the bedroom.</t4064>
<t4065>The next day, at six in the evening, Fernanda recognized the voice of the man who came to visit her.</t4065> <t4066>He was young, sallow, with dark and melancholic eyes that would not have surprised her so much if she had known the gypsies, and a dreamy air that any woman with a less rigid heart would have understood the reasons for her daughter.</t4066> <t4067>He was dressed in very worn linen, with shoes desperately defended with superimposed zinc white patches, and he carried in his hand a boater hat bought the previous Saturday.</t4067> <t4068>In his life, he had never been nor would he ever be more frightened than at that moment, but he had a dignity and composure that kept him safe from humiliation, and a legitimate presence that only failed in his chapped hands and nails splintered by rough work.</t4068> <t4069>Fernanda, however, only needed to see him once to sense his working-class condition.</t4069> <t4070>She realized he was wearing his only Sunday outfit, and that beneath his shirt, his skin was corroded by the scabies from the banana company.</t4070> <t4071>She did not allow him to speak.</t4071> <t4072>She did not even allow him to pass through the door, which she had to close a moment later because the house was filled with yellow butterflies.</t4072>
<t4073>—Get out —he said to him—.</t4073> <t4074>You have no business coming here among decent people.</t4074>
<t4075>His name was Mauricio Babilonia.</t4075> <t4076>He was born and raised in Macondo, and he was an apprentice mechanic in the banana company's workshops.</t4076> <t4077>Meme had met him by she dreamed that he saved her from a shipwreck and she felt not gratitude but anger.</t4087> <t4088>It was like giving him an opportunity he desired, while Meme longed for the opposite, not only with <t4097>—I came to see the new models —said Meme.</t4097>
<t4098>—It's a good excuse —he said.</t4098>
<t4099>Meme realized that she was burning in the fire of her pride, and desperately sought a way to humiliate him.</t4099> <t4100>But he didn't give her time. <t4117>—He is a very strange man —said Fernanda—.</t4117> <t4118>You can see it in his face that he is going to die.</t4118>
<t4119>Meme thought that her mother had been impressed by the butterflies.</t4119> <t4120>When they finished pruning the rosebush, she washed her hands and took the package to the bedroom to open it.</t4120> <t4121>It was a kind of Chinese toy, composed of five concentric boxes, and in the last one, a card laboriously drawn by someone who could barely write:</t4121> <t4122>See you on Saturday at the cinema.</t4122> <t4123>Meme felt the belated astonishment that the box had been on the banister for so long within Fernanda's reach, and although she was flattered by Mauricio Babilonia's audacity and ingenuity, she was moved by his naivety in expecting her to keep the date.</t4123> <t4124>Meme knew since then that Aureliano Segundo had a commitment on Saturday night.</t4124> <t4125>However, the fire of anxiety burned her so much during the week that on Saturday she convinced her father to leave her alone at the theater and come back for her when the show was over.</t4125> <t4126>A moth fluttered over her head while the lights were on.</t4126> <t4127>And then it happened.</t4127> <t4128>When the lights went out, Mauricio Babilonia sat next to her.</t4128> <t4129>Meme felt as if she were floundering in a quagmire of anxiety, from which only that man smelling of motor oil, whom she could barely distinguish in the dim light, could rescue her, as had happened in the dream.</t4129>
<t4130>—If you hadn't come —he said—, you would never have seen me again.</t4130>
<t4131>Meme felt the weight of his hand on her knee, and she knew that both of them, at that moment, had reached the other side of desolation.</t4131>
<t4132>—What shocks me about you —he smiled— is that you always say precisely what shouldn't be said.</t4132>
<t4133>She went crazy for him.</t4133><t4134>She lost sleep and appetite, and sank so deeply into loneliness that even her father became a hindrance to her.</t4134><t4135>She devised an intricate web <t4158>The night Fernanda caught them at the cinema, Aureliano Segundo felt overwhelmed by the weight of his conscience, and visited Meme in the bedroom where Fernanda had locked her up, hoping that she would confide in him the secrets she owed him.</t4158> <t4159>But Meme denied everything.</t4159> <t4160>She was so sure of herself, so clinging to her solitude, that Aureliano Segundo had the impression that there was no longer any bond between them, that the camaraderie and complicity were nothing more than an illusion of the past.</t4160> <t4161>He thought about talking to Mauricio Babilonia, believing that his authority as a former boss would make him give up his intentions, but Petra Cotes convinced him that those were women's matters, so he remained floating in a limbo of indecision, barely sustained by the hope that the confinement would end his daughter's tribulations.</t4161>
<t4162>Meme showed no sign of affliction.</t4162> <t4163>On the contrary, from the adjoining bedroom, Úrsula perceived the calm rhythm of her sleep, the serenity of her tasks, the order of her meals, and the good health of her digestion.</t4163> <t4164>The only thing that intrigued Úrsula after almost two months of punishment was that Meme did not bathe in the morning, as everyone else did, but at <t4177>The events that were to deal the mortal blow to Macondo began to come into view when they brought Meme Buendía's son to the house.</t4177> <t4178>The public situation was so uncertain at the time that no one had the spirit to deal with private scandals, so Fernanda had a favorable environment to keep the child hidden as if he had never existed.</t4178> <t4179>She had to take him in because the circumstances in which he was brought made rejection impossible.</t4179> <t4180>She had to endure him against her will for the rest of her life because, when it came down to it, she lacked the courage to carry out her private determination to drown him in the bath pool.</t4180> <t4181>She locked him in Colonel Aureliano Buendía's old workshop.</t4181> <t4182>She managed to convince Santa Sofía de la Piedad that she had found him floating in a basket.</t4182> <t4183>Úrsula was to die without knowing his origin.</t4183> <t4184>Little Amaranta Úrsula, who once entered the workshop when Fernanda was feeding the child, also believed the story of the floating basket.</t4184> <t4185>Aureliano Segundo, definitively estranged from his wife due to the irrational way she handled Meme's tragedy, did not learn of his grandson's existence until three years after he was brought to the house, when the child escaped captivity due to Fernanda's carelessness and peeked into the hallway for a fraction of a second, naked and with tangled hair and an impressive turkey-cock sex, as if he were not a human child but the encyclopedic definition of an anthropophage.</t4185>
<t4186>Fernanda did not count on that prank from her incorrigible destiny.</t4186> <t4187>The child was like the return of a shame she thought she had banished from the house forever.</t4187> <t4188>They had barely taken Mauricio Babilonia away with a fractured spine, and Fernanda had already conceived down to the smallest detail a plan to eliminate every trace of the disgrace.</t4188> <t4189>Without consulting her husband, she packed her bags the next day, put the three changes of clothes her daughter might need into a small suitcase, and went to fetch her from the bedroom half an hour before the train's arrival.</t4189>
<t4190>—Let's go, Renata —he said to her.</t4190>
<t4191>He gave her no explanation.</t4191> <t4192>Meme, for her part, neither expected nor wanted one.</t4192> <t4193>Not only did she not know where they were going, but it would have made no <t4204>At five in the afternoon, when they arrived at the final station of the swamp, he got off the train because Fernanda did.</t4204><t4205>They got into a little carriage that looked like a huge <t4221>Meme took her hand and let herself be led.</t4221> <t4222>The last time Fernanda saw her, trying to match her pace with that of the novice, the iron gate of the cloister had just closed behind her.</t4222> <t4223>She was still thinking of Mauricio Babilonia, of his smell of oil and his realm of butterflies, and she would continue thinking of him every day of her life, until the distant autumn dawn when she would die of old age, with their names changed and never having spoken a word, in a gloomy hospital in Krakow.</t4223>
<t4224>Fernanda returned to Macondo on a train protected by armed police.</t4224> <t4225>During the journey, she noticed the tension of the passengers, the military preparations in the towns along the line, and the air thick with the certainty that something serious was going to happen, but she lacked information until she arrived in Macondo and was told that José Arcadio Segundo was inciting the banana company workers to strike.</t4225> <t4226>“This is the last thing we needed,” Fernanda said to herself.</t4226> <t4227>“An anarchist in the family.”</t4227> <t4228>The strike broke out two weeks later and did not have the dramatic consequences that were feared.</t4228> <t4229>The workers hoped that they would not be forced to cut and ship bananas on Sundays, and the request seemed so <t4236>—Just like Aureliano —exclaimed Úrsula—.</t4236> <t4237>It's as if the world were spinning around.</t4237>
<t4238>Fernanda remained immune to the uncertainty of those days.</t4238> <t4239>She lacked contact with the outside world since the violent altercation she had with her husband for having determined <t4243>Unconcerned by the town's unrest, deaf to Úrsula's dire predictions, Fernanda gave the final turn to the screws of her accomplished plan.</t4243> <t4244>She wrote a lengthy letter to her son José Arcadio, who was about to receive minor orders, informing him that his sister Renata had passed away in the peace of the Lord due to black vomit.</t4244> <t4245>Then she placed Amaranta Úrsula in the care of Santa Sofía de la Piedad and dedicated herself to organizing her correspondence with the invisible doctors, disturbed by Meme's mishap.</t4245> <t4246>The first thing she did was set a <t4248>She was so urgent and so poorly informed that she explained in another letter that there was no such state of agitation, and that it was all the result of the madness of a brother-in-law of hers, who was going through a phase of unionist fervor, just as he had previously gone through phases of cockfighting and navigation.</t4248> <t4249>They still had not agreed on the hot Wednesday when Buendía.</t4251> <t4252>It was Meme's child.</t4252> <t4253>Fernanda's former spiritual director explained in a letter that the child had been born two months earlier, and they had taken the liberty <t4255>—We'll say we found him floating in the basket —he smiled.</t4255>
<t4256>—No one will believe it —said the nun.</t4256>
<t4257>—If they believed the Holy Scriptures —replied Fernanda—, I don't see why they shouldn't believe me.</t4257>
<t4258>The nun had lunch at home while the returning train passed by, and in accordance with the discretion that had been demanded of her, she did not mention the child again. However, Fernanda saw her as an undesirable witness to her shame and regretted that the medieval custom of hanging the bearer of bad news had been discarded.</t4258> <t4259>It was then that she decided to drown the child in the pool as soon as the nun left, but her heart couldn't bear it, and she preferred to patiently wait for God's infinite kindness to free her from the burden.</t4259>
<t4260>The new Aureliano had turned one year old when the public tension erupted without any warning.</t4260> <t4261>José Arcadio Segundo and other union leaders who had remained in hiding until then appeared unexpectedly one weekend and promoted demonstrations in the towns of the banana zone.</t4261> <t4262>The police were content to monitor the order.</t4262> <t4263>But on Monday night, the leaders were taken from their homes and sent with five-kilo shackles on their feet to the provincial capital's jail.</t4263> <t4264>Among them were José Arcadio Segundo and Lorenzo Gavilán, a colonel of the Mexican revolution, exiled in Macondo, who claimed to have witnessed the heroism of his comrade Artemio Cruz.</t4264> <t4265>However, within three months they were free, because the government and the banana company could not agree on who should feed them in jail.</t4265> <t4266>The workers' discontent this time was based on the unsanitary housing, the deceit of medical services, and the inequity of working conditions.</t4266> <t4267>They also claimed that they were not paid in cash, but with vouchers that were only good for buying Virginia ham at the company's commissaries.</t4267> <t4268>José Arcadio Segundo was imprisoned because he revealed that the voucher system was a company scheme to finance their fruit ships, which would have had to return empty from New Orleans to the banana shipping ports if not for the commissary goods.</t4268> <t4269>The other charges were public knowledge.</t4269> <t4270>The company's doctors did not examine the sick, but made them stand in single file in front of the dispensaries, and a nurse would place a pill the color of piedralipe on their tongues, whether they had malaria, gonorrhea, or constipation.</t4270> <t4271>It was such a widespread therapy that children would get in line several times, and instead of swallowing the pills, they would take them home to mark the numbers called in the lottery game.</t4271> <t4272>The company workers were crammed into miserable shacks.</t4272> <t4273>The engineers, instead of building latrines, brought a portable toilet for every fifty people to the camps at Christmas and gave public demonstrations on how to use them to make them last longer.</t4273> <t4274>The decrepit lawyers dressed in black who once harassed Colonel Aureliano Buendía, and who were then agents of the banana company, discredited these charges with tricks that seemed like magic.</t4274> <t4275>When the workers drafted a unanimous list of demands, a long time passed without being able to officially notify the banana company.</t4275> <t4276>As soon as he learned of the agreement, Mr. Brown hooked his luxurious glass car to the train and disappeared from Macondo along with the most well-known representatives of his company.</t4276> <t4277>However, several workers found one of them the following Saturday in a brothel and made him sign a copy of the list of demands while he was naked with the woman who had lured him into the trap.</t4277> <t4278>The mournful lawyers proved in court that the man had nothing to do with the company, and to ensure no one doubted their arguments, they had him imprisoned for impersonation.</t4278> <t4279>Later, Mr. Brown was caught traveling incognito in a third-class car and was made to sign another copy of the list of demands.</t4279> <t4280>The next day, he appeared before the judges with his hair dyed black and speaking flawless Spanish.</t4280> <t4281>The lawyers proved that he was not Mr. Jack Brown, superintendent of the banana company and born in Prattville, Alabama, but an innocent medicinal plant seller, born in Macondo and baptized there with the name Dagoberto Fonseca.</t4281> <t4282>Shortly after, in response to a new attempt by the workers, the lawyers displayed in public places the death certificate of Mr. Brown, authenticated by consuls and chancellors, which certified that on the past June ninth, he had been run over by a fire truck in Chicago.</t4282> <t4283>Tired of that hermeneutic delirium, the workers repudiated the authorities of Macondo and took their complaints to the supreme courts.</t4283> <t4284>It was there that the legal illusionists demonstrated that the claims were entirely invalid, simply because the banana company did not have, had never had, and would never have workers in its service, but recruited them occasionally and temporarily.</t4284> <t4285>Thus, the charade of the Virginia ham, the miraculous pills, and the Easter toilets was dismantled, and it was established by court ruling and solemnly proclaimed that the workers did not exist.</t4285>
<t4286>The great strike broke out.</t4286> <t4287>The crops were left half-finished, the fruit overripe on the vines, and the trains with one hundred and twenty cars stopped on the branches.</t4287> <t4288>Idle workers overflowed the towns.</t4288> <t4289>The Street of the Turks reverberated in a Saturday of many days, and in the billiard room of Jacob's Hotel, twenty-four-hour shifts had to be established.</t4289> <t4290>There was José Arcadio Segundo, on the day it was announced that the army had been tasked with restoring public order.</t4290> <t4291>Although he was not a man of omens, the news was for him like an announcement of death, which he had awaited since the distant morning when Colonel Gerineldo Márquez allowed him to witness an execution.</t4291> <t4292>However, the bad omen did not alter his solemnity.</t4292> <t4293>He made the move he had planned and did not miss the carom.</t4293> <t4294>Shortly after, the drum rolls, the trumpet barks, the shouts, and the clamor of the people indicated to him that not only the billiard game but also the silent and solitary game he had been playing with himself since the dawn of the execution had finally ended.</t4294> <t4295>Then he looked out onto the street and saw them.</t4295> <t4296>They were three regiments whose march, paced by the drum of galley slaves, made the ground tremble.</t4296> <t4297>Their multi-headed dragon breath impregnated the midday clarity with a pestilent vapor.</t4297> <t4298>They were small, stocky, brutish.</t4298> <t4299>They sweated horse sweat, and had the smell of meat macerated by the sun, and the taciturn and impenetrable impassivity of men from the wasteland.</t4299> <t4300>Although they took more than an hour to pass, one might have thought they were a few squads turning in circles, because they were all identical, children of the same mother, and all bore with equal stolidity the weight of the knapsacks and canteens, and the shame of the rifles with bayonets fixed, and the nuisance of blind obedience and the sense of honor.</t4300> <t4301>Úrsula heard them pass from her bed of darkness and raised her hand with her fingers in a cross.</t4301> <t4302>Santa Sofía de la Piedad existed for an instant, bent over the embroidered tablecloth she had just ironed, and thought of her son, José Arcadio Segundo, who watched the last soldiers pass by the door of Jacob's Hotel without flinching.</t4302>
<t4303>Martial law empowered the army to assume the role of arbitrator in the controversy, but no attempt at conciliation was made.</t4303><t4304>As soon as they appeared in Macondo, the soldiers set aside their rifles, cut and loaded the bananas, and mobilized the trains.</t4304><t4305>The workers, who until then had been content to wait, took to the hills with no more weapons than their work machetes, and began to sabotage the sabotage.</t4305><t4306>They set fire to farms and commissaries, destroyed the rails to prevent the passage of trains that were starting to make their way with machine gun fire, and cut the telegraph and telephone wires.</t4306><t4307>The ditches were stained with blood.</t4307><t4308>Mr. Brown, who was alive in the electrified chicken coop, was taken out of Macondo with his family and those of other compatriots, and escorted to safe territory under the protection of the army.</t4308><t4309>The situation threatened to evolve into an unequal and bloody civil war when the authorities called on the workers to gather in Macondo.</t4309><t4310>The call announced that the Civil and Military Chief of the province would arrive the following Friday, ready to intercede in the conflict.</t4310>
<t4311>José Arcadio Segundo was among the crowd that gathered at the station since Friday morning.</t4311> <t4312>He had participated in a meeting of the union leaders and had been commissioned along with Colonel Gavilán to blend in with the crowd and guide it according to the circumstances.</t4312> <t4313>He didn't feel well, and he kneaded a salty paste in his palate, ever since he noticed that the army had set up machine gun nests around the square, and that the barbed-wired city of the banana company was protected with artillery pieces.</t4313> <t4314>By noon, waiting for a train that did not arrive, more than three thousand people, including workers, women, and children, had overflowed the open space in front of the station and were crowding the adjacent streets that the army had closed off with rows of machine guns.</t4314> <t4315>It seemed then, more than a reception, a joyous fair.</t4315> <t4316>They had moved the food stalls and drink shops from the Street of the Turks, and people endured the annoyance of waiting and the scorching sun with very good spirits.</t4316> <t4317>A little before three, a rumor spread that the official train would not arrive until the next day.</t4317> <t4318>The tired crowd exhaled a sigh of discouragement.</t4318> <t4319>An army lieutenant then climbed onto the roof of the station, where there were four machine gun nests aimed at the crowd, and called for silence.</t4319> <t4320>Next to José Arcadio Segundo was a very fat, barefoot woman with two children about four and seven years old.</t4320> <t4321>She picked up the younger one and asked José Arcadio Segundo, without knowing him, to lift the other so he could hear better what was going to be said.</t4321> <t4322>José Arcadio Segundo hoisted the child onto his shoulders.</t4322> <t4323>Many years later, that child would continue to tell, without anyone believing him, that he had seen the lieutenant reading with a gramophone horn the Decree Number 4 of the Civil and Military Chief of the province.</t4323> <t4324>It was signed by General Carlos Cortes Vargas, and by his secretary, Major Enrique García Isaza, and in three articles of eighty words, it declared the strikers a gang of criminals and empowered the army to shoot them.</t4324>
<t4325>After the decree was read, amidst a deafening jeer of protest, a captain replaced the lieutenant on the station roof, and with the gramophone horn signaled that he wanted to speak.</t4325> <t4326>The crowd fell silent again.</t4326>
<t4327>—Ladies and gentlemen —said the captain in a low, slow, somewhat tired voice—, you have five minutes to leave.</t4327>
<t4328>The jeering and the redoubled shouts drowned out the bugle call that announced the beginning of the deadline.</t4328> <t4329>Nobody moved.</t4329>
<t4330>—Five minutes have passed —said the captain in the same tone—.</t4330> <t4331>One more minute and we will open fire.</t4331>
<t4332>José Arcadio Segundo, sweating ice, took the child off his shoulders and handed him to the woman.</t4332> <t4333>“These bastards are capable of shooting,” she murmured.</t4333> <t4334>José Arcadio Segundo didn’t have time to speak, because he instantly recognized the hoarse voice of Colonel Gavilán echoing the woman’s words with a shout.</t4334> <t4335>Intoxicated by the tension, by the marvelous depth of the silence, and also convinced that nothing would move that crowd stunned by the fascination of death, José Arcadio Segundo stood on tiptoe above the heads in front of him, and for the first time in his life raised his voice.</t4335>
<t4336>—Bastards! —he shouted—.</t4336> <t4337>We give you the remaining minute.</t4337>
<t4338>At the end of his scream, something happened that did not cause him fright, but rather a kind of hallucination.</t4338> <t4339>The captain gave the order to fire and fourteen machine gun nests responded immediately.</t4339> <t4340>But everything seemed like a farce.</t4340> <t4341>It was as if the machine guns had been loaded with pyrotechnic tricks, because their eager rattling could be heard, and their incandescent spits could be seen, but there was not the slightest reaction, not a voice, not even a sigh, among the compact crowd that seemed petrified by an instant invulnerability.</t4341> <t4342>Suddenly, to one side of the station, a death cry shattered the enchantment: "Aaaay, my mother."</t4342> <t4343>A seismic force, a volcanic breath, a roar of cataclysm, exploded in the center of the crowd with a colossal expansive power.</t4343> <t4344>José Arcadio Segundo barely had time to lift the child, while the mother with the other was absorbed by the crowd centrifuged by panic.</t4344>
<t4345>Many years later, the boy would still recount, despite the neighbors continuing to believe him a crazy old man, that José Arcadio Segundo lifted him above his head, and let himself be dragged, almost in the air, as if floating in the terror of the crowd, towards an adjacent street.</t4345> <t4346>The boy's privileged position allowed him to see that at that moment the frenzied mass began to reach the corner and the line of machine guns opened fire.</t4346> <t4347>Several voices shouted at the same time:</t4347>
<t4348>—Get down on the ground!</t4348> <t4349>Get down on the ground!</t4349>
<t4350>Those in the front lines had already done so, swept away by the bursts of shrapnel.</t4350> <t4351>The survivors, instead of throwing themselves to the ground, tried to return to the square, and then panic gave a dragon's lash, sending them in a compact wave against the other compact wave moving in the opposite direction, driven by the other dragon's lash from the opposite street, where the machine guns were also firing relentlessly.</t4351> <t4352>They were cornered, spinning in a gigantic whirlwind that gradually shrank to its epicenter because its edges were systematically trimmed in a circle, like peeling an onion, by the insatiable and methodical scissors of the shrapnel.</t4352> <t4353>The boy saw a woman kneeling, with her arms in a cross, in a clear space, mysteriously forbidden to the stampede.</t4353> <t4354>There José Arcadio Segundo placed him, at the moment of collapsing with his face bathed in blood, before the colossal throng swept away the empty space, the kneeling woman, the light of the high drought sky, and the damn world where Úrsula Iguarán had sold so many little caramel animals.</t4354>
<t4355>When José Arcadio Segundo woke up, he was lying face up in the darkness.</t4355> <t4356>He realized he was on an endless and silent train, and that his hair was matted with dried blood and all his bones ached.</t4356> <t4357>He felt an unbearable sleepiness.</t4357> <t4358>Determined to sleep for many hours, safe from terror and horror, he settled on the side that hurt the least, and only then did he discover that he was lying on the dead.</t4358> <t4359>There was no free space in the wagon, except for the central aisle.</t4359> <t4360>Several hours must have passed since the massacre, because the corpses had the same temperature as plaster in autumn, and the same consistency of petrified foam, and those who had placed them in the wagon had had time to stack them in the order and direction in which banana bunches were transported.</t4360> <t4361>Trying to escape the nightmare, José Arcadio Segundo crawled from one wagon to another, in the direction the train was moving, and in the flashes that burst through the wooden slats as they passed through the sleeping towns, he saw the dead men, the dead women, the dead children, who were going to be thrown into the sea like rejected bananas.</t4361> <t4362>He only recognized one woman who sold refreshments in the square and Colonel Gavilán, who still had the belt with the Morelian silver buckle with which he had tried to make his way through the panic rolled up in his hand.</t4362> <t4363>When he reached the first wagon, he jumped into the darkness and lay in the ditch until the train finished passing.</t4363> <t4364>It was the longest he had ever seen, with almost two hundred freight wagons, and a locomotive at each end and a third in the center.</t4364> <t4365>It carried no lights, not even the red and green position lamps, and it slid along at a nocturnal and stealthy speed.</t4365> <t4366>On top of the wagons, the dark shapes of soldiers with mounted machine guns could be seen.</t4366>
<t4367>After midnight, a torrential downpour broke out.</t4367> <t4368>José Arcadio Segundo did not know where he had jumped off, but he knew that by walking in the opposite direction of the train, he would reach Macondo.</t4368> <t4369>After more than three hours of walking, soaked to the bone, with a terrible headache, he saw the first houses in the light of dawn.</t4369> <t4370>Attracted by the smell of coffee, he entered a kitchen where a woman with a child in her arms was leaning over the stove.</t4370>
<t4371>—Good —he said exhaustedly—.</t4371> <t4372>I am José Arcadio Segundo Buendía.</t4372>
<t4373>He pronounced the full name, letter by letter, to convince himself that he was alive.</t4373> <t4374>He did well, because the woman had thought it was an apparition upon seeing the gaunt, somber figure at the door, with his head and clothes dirty with blood, touched by the solemnity of death.</t4374> <t4375>She knew him.</t4375> <t4376>She brought a blanket for him to wrap himself in while his clothes dried by the stove, heated water for him to wash his wound, which was only a skin tear, and gave him a clean cloth to bandage his head.</t4376> <t4377>Then she served him a cup of coffee, without sugar, as she had been told the Buendías drank it, and opened his clothes near the fire.</t4377>
<t4378>José Arcadio Segundo did not speak until he finished drinking his coffee.</t4378>
<t4379>—There must have been around three thousand —he murmured.</t4379>
<t4380>—What?</t4380>
<t4381>—The dead —he clarified—.</t4381> <t4382>They must have been all the ones who were at the station.</t4382>
<t4383>The woman measured him with a look of pity.</t4383> <t4384>“There have been no deaths here,” she said.</t4384> <t4385>“Since the times of your uncle, the colonel, nothing has happened in Macondo.”</t4385> <t4386>In three kitchens where José Arcadio Segundo stopped before reaching the house, they told him the same thing: “There were no deaths.”</t4386> <t4387>He passed by the station square and saw the tables of fried foods piled on top of each other, and there too he found no trace of the massacre.</t4387> <t4388>The streets were deserted under the relentless rain and the houses were closed, with no signs of life inside.</t4388> <t4389>The only human news was the first bell for mass.</t4389> <t4390>He knocked on the door of Colonel Gavilán's house.</t4390> <t4391>A pregnant woman, whom he had seen many times, shut the door in his face.</t4391> <t4392>“He’s gone,” she said, frightened.</t4392> <t4393>“He went back to his homeland.”</t4393> <t4394>The main entrance of the fenced chicken coop was guarded, as always, by two local policemen who seemed like stone under the rain, with raincoats and rubber helmets.</t4394> <t4395>In their marginal street, the Antillean blacks sang the Saturday psalms in chorus.</t4395> <t4396>José Arcadio Segundo jumped over the backyard fence and entered the house through the kitchen.</t4396> <t4397>Santa Sofía de la Piedad barely raised her voice.</t4397> <t4398>“Don’t let Fernanda see you,” she said.</t4398> <t4399>“She was getting up a while ago.”</t4399> <t4400>As if fulfilling an implicit pact, she took the son to the chamber pot room, fixed Melquíades' rickety cot for him, and at two in the afternoon, while Fernanda was taking a nap, she passed him a plate of food through the window.</t4400>
<t4401>Aureliano Segundo had slept at home because the rain caught him there, and at three in the afternoon he was still waiting for it to stop.</t4401> <t4402>Informed secretly by Santa Sofía de la Piedad, at that hour he visited his brother in Melquíades' room.</t4402> <t4403>He also did not believe the version of the massacre nor the nightmare of the train loaded with the dead traveling towards the sea.</t4403> <t4404>The previous night they had read an extraordinary national decree, informing that the workers had obeyed the order to evacuate the station and were heading to their homes in peaceful caravans.</t4404> <t4405>The decree also informed that the union leaders, with a high patriotic spirit, had reduced their demands to two points: reform of medical services and construction of latrines in the homes.</t4405> <t4406>It was later reported that when the military authorities obtained the workers' agreement, they hurried to communicate it to Mr. Brown, and that he not only accepted the new conditions but also offered to pay for three days of public festivities to celebrate the end of the conflict.</t4406> <t4407>Only when the military asked him for a date to announce the signing of the agreement, he looked through the window at the sky streaked with lightning and made a deep gesture of uncertainty.</t4407>
<t4408>—It will be when it stops raining —he said—.</t4408> <t4409>While the rain lasts, we suspend all kinds of activities.</t4409>
<t4410>It hadn't rained for three months and it was a time of drought.</t4410> <t4411>But when Mr. Brown announced his decision, a torrential downpour fell across the entire banana zone, surprising José Arcadio Segundo on the road to Macondo.</t4411> <t4412>A week later, it was still raining.</t4412> <t4413>The official version, repeated and hammered a thousand times throughout the country by every means of dissemination the government could find, eventually prevailed: there were no deaths, the satisfied workers had returned to their families, and the banana company was suspending activities until the rain passed.</t4413> <t4414>Martial law continued, in anticipation that emergency measures might be necessary for the public calamity of the endless downpour, but the troops were quartered.</t4414> <t4415>During the day, the soldiers walked through the torrents in the streets, with their pants rolled up to mid-calf, playing shipwrecks with the children.</t4415> <t4416>At night, after the curfew, they would break down doors with rifle butts, drag suspects out of their beds, and take them on a one-way trip.</t4416> <t4417>It was still the search and extermination of the criminals, murderers, arsonists, and rebels of Decree Number Four, but the soldiers denied it even to the relatives of their victims, who crowded the commanders' office in search of news.</t4417> <t4418>“It must have been a dream,” the officers insisted.</t4418> <t4419>“Nothing has happened in Macondo, nothing is happening, and nothing will ever happen.</t4419> <t4420>This is a happy town.”</t4420> <t4421>Thus they consummated the extermination of the union leaders.</t4421>
<t4422>The only survivor was José Arcadio Segundo.</t4422> <t4423>One February night, the unmistakable knocks of rifle butts were heard at the door.</t4423> <t4424>Aureliano Segundo, who was still waiting for the rain to stop so he could go out, opened the door to six soldiers led by an officer.</t4424> <t4425>Soaked from the rain, without saying a word, they searched the house room by room, wardrobe by wardrobe, from the living rooms to the barn.</t4425> <t4426>Úrsula woke up when they turned on the light in the bedroom, and she did not let out a sigh during the search, but she kept her fingers crossed, moving them towards where the soldiers moved.</t4426> <t4427>Santa Sofía de la Piedad managed to warn José Arcadio Segundo, who was sleeping in Melquíades' room, but he understood that it was too late to attempt an escape.</t4427> <t4428>So Santa Sofía de la Piedad closed the door again, and he put on his shirt and shoes, and sat on the cot to wait for them to arrive.</t4428> <t4429>At that moment, they were searching the goldsmith's workshop.</t4429> <t4430>The officer had the padlock opened, and with a quick sweep of the flashlight, he saw the workbench and the glass cabinet with the acid bottles and instruments that were still in the same place where their owner had left them, and he seemed to understand that no one lived in that room.</t4430> <t4431>However, he cunningly asked Aureliano Segundo if he was a silversmith, and he explained that it had been Colonel Aureliano Buendía's workshop.</t4431> <t4432>"Aha," said the officer, and he turned on the light and ordered such a thorough search that they did not miss the eighteen little gold fishes that had not been melted down and were hidden behind the bottles in the tin can.</t4432> <t4433>The officer examined them one by one on the workbench and then became completely humanized.</t4433> <t4434>"I would like to take one, if you permit me," he said.</t4434> <t4435>"At one time they were a symbol of subversion, but now they are a relic."</t4435> <t4436>He was young, almost an adolescent, without any sign of shyness, and with a natural charm that had not been noticed until then.</t4436> <t4437>Aureliano Segundo gave him the little fish.</t4437> <t4438>The officer put it in his shirt pocket, with a childlike gleam in his eyes, and threw the others back in the can to put them where they were.</t4438>
<t4439>—It is an invaluable memory —he said—.</t4439> <t4440>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was one of our greatest men.</t4440>
<t4441>However, the blow of humanization did not modify his professional conduct.</t4441> <t4442>In front of Melquíades' room, which was once again locked, Santa Sofía de la Piedad turned to a last hope.</t4442> <t4443>“It’s been like a century since anyone lived in that room,” she said.</t4443> <t4444>The officer had it opened, scanned it with the beam of his flashlight, and Aureliano Segundo and Santa Sofía de la Piedad saw the Arab eyes of José Arcadio Segundo at the moment when the beam of light passed over his face, and they understood that this was the end of one anxiety and the beginning of another that would only find relief in resignation.</t4444> <t4445>But the officer continued examining the room with the flashlight and showed no sign of interest until he discovered the seventy-two chamber pots piled up in the closets.</t4445> <t4446>Then he turned on the light.</t4446> <t4447>José Arcadio Segundo was sitting on the edge of the cot, ready to leave, more solemn and thoughtful than ever.</t4447> <t4448>At the back were the shelves with the unstitched books, the rolls of parchment, and the clean and orderly work table, with the ink in the inkwells still fresh.</t4448> <t4449>There was the same purity in the air, the same clarity, the same privilege against dust and destruction that Aureliano Segundo had known in his childhood, and that only Colonel Aureliano Buendía could not perceive.</t4449> <t4450>But the officer was only interested in the chamber pots.</t4450>
<t4451>—How many people live in this house? —he asked.</t4451>
<t4452>—Five.</t4452>
<t4453>The officer, evidently, did not understand.</t4453> <t4454>He fixed his gaze on the space where Aureliano Segundo and Santa Sofía de la Piedad were still watching José Arcadio Segundo, and he too realized that the soldier was looking at him without seeing him.</t4454> <t4455>Then he turned off the light and adjusted the door.</t4455> <t4456>When he spoke to the soldiers, Aureliano Segundo understood that the young officer had seen the room with the same eyes with which Colonel Aureliano Buendía had seen it.</t4456>
<t4457>—It is true that no one has been in that room for at least a century —said the officer to the soldiers—.</t4457> <t4458>There must even be snakes in there.</t4458>
<t4459>When the door closed, José Arcadio Segundo was certain that his war had ended.</t4459> <t4460>Years before, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had spoken to him about the fascination of war and had tried to demonstrate it with countless examples drawn from his own experience.</t4460> <t4461>He had believed him.</t4461> <t4462>But the night the soldiers looked at him without seeing him, while he thought about the tension of the past few months, the misery of the prison, the panic at the station, and the train loaded with dead bodies, José Arcadio Segundo concluded that Colonel Aureliano Buendía was nothing more than a fraud or an idiot.</t4462> <t4463>He couldn't understand why so many words were needed to explain what one felt in war, when a single word sufficed: fear.</t4463> <t4464>In Melquíades' room, however, protected by the supernatural light, the sound of the rain, and the sensation of being invisible, he found the rest he had never had for a single moment in his previous life, and the only fear that persisted was that of being buried alive.</t4464> <t4465>He told this to Santa Sofía de la Piedad, who brought him his daily meals, and she promised to fight to stay alive beyond her strength to ensure that he would be buried dead.</t4465> <t4466>Free from all fear, José Arcadio Segundo then dedicated himself to reviewing Melquíades' parchments many times, and all the more content the less he understood them.</t4466> <t4467>Accustomed to the sound of the rain, which after two months became a new form of silence, the only thing that disturbed his solitude were the comings and goings of Santa Sofía de la Piedad.</t4467> <t4468>That is why he begged her to leave the food on the windowsill and lock the door.</t4468> <t4469>The rest of the family forgot about him, including Fernanda, who had no problem leaving him there when she learned that the soldiers had seen him without recognizing him.</t4469> <t4470>After six months of confinement, seeing that the soldiers had left Macondo, Aureliano Segundo removed the lock looking for someone to talk to while the rain passed.</t4470> <t4471>From the moment he opened the door, he felt assaulted by the stench of the chamber pots that were placed on the floor, all of them often used.</t4471> <t4472>José Arcadio Segundo, devoured by hair, indifferent to the air thickened by nauseating vapors, continued reading and rereading the unintelligible parchments.</t4472> <t4473>He was illuminated by a seraphic glow.</t4473> <t4474>He barely looked up when he felt the door open, but his brother needed only that glance to see the irreparable destiny of their great-grandfather repeated in it.</t4474>
<t4475>—There were more than three thousand —was all that José Arcadio Segundo said—.</t4475> <t4476>Now I am sure that they were all the ones who were at the station.</t4476>
<t4477>It rained for four years, eleven months, and two days.</t4477> <t4478>There were periods of drizzle when everyone put on their Sunday best and wore a convalescent face to celebrate the clearing, but soon they got used to interpreting the pauses as announcements of a resurgence.</t4478> <t4479>The sky was unpaved in storms of destruction, and the north sent hurricanes that chipped roofs and knocked down walls, and uprooted the last vines of the plantations.</t4479> <t4480>As happened during the insomnia plague, which Úrsula remembered during those days, the calamity itself inspired defenses against boredom.</t4480> <t4481>Aureliano Segundo was one of those who did the most to not succumb to idleness.</t4481> <t4482>He had gone to the house for some casual matter the night Mr. Brown summoned the storm, and Fernanda tried to help him with a half-broken umbrella she found in a closet.</t4482> <t4483>“No need,” he said.</t4483> <t4484>“I’ll stay here until it clears up.”</t4484> <t4485>It was not, of course, an unavoidable commitment, but he almost fulfilled it to the letter.</t4485> <t4486>Since his clothes were at Petra Cotes's house, he took off the ones he was wearing every three days and waited in his underwear while they were washed.</t4486> <t4487>To avoid getting bored, he devoted himself to fixing the numerous defects in the house.</t4487> <t4488>He adjusted hinges, oiled locks, screwed bolts, and leveled latches.</t4488> <t4489>For several months he was seen wandering with a toolbox that the gypsies must have forgotten in the times of José Arcadio Buendía, and no one knew if it was due to the involuntary exercise, the winter boredom, or the enforced abstinence, but his belly gradually deflated like a wineskin, and his beatific turtle face became less sanguine and his double chin less protuberant, until he ended up being less pachydermic and could tie his shoelaces again.</t4489> <t4490>Seeing him install doorknobs and disconnect clocks, Fernanda wondered if he was also falling into the vice of doing to undo, like Colonel Aureliano Buendía with his goldfish, Amaranta with the buttons and the shroud, José Arcadio Segundo with the parchments, and Úrsula with her memories.</t4490> <t4491>But it wasn’t true.</t4491> <t4492>The problem was that the rain disrupted everything, and the driest machines sprouted <t4498>He would have had no problem returning to the insipid love of Fernanda, whose beauty had settled with maturity, but the rain had saved him from any passionate emergency, and had infused him <t4500>He had been one of the first to bring zinc sheets to Macondo, long before the banana company made them fashionable, just to roof Petra Cotes's bedroom with them and enjoy the deep intimacy that <t4529>No cortege more desolate could have been conceived.</t4529> <t4530>They had placed the coffin on an ox cart upon which they built a shelter of banana leaves, but the pressure of the rain was so intense and the streets were so muddy that at every step the wheels got stuck and the shelter was about to collapse.</t4530> <t4531>The sad streams of water falling on the coffin were soaking the flag they had placed on top of it, which was actually the flag stained with blood and gunpowder, repudiated by the most dignified veterans.</t4531> <t4532>On the coffin, they had also placed the saber with copper and silk tassels, the same one that Colonel Gerineldo Márquez used to hang on the coat rack in the living room to enter unarmed into Amaranta's sewing room.</t4532> <t4533>Behind the cart, some barefoot and all with their pants rolled up to mid-calf, the last survivors of the capitulation of Neerlandia splashed through the mud, carrying in one hand a walking stick and in the other a wreath of paper flowers faded by the rain.</t4533> <t4534>They appeared like an unreal vision on the street that still bore the name of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, and everyone looked at the house as they passed, and turned at the corner of the square, where they had to ask for help to get the cart unstuck.</t4534> <t4535>Úrsula had been brought to the door by Santa Sofía de la Piedad.</t4535> <t4536>She followed the burial's vicissitudes with such attention that no one doubted she was seeing it, especially because her raised hand, like that of an announcing archangel, moved with the jolts of the cart.</t4536>

<t4565>Aureliano Segundo returned to the house with his trunks, convinced that not only Úrsula, but all the inhabitants of Macondo, were waiting for the rain to stop so they could die.</t4565> <t4566>He had seen them as he passed by, sitting in the living rooms with their gaze absorbed and their arms crossed, feeling an entire time pass, an untamed time, because it was useless to divide it into months and years, and the days into hours, when nothing could be done but watch the rain.</t4566> <t4567>The children received Aureliano Segundo with joy, who once again played the asthmatic accordion for them.</t4567> <t4568>But the concert did not capture their attention as much as the encyclopedic sessions, so once again they gathered in Meme's bedroom, where Aureliano Segundo's imagination turned the airship into a flying elephant looking for a place to sleep among the clouds.</t4568> <t4569>On one occasion, he found a man on horseback who, despite his exotic attire, retained a familiar air, and after much examination, he concluded that it was a portrait of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t4569> <t4570>He showed it to Fernanda, and she also admitted the resemblance of the rider not only to the colonel but to all the members of the family, although in truth he was a Tartar warrior.</t4570> <t4571>Thus, time passed for him, between the Colossus of Rhodes and the snake charmers, until his wife announced that there were no more than six kilos of salted meat and a sack of rice left in the granary.</t4571>

<t4616>Convinced that Úrsula would take the secret to her grave, Aureliano Segundo hired a team of diggers under the pretext of building drainage channels in the yard and backyard, and he himself
